2001 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Goldberg v. Goldberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Central Trial Term, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 224; 291 N.Y.S.2d 482; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1359



June 26, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner's motion having been denied, the Clerk of Central Trial Term is directed to place the matter on the calendar on a mutually satisfactory date for all parties.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former wife sought an upward modification of a child support award in a divorce decree entered in Mexico based upon changed circumstances. Respondent former husband sought a downward modification of the child support award in the divorce decree. The wife filed a motion to dismiss the husband's cross-petition.


OVERVIEW: The wife argued that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 466(c) did not allow for a downward modification of an award of child support in a divorce decree. The wife alleged further that the court was bound by the principles of comity not to lower the child support award. Upon review, the court concluded that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 466(c) allowed for either upward or downward modification of the child support award. The court determined that, under principles of comity, the court had the power to deny even prima facie validity to the judgments of foreign countries for policy reasons. The court held that to permit a former wife to seek upward modification of alimony provisions contained in a foreign divorce decree on the ground of change of circumstances, yet deny the same right to a divorced husband to seek downward modification under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 466(c), would have been violative of the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. 14, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court denied the wife's motion to dismiss the husband's cross-petition.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- foreign divorce -- even if wife seeks only enforcement, not modification, of support provisions of separation agreement incorporated in Mexican divorce decree, husband may cross-move for downward modification.

Support provisions for wife and children, in a separation agreement, were incorporated in a Mexican divorce decree; and the wife subsequently applied to the New York Family Court for enforcement -- not modification -- of those provisions. Thereupon the husband properly cross-moved for a downward modification of the support provisions, on account of change of circumstances. Even if the Mexican court has no power to modify its own divorce decrees, our Family Court has been given the power of enforcing or modifying the alimony provisions; and of course, the children were not parties to the agreement or the divorce and they are not bound by those support provisions. (Family Ct. Act § 466, subd. [c], pars. [i], [ii]; cf. § 461); Matter of Seitz v. Drogheo, 21 N Y [***2] 2d 181, followed.)

 

2002 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Rosetti


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 62; 291 N.Y.S.2d 738; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1362



June 25, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff father brought an action against defendant property clerk for improperly invoking forfeiture of an automobile after his son, who sold him the car, was sentenced for killing two people.


OVERVIEW: The son owned the vehicle when he took a gun from the car and used it in the killings. The police took possession of the parked vehicle. The son was ultimately sentenced to prison. The father objected to the forfeiture of the car. The property clerk claimed that the sale to the father was invalid by virtue of the son's murder sentence. The court found that there was no lawful basis for the forfeiture because the vehicle was not an instrument of the crimes. Under New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § 435, the assignment of the vehicle to the father, prior to sentencing, was valid, and forfeiture was inappropriate. When the assignment was made, the son possessed all his civil rights; no encumbrance to or suspension of his civil rights existed. He was free to fully exercise such civil rights to sell the car. Moreover, the car was used by the son for the purpose of transporting himself to a restaurant. He did not operate the motor vehicle with a view to committing the murders in which he was subsequently involved.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the father was entitled to retain the car and that it was not subject to forfeiture by the property clerk.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Replevin -- property seized by police -- man accidentally met two people and quarreled with them and took gun out of his car and killed them; police arrested him and seized gun and car; before his sentencing, he rightfully sold his car (cf. Civil Rights Law, § 79); car had not been used as part of crime, and therefore its purchaser is granted judgment of replevin against Police Department Property Clerk (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 435-4.0).

A man accidentally met and quarreled with another man and woman, and then went to his own parked car nearby and took a gun out of the glove compartment, and returned to the sidewalk where the other two were standing, and there he shot and killed both of them. Several hours later the police found him with the gun at home, and arrested him and seized the gun. Subsequently they found and seized his automobile; it was still parked where he had left it, about nine blocks from his home. Two months thereafter he validly sold the automobile to his father; and, seven months after the homicides, he was sentenced to 10 to 25 years in State prison, and there he is now confined. The conviction did not forfeit his property or deprive [***2] him of the right to transfer his property; in any event, at the time of the sale he had not yet been sentenced (Penal Law, § 510; now in Civil Rights Law, § 79). His father is entitled to judgment against the Police Department Property Clerk for the replevin of the automobile. The Administrative Code of the City of New York (§ 435-4.0, subd. f) provides that a purchaser of property seized by the police must prove "that such property or money was held and used in a lawful manner"; but the circumstances of these homicides show that the automobile was only incidentally involved and that the criminal did not use the automobile to further those crimes.

 

2003 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. "Y. O. 2404"


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


57 Misc. 2d 30; 291 N.Y.S.2d 510; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1365



June 24, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a youthful offender, was charged with a felony. He executed a consent to be investigated for youthful offender treatment and, under protest, signed the jury waiver as required by N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 913-h.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that he had a right to a jury trial even as a youthful offender and challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 913-h that required that he waive a jury trial before being eligible for youthful offender treatment. Upon review, the court concluded that § 913-h was not unconstitutional. The court determined that § 913-h was a means for expression of the right to waive a jury trial as provided in N.Y. Const. art. I, § 2. The court ruled that, as no appellate court had extended the jury mandate provisions of U.S. Const. amend. VI to youthful offender proceedings in New York, the court was required to uphold the constitutionality of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 913-h.


OUTCOME: The court disallowed defendant's protest and directed that defendant either waive a jury trial and stand for youthful offender treatment or proceed to trial as declining the offer.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- youthful offenders -- requirement that youth, who is accused of felony and who wishes to be tried as youthful offender and not as criminal, must sign waiver of trial by jury, is constitutional -- signature under protest not allowed.

An 18-year-old youth was charged with a felony, and he executed a consent to be investigated and tried on a charge of being a youthful offender, but he signed under protest the jury trial waiver which is required by the Youthful Offender Act (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 913-e-913-r). The requirement of such waiver is constitutional (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2, which, unlike U. S. Const., 6th Amdt., provides for waiver). The court disallows such signing under protest, and directs that the defendant shall either waive a trial by jury of the charge of being a youthful offender or shall proceed to a trial of the felony charge.

 

2004 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Parrow


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Ontario County


56 Misc. 2d 863; 291 N.Y.S.2d 175; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1384



June 17, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Both motions are denied, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Third-party defendant supplier filed a motion seeking a dismissal for general delay pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216(b) of actions brought by plaintiff buyer and defendant/third-party plaintiff seller regarding the sale of a defective truck part. The seller filed a similar motion seeking to dismiss the buyer's action.


OVERVIEW: The supplier provided a truck part to the seller, who then sold the part to the buyer. The buyer installed the part when repairing his truck, and suffered damages when the part proved to be defective. The buyer filed an action to recover damages from the seller. The seller filed a third-party action against the supplier. All of the parties were served with process and answers were timely filed. The supplier demanded a bill of particulars from the seller, and the seller demanded the same from the buyer. The seller and buyer complied with the requests. None of the parties made demands to resume prosecution pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216(b) and no steps were taken to file a note of issue. The supplier and the seller filed motions to dismiss for general delay. The court found that neither the supplier nor the seller complied with N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216(b) by demanding prosecution be resumed and that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216(b) was not an unconstitutional limit on a court's discretion to dismiss an action.


OUTCOME: The court denied the supplier's motion to dismiss and the seller's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Dismissal and nonsuit -- dismissal for delay -- CPLR 3216, which prevents dismissal for want of prosecution unless issue joined, one year has elapsed since joinder and 45-day notice served on dilatory party to resume prosecution, is constitutional.

Section 30 of article VI of the Constitution, which states that "The legislature shall have the same power to alter and regulate the jurisdiction and proceedings in law and in equity that it has heretofore exercised" authorizes the Legislature to enact laws such as CPLR 3216 providing that, before an action may be dismissed for want of prosecution, issue must have been joined and one year elapsed since the joinder and in addition a notice must be served giving the dilatory party 45 days in which to resume prosecution. CPLR 3216 is constitutional and motions by defendant and third-party defendant to dismiss for general delay without service of the 45-day notice are denied. ( Cohn v. Borchard Affiliations, 30 A D 2d 74, disapproved.)

 

2005 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Solomonow


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


56 Misc. 2d 1050; 291 N.Y.S.2d 145; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1385



June 17, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with disorderly conduct, and one defendant was also charged with resisting arrest.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested when they sought to protest the arrest and trial of four writers in the Soviet Union. The police restrained the protesters in a particular area, but the restraint was not observed. The police justified their order denying picketing before an embassy on the grounds that there was a security problem in protecting the embassy. The court concluded that defendants were not guilty of disorderly conduct for crossing into a closed area. The prohibition on the location of the picketing was not based on any policy established by the police department, and it was not contained in any ordinance or statute. Therefore, the court concluded that the police lacked the authority to close the public street in front of the embassy. As a result, the charges of disorderly conduct were invalid. Defendants had a right to exercise their First Amendment rights. For the same reason, the charge of resisting arrest with respect to one defendant was also invalid.


OUTCOME: The court found that defendants were not guilty of the charge of disorderly conduct and that one defendant was not guilty of the charge of resisting arrest.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- resisting arrest -- free speech by means of peaceful picketing on public street in front of foreign embassy is not disorderly conduct and cannot be prohibited by police, in absence of statute or ordinance -- picket who sat on sidewalk but then peacefully accompanied arresting officer to police station did not resist arrest.

Without authority of statute or ordinance, the police cannot, on the grounds of security, prohibit free speech in the form of peaceful picketing on the public street in front of the U. N. embassy of the Soviet Union or of any other nation. (U. S. Const., 1st Amdt.) All of the five arrested pickets are found not guilty of disorderly conduct; and additionally, the first named defendant, who sat down on the street and was arrested and was assisted to his feet by a policeman and peacefully accompanied the policeman to the nearby police station, is found not guilty of resisting arrest.

 

2006 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Storm v. None


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 342; 291 N.Y.S.2d 515; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1383



June 17, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother and respondent father submitted a revised proposed child support agreement for the court's consideration after the court had granted adjournment to the parties to consider how United States Supreme Court decisions, decided after the proposed agreement was submitted, impacted the agreement.


OVERVIEW: The mother and father of a child that was born out of wedlock submitted a proposed child support agreement for the court's approval. The court granted adjournment to both parties to consider how United States Supreme Court decisions had been recently decided impacted the agreement. The parties then submitted a revised proposed child support agreement of the court's approval. The court approved the proposed settlement agreement. The court held that pursuant to decisions that had been decided by the United States Supreme Court, statutes which discriminated against children born out of wed lock violated the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution. The statute for support of a child who was born out of wedlock provided that each parent was liable for the necessary support of the child, while the support provision for children born in wedlock provided that the father was required to pay support according to his means. The parties had revised the agreement, which had been based on the out of wedlock statute, to comply with the in wedlock statute, and thus was approved by the court because it avoided discrimination based on birth status.


OUTCOME: The court approved the revised proposed child support agreement submitted by the parents of a child born out of wedlock.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children born out of wedlock -- support -- State statutes which do not give child born out of wedlock same right to support by father in accordance with means given to child born in wedlock are unconstitutional -- agreement between parents providing for weekly support payment for child born out of wedlock cannot be approved in absence of evidence of father's income and grant to court of power to modify payments upward if father's income increases -- upon amendment of agreement so as to state father's income and giving court said jurisdiction, agreement is approved and may be incorporated in order.

1. State statutes which discriminate against a child born out of wedlock by not giving him the right to support commensurate with the father's income which a child born in wedlock receives violate the Equal Protection Clause of the Constitution.

2. An agreement between the parents of a child born out of wedlock providing for payment to the mother of her expenses in connection with pregnancy, confinement and recovery, for a weekly support payment for the child, for payments for a college education and for revision of the payments for support if the father's income is reduced, cannot [***2] be approved without evidence of the father's current means. The court must be given the power to modify not only if the father's income is decreased but if the father's income is increased.

3. Upon modification of the agreement by the parents so as to state the father's yearly earnings and giving the court continuing jurisdiction to review the amount of support if the father's income changed, the agreement is approved and may be incorporated into an order.

 

2007 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Accavallo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Trial Term, Nassau County


57 Misc. 2d 264; 291 N.Y.S.2d 972; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1398



June 11, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Upon the foregoing, it is ordered that the oral and written statements made by defendant are admissible on the trial and that defendant's motion to suppress the evidence is denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating New York's Labor Law in that he wrongfully received unemployment benefits while he was employed. He made a motion to suppress oral and written statements he had made.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was asked by the New York State Department of Labor to come to its office for an interview. There, defendant met with two Department agents and verified that signatures were his. He was then told that he was in trouble because the agents had obtained a statement from his employer that he was employed while he was collecting unemployment benefits. Defendant made three written statements. He was not given Miranda warnings. The court first stated that the agents were similar to employees of the Internal Revenue Service, to whom the United States Supreme Court had held the Miranda rule applied. The court held, however, that the Miranda warnings were not required because defendant was not in custodial interrogation. Defendant had frequently been in the Department's office, though not in the manager's office where the interview took place. The agents were not armed, and the office was drab but not prisonlike in appearance. Any fear defendant felt arose from his fear of unpleasant consequences rather than from external physical circumstances or from any intimidation. Moreover, the investigation had not reached such a critical stage as to mandate the presence of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to suppress.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- confessions -- Miranda warnings must be given by agents of State even if not peace officers -- defendant, charged with wrongfully receiving unemployment insurance benefits, was not in custody when interviewed and Miranda warnings were not required -- defendant's statements were voluntary, are admissible and are not suppressed.

1. The warnings required to be given under Miranda (384 U.S. 436) must be given by agents of the State, regardless of the fact that they are not peace officers, so long as their function is to obtain information from witnesses or suspects which may be used in a prosecution, here, of a person who has wrongfully received unemployment benefits at a time when he was employed.

2. The interview of defendant in the manager's office which was not prison-like in appearance, was not custodial in nature and therefore Miranda does not apply.

3. Defendant's statements were voluntary. The investigation had not reached a point to mandate the presence [***2] of counsel. The motion to suppress the statements is denied and they are admissible on the trial.

 

2008 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re City of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 156; 291 N.Y.S.2d 656; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1420



June 4, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The award, therefore, is modified as follows: Land $ 270,500, building $ 167,500, total $ 438,000.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a condemnation proceeding, the claimants sought an interest rate of six percent on the award, while the statutory rate under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 3-a was only four percent.


OVERVIEW: In an eminent domain proceeding, the law required the city to pay the interest rate of four percent under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 3-a, and the claimants sought six percent. The court awarded the claimants an interest rate of six percent because four percent was so unreasonably low as to deprive the claimants just compensation and "substantial justice" would not have been fulfilled by the unrealistic and outmoded rate. The court also held that amending N.Y. State Fin. Law § 16 to provide for six percent interest on condemnation awards while leaving the municipal rate at four percent as the legislature had done represented an unreasonable and palpably improper classification and constituted a denial to the claimants of the constitutional right to equal treatment of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court granted the claimants an interest rate of six percent on their condemnation awards.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- just compensation -- interest -- in condemnation, interest is part of "just compensation" to be paid by condemnor and is subject to judicial determination -- interest at 4% which municipal corporations must pay is unreasonably low; increase in State interest rate in condemnations effective August 1, 1966 without similar increase in rate for municipal corporations is unconstitutional denial to claimants against city of equal treatment of laws -- just compensation includes interest at 6% from August 1, 1966 to date of payment -- tentative awards in several cases are modified.

1. In a condemnation proceeding, interest is an integral part of the constitutional "just compensation" which the condemnor must pay and a matter for judicial determination despite section 3-a of the General Municipal Law which specifies a rate of 4% to be paid by municipal corporations on awards in condemnation proceedings.

2. Interest at the rate of 4% is unreasonably low and deprives claimants of "just compensation" in view of the high interest rates now prevailing. Moreover, the [***2] city earns 5.45% on its investments and it is to its profits to pay 4% interest on condemnation awards. The increase in the interest rate in State condemnation proceedings effective August 1, 1966 (State Finance Law, § 16) without increasing the interest rate in condemnations by municipalities constitutes a denial to claimants of the constitutional right to equal treatment of the laws.

3. Just compensation to claimants must include interest at 4% from the vesting dates to August 1, 1966 but at 6% from August 1, 1966 to date of payment.

4. Tentative awards with respect to several damage parcels are, on review of the record, modified.

 

2009 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Union Free School Dist. v. Nowicki


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


57 Misc. 2d 66; 291 N.Y.S.2d 679; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1425



June 3, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the defendant to dismiss the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff old school board in one of two districts that were merged to form a new school district asked for a declaratory judgment against defendants, the new school board and its members. The old board asked that the new board be declared without power to act officially until at least the next school year, alleging that members of the new board were acting beyond their authority by acting as a board. Defendants filed a motion for dismissal.


OVERVIEW: The only interference the old board alleged resulted from the new board's activities preparing for the school year and from the publicity given these activities. The court noted that the old school district was about to go out of existence, and the new board had to assume additional burdens placed on it by law within a short time, providing for all the students in the area. The court explained that district was organized by an election, and the board members were elected at a school meeting. The court concluded that under N.Y. Educ. Law art. 37, the new school district came into existence the instant the New York Commissioner of Education issued an order creating it, and the district was organized when the voters approved it. The court noted other litigation between the same parties. The court concurred in the ruling of the Commissioner that any attempt by the old board to provide for the schools after the end of the current school year was ultra vires and void. The court ruled that the new board acted within its statutory powers by making plans for the new school year, negotiating wage contracts, preparing to and raising funds by a bond referendum, and incurring obligations.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- central school districts -- action by merged union free school district for declaratory judgment against new central school district board's activities in negotiating wage contracts for forthcoming school year and conducting bond referendum, dismissed.

The Commissioner of Education merged two union free school districts into a new central school district in July, 1967; and the voters of both districts approved this in August, 1967, and in September, 1967 they elected a central school board, which will be in sole charge when the new school year begins on July 1, 1968 (Education Law, art. 37). Meantime, the old union free school district boards have the duty of administering their schools and then winding up their accounts and transferring their funds and their bills to the new central school board by the end of July, 1968; and, meantime, the new central school board has the duty of preparing for July 1, 1968, and the power of negotiating wage contracts for the new year with the employees of the existing union free school districts, levying taxes in the present year for the coming year and for paying [***2] the remaining obligations of the union free school districts, issuing tax anticipation notes, conducting a bond referendum, etc. (Education Law, § 1804, subds, 5, 6; § 1805; Local Finance Law, § 24.00, subd. d, par. 1.) No constitutional issues are involved in this, and if there are any such issues, they can be asserted by the voters and bondholders and others but not by the outgoing boards. Accordingly, this complaint by one of the outgoing union free school district boards, seeking a declaratory judgment against the activities of the central school district board, is dismissed. Moreover, these issues have already been determined against this board in an article 78 proceeding and in its suit to invalidate the election of the central school board; and it should pursue any appeal therefrom rather than institute this new suit; or, it should submit its disputes to the administrative procedures and remedies within the competence of the State Department of Education.

 

2010 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


National Asso. of Harness Drivers v. New York State Racing Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 135; 291 N.Y.S.2d 475; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1439



May 28, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction is denied and the cross motions of the defendants to dismiss the complaint are granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a national association of harness drivers, sought an order enjoining and restraining defendant, the New York State Racing Commission, during the pendency of the action from paying out and distributing in purses for races run at the thoroughbred race tracks an extra one half percent of the "handle." The association's complaint and motion for an injunction pendente lite challenged the constitutionality of 1968 N.Y. Laws §§ 3, 4, 8.


OVERVIEW: 1968 N.Y. Laws §§ 3, 4, 8 permitted flat tracks and harness tracks to retain an additional percent of the total revenue of the pari-mutuel betting pool and permitted one half of that sum to be used for general corporate purposes. It required harness tracks to pay the other one half percent to the State as a tax. Flat tracks were permitted to retain the one half percent if the moneys were used for purse enrichment. The association contended that the statute was unconstitutional because no similar provision was made for harness tracks and because there was no rational basis for the distinction in treatment between the flat tracks and the harness tracks, or between the owners and drivers of thoroughbred horses and those of harness racing horses. The court dismissed the complaint. The court of appeals had previously upheld the constitutionality of a state statute permitting higher local admission taxes at harness tracks than at flat tracks, recognizing that the legislature was allowed to constitutionally distinguish between the tracks for the purpose of taxation. There was a "rational basis" for placing the two sports, for purposes of control and taxation, in different classifications.


OUTCOME: The court granted the Commission's motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Horse racing -- taxation -- "flat tracks", as distinguished from "harness tracks", may use 1/2% of "handle" to increase their purses; statute (L. 1968, ch. 91) is constitutional.

There is a rational basis for legislation differentiating between horse racing at so-called "flat tracks" and horse racing at so-called "harness tracks", as to controls and taxation. Thus, recent legislation (L. 1968, ch. 91) validly and constitutionally permits "flat tracks", as distinguished from "harness tracks", to use 1/2% of the so-called "handle" for the purpose of increasing purses. Moreover, the plaintiffs, representing harness horse racing drivers, have failed to demonstrate that they are personally aggrieved by this statute. Their action for a declaration of unconstitutionality and for an injunction is dismissed.

 

2011 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Novak v. Poughkeepsie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Dutchess County


57 Misc. 2d 927; 293 N.Y.S.2d 780; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1465



May 21, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff plumber filed an action for a declaratory judgment declaring a portion of the Town of Poughkeepsie, N.Y. Plumbing Code to be unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Town of Poughkeepsie, N.Y., Plumbing Code §§ 14.3.1, 14.2.5, 14.2.2 provided the requirements for the licensing of plumbers by the Town. Section 14.2.2 provided that the qualifications necessary would be determined by the Board of Plumbing Examiners of the Town of Poughkeepsie. The plumber claimed that § 14.2.2 was unconstitutional because it was an invalid delegation of legislative power to the Board. The court agreed and declared the licensing provisions to be unconstitutional. The court held that although the Town could regulated the plumbing trade by requiring licensing, it could not delegate that power to the Board without any limitations or guides. The court found that § 14.2.2 was unconstitutional because it allowed the Board to act solely on their own ideas of public policy without providing standards for the Board to follow.


OUTCOME: The court granted the plumber's petition and declared certain licensing provisions of the plumbing code to be unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Administrative law -- delegation of power -- town's plumbing code providing for licensing of plumbers providing they pass examination for which they have such qualifications as deemed necessary by Board of Plumbing Examiners is unconstitutional as delegation of power without standards.

A town's plumbing code which provides for the licensing of plumbers by a Board of Plumbing Examiners upon their passing an examination to qualify for which they "shall have such qualifications as may be deemed necessary by the board" is unconstitutional. The Town Board could not validly delegate legislative power to the Board of Plumbing Examiners without prescribing any standard or rule by which action by that administrative body is to be governed. The licensing power conferred upon the board is without limitation or guide.

 

2012 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Campoli v. Grand Union Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Rockland County


58 Misc. 2d 7; 294 N.Y.S.2d 295; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1467



May 17, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment appealed from is in all respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation sought review of the judgment from the Justice of the Peace of the Town of Stony Point (New York), which returned a verdict in favor of plaintiff consumer, in her negligence action against the corporation.


OVERVIEW: The consumer filed a negligence action against the corporation, owner of a grocery store. The jury returned a verdict in favor of the consumer. On appeal, the corporation contended that N.Y. Uniform Just. Ct. Act §§10, 51, insofar as they purported to vest the justice court with jurisdiction over a foreign corporation, violated N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 18 and, accordingly, were unconstitutional. The court affirmed the verdict in favor of the consumer and held that the constitutional prohibition of former § 18 did not prevent the justice court, from acquiring jurisdiction over a foreign corporation if the foreign corporation was served with process within the territorial limits of the justice court. The corporation was served with process within the territorial limits of the justice court; therefore, service was sufficient to vest the justice court with jurisdiction over the corporation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the verdict in favor of the consumer, in her negligence action against the corporation.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service on nonresidents -- plaintiff bought jar of pickles in defendant's store, and ate part of pickle with worm and became sick; $ 450 verdict proper, even without medical testimony as to cause -- plaintiff's own testimony that she paid doctor $ 5 proper -- store and plaintiff's residence were in same town; process was served in said town on store's manager; defendant store was foreign corporation; town's Justice Court had jurisdiction (former Justice Court Act, § 10, subd. 3; § 51, subd. 3) -- judgment is affirmed by Couty Court.

1. Plaintiff became ill after eating part of a pickle which contained a worm. The pickle was in a jar of pickles which she had bought in defendant's store. A verdict of $ 450 was proper, even though she did not offer any medical testimony as to cause and effect.

2. Although plaintiff's physician was not called, plaintiff properly testified that she had paid him $ 5 for one visit and had paid for the medication he prescribed.

3. The action was tried on May 21, 1967 in the Justice Court of the town in which plaintiff resided and in which defendant had its store and in which [***2] the manager of the store was served with process. Even though the defendant was a foreign corporation, the Justice Court had jurisdiction of the subject and the defendant (former Justice Court Act, § 10, subd. 3 and last unnumbered par.; § 51, subd. 3; N. Y. Const., former art. VI, § 18).

4. Accordingly, the County Court affirms the judgment of the Justice Court.

In re Estate of Harmoza


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Nassau County

The proceedings thus far had in this estate indicate that it is "uncertain" that a nephew who is a residuary beneficiary and who resides in the U.S.S.R. would have the benefit or use or control of the money due him; and therefore the final decree which will now be entered, settling the executrix' accounts, will direct that the share of said Soviet national shall be paid into court for his benefit or for the benefit of the person or persons who may hereafter be shown to be entitled thereto (SCPA 2218). The cited statute has heretofore been held to be constitutional.

 

2014 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Dell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Monroe County


56 Misc. 2d 1017; 290 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1495



May 13, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was a motion filed by respondent juvenile to dismiss the summons, petition, and all proceedings that charged him with being a juvenile delinquent.


OVERVIEW: A clerk of the court issued the summons in a proceeding to determine if the juvenile was delinquent. The juvenile filed a motion to dismiss the summons, petition, and all proceedings therein, upon the ground that the summons and petition were not issued in compliance with the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 736. Specifically, the juvenile alleged that his due process rights under the State and Federal Constitutions were violated because a judge had not found that probable cause existed prior to the issuance of the summons. The court denied the juvenile's motion and concluded that his due process rights were not violated. The court found that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 736 did not require that a judge issue the summons. The court determined that the legislature had not intended to burden family court judges with the unnecessary duty to examine each petition in each new neglect, support, family offense, juvenile delinquent and persons in need of supervision proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court denied the juvenile's motion to dismiss and restored the matter for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Family Court -- summons in juvenile delinquency proceeding may validly be issued and signed by Clerk of court (Family Ct. Act, § 736).

The Family Court Act ( § 736) provides that, when a juvenile delinquency petition is filed, "the court" may cause a summons to be issued, and that such summons shall be signed by the court "or by the clerk" of the court. This is valid and constitutional. It is only when and if, subsequently, a warrant becomes necessary, that "the court" itself must issue the warrant (Family Ct. Act § 738).

People v. Anonymous


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


56 Misc. 2d 725; 289 N.Y.S.2d 782; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1531



May 1, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly the motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: Defendant brought a motion seeking a jury trial in a youthful offender proceeding. The court found that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 913-h was not unconstitutional in providing for a trial without a jury of the charge of being a youthful offender. The court held that under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 2 there was no right to a jury trial in a youthful offender proceeding. The motion was denied.

HEADNOTES

Infants -- youthful offenders -- no right to trial by jury of charge of being youthful offender.

There is no constitutional or statutory right to a jury trial in a youthful offender proceeding (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 913-g, 913-h; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2; Nieves v. United States, 280 F. Supp. 994, distinguished). Defendant's motion for trial by jury is denied.

 

2016 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kearse


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Syracuse, Criminal Division


56 Misc. 2d 586; 289 N.Y.S.2d 346; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1563



April 17, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motions of the defendants to dismiss herein are granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the information against them on the ground that the city ordinance under which they were arrested was unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested pursuant to a city ordinance that granted the mayor the authority to proclaim a state of emergency and invoke a curfew forbidding people to be present in public places during certain hours of the day. Defendants claimed that the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face in that (1) it was an unlawful delegation of legislative authority from the city council to the mayor, (2) the city had tried to legislate in an area which had been fully pre-empted by state law, (3) it denied defendants their constitutionally guaranteed liberties, and (4) it was overreaching and excessively broad and was susceptible to arbitrary enforcement in violation of defendants' constitutional rights. In dismissing the information, the court held that the portions of the ordinance having to do with firearms, curfew, and penalties were invalid in their entirety. However, because the ordinance contained a separability clause, the remainder of the ordinance was declared valid.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motions to dismiss of defendants and dismissed the information against them on the ground that the city ordinance under which they were arrested was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- curfew -- ordinance purporting to authorize Mayor to proclaim emergency curfews invalid -- other parts of ordinance may be separable and valid.

1. A city ordinance purporting to authorize the Mayor to proclaim Emergency curfews during which "No person shall enter or remain in any public street, park, square * * * in any such part or parts of the city during the hours of the day as may be prescribed by the Mayor", is excessively broad and hence peculiarly susceptible to arbitrary enforcement in violation of individual rights which are guaranteed by the Federal and State Constitutions. Moreover, though its subject matter is not pre-empted by the Penal Law (art. 188, "Riots and Unlawful Assemblies") and by the Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 102 et seq., "Prevention and Suppression of Riots") the ordinance contravenes the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 1604, "Local ordinances prohibited"). Furthermore, the penalties specified in the ordinance -- fines of from $ 25 to $ 500, or imprisonment of up to 180 days, or both -- exceed the limits prescribed by the Second Class Cities Law ( § 42) and [***2] by the city's charter (which is itself a local law; Local Laws, 1960, No. 13 of City of Syracuse, § 8-118), both of which statutes authorize ordinances prescribing fines not exceeding $ 150 or imprisonment not exceeding 150 days or both. This prohibitive ordinance is not authorized by the State Constitution (art. IX) or by the General City Law ( § 20, subds. 13, 22) or by the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 10). Accordingly, informations charging each of three defendants with separately entering or remaining on public streets after the emergency curfew hours which had been proclaimed by the Mayor are dismissed.

2. However, there are other parts of this ordinance which are not involved in this case and which may be separable and valid, particularly so since this ordinance contains a provision for separability in case of partial invalidity.

 

2017 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


County of Nassau v. Metropolitan Transp. Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


57 Misc. 2d 1025; 293 N.Y.S.2d 1017; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1588



April 10, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county filed an action challenging the approval by respondent New York Metropolitan Transportation Authority (authority) of its subsidiary's rate increase.


OVERVIEW: The county challenged a rate increase approved by the authority for one of its subsidiaries. The court dismissed the petition. The court held that although the county did not have standing to bring the action as an aggrieved party, under N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 109, it had the right to bring the action. The crux of the county's argument was that the authority could not act as both petitioner for the rate increase and the trier of the facts. However, N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1266(3) allowed the authority to levy such fares as it deemed necessary, convenient, or desirable. Because the hearing was quasi-legislative, disagreement with the authority's conclusion was no ground for finding it illegal.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the county' petition challenging the authority's approval of a rate increase for its subsidiary.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- Metropolitan Transportation Authority -- Long Island Railroad fare increase was properly granted by Metropolitan Transportation Authority; review petition dismissed -- court will take jurisdiction of proceeding which should have been in form of action -- county is proper petitioner -- Metropolitan Transportation Authority need not make regulations governing its hearings and rate determinations.

1. The Metropolitan Transportation Authority is an autonomous, semi-legislative body. It is empowered to establish or raise fares, including those of its wholly owned subsidiary, the Long Island Rail Road Company, "as it may deem necessary, convenient or desirable * * * only after a public hearing" (Public Authorities Law, § 1266, subd. 3). Such a hearing is not subject to the usual requirements of quasi-judicial hearings; rather, it is a quasi-legislative hearing and is held for the Authority's own information. In any event, the hearing met constitutional requirements, and the Authority's decision to raise the railroad's rates was not arbitrary or capricious or abusive of its discretion. Accordingly, this review [***2] petition ( CPLR, art. 78) is dismissed.

2. Even if this should have been an "action" instead of an article 78 "proceeding", the court has jurisdiction ( CPLR 103).

3. The County of Nassau is a proper petitioner or party (Public Service Law, § 109; cf. Public Authorities Law, § 1266, subd. 8).

4. Metropolitan "may" make rules and regulations, which must then be filed with the Secretary of State and published in the Official Compilation of Codes, Rules and Regulations of the State of New York (Public Authorities Law, § 1266, subd. 4; Executive Law, § 102); but it need not make and has not made any rules or regulations governing its public hearings and determinations of fares.

 

2018 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


56 Misc. 2d 557; 289 N.Y.S.2d 71; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1620



March 29, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, it is ordered that the judgments of conviction are hereby affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a judgment from the Police Justice Court of the Incorporated Village of Rockville Centre (New York), which convicted them of disturbing the peace in violation of Village of Rockville Centre, N.Y., Ordinance 1.3(b).


OVERVIEW: At approximately 4:20 p.m. defendants had congregated in front of the office of the Rockville Centre Urban Renewal Agency. They had asked to see the Director of the Urban Renewal Agency in order to present certain grievances. At about 4:30 p.m. the Director had returned to the office and told defendants that the office was closed, but defendants refused to leave. Subsequently, defendants were convicted of disturbing the peace in violation of the Village of Rockville Center, N.Y., Ordinance 1.3(b). On appeal, defendants claimed that their actions were protected by U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. The court noted that the State could regulate a public facility, but it had to do so in a reasonable and non-discriminatory manner. However, defendants in this case did not assert a claim of discrimination, and the court did not find any indication in the record of discriminatory or unequal application of the ordinance. The court concluded that defendants' refusal to leave within a reasonable time at the conclusion of the workday was unlawful and constituted a violation of § 1.3(b).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendants of disturbing the peace in violation of the village ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disturbing peace -- defendants were properly convicted of violating village ordinance against disturbing peace where they refused to leave office of village agency at closing time.

Defendants, who entered the office of a village's Urban Renewal Agency at 4:20 p.m. and refused to leave when the closing time of the agency arrived although warned that they would be arrested if they failed to do so, violated by their conduct an ordinance entitled "Disturbing the peace" and providing that anyone who "Acts in such manner as to annoy, disturb, interfere with, obstruct or be offensive to others" is guilty of violating the ordinance. Their conviction is affirmed.

 

2019 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Paddock


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Special Sessions of New York, Village of Dansville


56 Misc. 2d 123; 288 N.Y.S.2d 96; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1659



March 12, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The charge should be and is dismissed and the defendant discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1220(a) for throwing refuse on a public highway. Defendant claimed that as to him the statute, because of the penalties prescribed by the Vehicle and Traffic Law and mandated by it, violated the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11, and was, therefore, invalid.


OVERVIEW: The provisions of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1220 were formerly found in a section of the old penal law, which provided a penalty of a fine of not less than $ 5 nor more than $ 50. In all cases arising out of a conviction of any section of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 514(3) mandated that the conviction had to have been indorsed on the license, enabling a magistrate, in the future, to have knowledge of the former conviction. That brought into effect and made mandatory an extra and more severe penalty prescribed by § 1800 in the case of a licensee, as opposed to one that did not have a driver's license. The court dismissed the charge and discharged defendant. The court found that because of the different forms of treatment afforded violators of § 1220 and the unnatural classifications of violators and penalties to be imposed for such transgressions, § 1220 violated the rights and privileges afforded persons under both the United States Constitution and the Constitution of New York State.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charge and discharged defendant, who was charged with violating a particular section of the Vehicle and Traffic Law for throwing refuse on a public highway.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- littering -- penalties for littering highways apply very unequally as between licensed operators and unlicensed persons or pedestrians, hence statute ( Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1220, subd. [a]) is unconstitutional -- court suggests that substance of statute be transferred to Penal Law or Conservation Law.

1. The Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 1220, subd. [a]) provides that "No person shall throw * * * upon any highway * * * any refuse, trash, garbage * * * or offensive matter." This, and the general provisions for fines ( Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1800), seem to apply equally to motorists and pedestrians. But holders of operator's licenses are subjected to additional penalties ( Vehicle and Traffic Law, §§ 510, 514) which are inapplicable to, or seem to be administratively unenforcible against, nonlicensed persons. Moreover, licensed or unlicensed, a person who is under 18 and who lives with his parent or guardian is visited with a penalty which is not inflicted upon one who does not reside with his parent or guardian ( Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 514; Code Crim. Pro., § 335-a).  [***2] These inequalities render section 1220 (subd. [a]) of the Vehicle and Traffic Law unconstitutional, and therefore the defendant in this case is discharged.

2. The Justice suggests that the Legislature repeal section 1220 (subd. [a]) of the Vehicle and Traffic Law and, if it wishes, enact its substance as part of the Penal Law or as part of the Conservation Law.

 

2020 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Federated Homes, Inc. v. Berman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


56 Misc. 2d 160; 288 N.Y.S.2d 348; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1677



March 5, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition to set aside the Administrator's determination is denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord sought to set aside a determination of respondent City Rent and Rehabilitation Administrator, which fixed maximum rents for certain parcels of land.


OVERVIEW: The landlord's petition to reverse the determination, which denied its protests regarding the parcels, was based on the contention that neither the state legislature nor the city council could have constitutionally imposed rent control on the parcels of land. The court found that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y51-1.0 was enacted to deal with the emergency living needs created by war and the effects and aftermath of war hostilities. The court held that it was not to substitute its judgment for that of the City Council where there were facts that supported the legislature's decision to act. The court rejected the landlord's contention that if the parcels could have constitutionally been placed under control, they were subject to decontrol pursuant to Rent, Eviction, and Rehabilitation Regulations, § 2(e)(2)(i)(4) and (f)(12). The court concluded that the Administrator correctly ruled that the "housing accommodations" involved were not subject to decontrol under the regulations because they authorized decontrol only of "one or two-family houses" and not of the land under the houses, which land was the "housing accommodation" subject to control.


OUTCOME: The petition to set aside the Administrator's determination was denied.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- rent control laws validly and constitutionally cover parcels of land which were rented before May 1, 1950 for purpose of permitting tenants to erect their own private dwellings and on which such dwellings were erected, even though such dwellings became vacant after April 1, 1953 and were thereafter sold to present tenants of such parcels -- although Rent Administrator previously decontrolled similar parcels, that was mistake of law and he is not obligated to repeat it.

1. Rent control laws validly and constitutionally cover "any plot or parcel of land (as distinguished from any building constructed or placed thereon) * * * which was rented prior to" May 1, 1950 "for the purpose of permitting the tenant thereof to construct his own private dwelling * * * thereon and on which there exists such a private dwelling owned and occupied by a tenant of such plot or parcel" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-3.0, subd. e, par. 1). Accordingly, the New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Administrator properly fixed a maximum rent of approximately [***2] $ 25 a month for each of 250 parcels of such land on each of which the tenant thereof had erected his own one-family bungalow which cost him as much as or more than the value of the land. Each of said bungalows had become vacant on or after October 1, 1953 and had thereafter been purchased by the present tenant of said parcel. The statute provides for decontrol of "Housing accommodations in one or two family houses which were or shall become vacant on or after" April 1, 1953 (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-3.0, subd. e, par. 2, subpar. [i], cl. [4]; N. Y. City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations, § 2, subd. f, par. [12]); but this was not meant to apply to such parcels of land. These statutes do not invidiously discriminate against landlords of such parcels. If the rents of such parcels of land were decontrolled, many of the tenants would have to sell or dispose of their bungalows at substantial sacrifices, since the landlords would be able to exact" unjust, unreasonable and oppressive rents" contrary to the legislative intent (L. 1962, ch. 21, § 1, subd. 2; Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-1.0, subd. a).

2. The City Rent and [***3] Rehabilitation Administrator had decontrolled 7 such parcels of land before he realized his mistake of law and properly refused to decontrol these 250 parcels. He is not required by law to repeat his mistakes.

 

2021 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carmichael


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Genesee County


56 Misc. 2d 388; 288 N.Y.S.2d 931; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1694



February 29, 1968 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State sought review of a decision by the Special Sessions Court for the Town of Oakfield (New York), which held that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 381(6) was unconstitutional. Appellee motorcycle rider had been charged with riding a motorcycle without a helmet in violation of the law.


OVERVIEW: The motorcycle rider was charged with violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 381(6) for riding his motorcycle without wearing a helmet. The trial court dismissed the charge and held the statute unconstitutional. The State sought review, and the court reversed. The court held that the statute was validly enacted for a legitimate purpose and did not unduly restrict the rider's rights. The court stated that a determination of the law's validity depended on whether the State was entitled to regulate the proscribed conduct under its police power. The court defined the State's police power as the means by which the State exercised supervision over matters regarding the common welfare and enforced the observance by each member of society of the duties he or she owed to others and to the community at large. The court found that § 381(6) was enacted as the result of an extensive study of accidents involving motorcycles and the number of head injuries that resulted. The court held that the challenged legislation represented a valid purpose of legislative action under the State's police power. The court also held that the means used to accomplish that purpose were reasonable and not oppressive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a decision that held unconstitutional a State law that required motorcyclists to wear helmets.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- motorcycles -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 381, subd. 6), requiring motorcyclists to wear protective helmets, is valid and constitutional.

Subdivision 6 of section 381 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, requiring motorcyclists to wear protective, reflectorized helmets which must meet specifications promulgated by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles, is a valid and constitutional exercise of the police power and of the legislative power to delegate administrative duties. Accordingly, on appeal by the People, a dismissal by a town Court of Special Sessions of an information against an unhelmeted motorcyclist is reversed by the County Court.

 

2022 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Grimm v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


56 Misc. 2d 525; 289 N.Y.S.2d 358; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1721



February 19, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city moved for summary judgment on plaintiff gun owners' action to declare unconstitutional and permanently enjoin the enforcement of the city's local gun control law.


OVERVIEW: The city's local gun control law provided that a permit and registration was required for a rifle or a shotgun. It enumerated those to whom the issuance of a permit would not be in the interest of public health, safety, or welfare. The gun owners sued to have the law declared unconstitutional and to permanently enjoin its enforcement. The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment. The court held that the gun owners did not meet their burden of demonstrating unconstitutionality. The gun control law was reasonably related to an actual manifest evil, and represented a reasonable means for attempting to solve the problem. The gun owners did not demonstrate that the gun control law in any way exceeded the city's police power. The gun owners did not support their contention that the field of gun control was preempted by the state. The standards of the gun control law were not so vague and indefinite as to be unconstitutional; rather, the law set down an intelligible principle and standards. The gun control law was regulatory, not aimed at persons inherently suspect of criminal activities; thus, it was not violative of the constitutional privilege of self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court awarded summary judgment to the city on the action of the gun owners to declare the city's local gun control law unconstitutional and to permanently enjoin the enforcement of it.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- control of guns -- New York City's Gun Control Law is constitutional -- subject has not been pre-empted by State -- standards of law are not vague -- law does not violate Second Amendment of United States Constitution.

1. The Gun Control Law enacted by Local Law No. 106 of 1967 of the City of New York is constitutional. By its terms, it does not deprive the average citizen of the use and enjoyment of rifles or shotguns. It provides that no person shall be denied a permit unless he falls within the disabilities of the law. It promotes the public welfare and safety and falls within the police power. The city has been granted police power in the Constitution, the Municipal Home Rule Law and the General City Law and the Gun Control Law is not in conflict with the Constitution nor inconsistent with the general laws of the State.

2. Article 265 of the Penal Law, while it touches upon the possession of rifles or shotguns by persons under the age of 16 years, aliens, convicted felons and adjudicated incompetents, does not treat so extensively with the subject as to evidence any design or intention by the State to pre-empt the entire field.

3. The standards [***2] of the Gun Control Law are not so vague and indefinite as to be unconstitutional.

4. The Second Amendment of the United States Constitution is not a limitation on the States and so the Gun Control Law does not violate the Second Amendment.

 

2023 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rice v. State


Claim No. 48755


Court of Claims of New York


55 Misc. 2d 964; 287 N.Y.S.2d 263; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1740



February 13, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The State's motion to dismiss the claim must be and hereby is granted. The claimant's cross motion for summary judgment must be and hereby is denied. The claim must be and hereby is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant delegate filed an action against defendant State seeking the balance allegedly owed him between the sum that he was paid for the 72 days that he worked and the salary that delegates who worked the 176 days the Constitutional Convention was in session. The State filed a motion to dismiss the claim for lack of jurisdiction and failure to state a cause of action. The delegate filed a cross motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: A candidate was elected delegate to the Convention. The votes were recounted, and it was determined that there had been a tie between the candidate and delegate. In a special election, the delegate was certified the winner. He served 72 days out of a total of 176 days during which the Convention was in session. He was paid for the 72 days he worked. The delegate alleged that the Comptroller should have paid the full amount because he had a contract or the Comptroller was tortiously withholding the balance. The court held that the delegate failed to state a cause of action against the State. The State was not liable to him for the full salary. In spite of his election, he could not have assumed his duties until he was permitted to take the oath by the Convention. After he took the oath, the Comptroller was notified that he was discharging his duties as a delegate, and it was then that he was entitled to be paid for services rendered. The delegate was seeking payment for the days in which he neither was qualified to nor discharged the duties of a delegate. Payment of for the full sum would amount to a gift, which was prohibited by N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 8.


OUTCOME: The court granted the State's motion to dismiss the delegate's claim, denied the delegates' cross motion for summary judgment, and dismissed his claim.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- New York State Constitutional Convention -- delegate who was elected in special election after Constitutional Convention of 1967 began and who thus served only 72 days was properly paid $ 85.22 per day; he has no claim against State for difference between $ 6,135.84 thus paid to him and $ 15,000 which was paid to other delegates who served for full period of 176 days.

Delegates to the New York State Constitutional Convention of 1967 were elected in November, 1966. In the Thirty-third Senate District, Bronx County, one Santangelo was certified by the local Board of Elections as elected. In January, 1967, claimant Rice, who had also run, commenced proceedings for a recanvass of the votes; and thus in May, 1967 the Court of Appeals decided that there had been a tie vote. Meantime, in April, 1967, the Convention assembled and began to work. Delegate Santangello had taken the oath of office, and he was properly seated and he performed his duties and he was properly [***2] paid. After the Court of Appeals decision, the Convention passed a resolution asking the Governor to call a special election for delegate in the Thirty-third Senate District. The Governor did so and the election was held on July 11, 1967 and the local board certified Rice as elected. Rice took the oath of office on July 17, 1967 and was properly seated. He was a delegate for the 72 days from that date until the Convention closed on September 26, 1967. The President of the Convention certified the payrolls and the State Comptroller paid them. The delegates were paid $ 15,000 for the total of 176 days. This averaged $ 85.22 a day. Accordingly, delegate Rice was paid $ 85.22 x 72=$ 6,135.84. His claim against the State for the difference between $ 6,135.84 and $ 15,000 is dismissed. (N. Y. Const., art. V, 1; art. VII, § 8; art. XIX, § 2; Legislative Law, § 5; 2 NYCRR 3.4, 3.6; Rules of 1967 N. Y. Constitutional Convention, rule VIII.)

 

2024 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Walkenhorst


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Special Sessions of New York, Village of Rockville Centre, Nassau County


55 Misc. 2d 935; 287 N.Y.S.2d 760; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1868



February 13, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with violating Village of Rockville Centre, N.Y., Ordinance 1.13, which prohibited soliciting on private residential property. They made motions to dismiss the informations against them.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance did not apply to persons who had been residents of the village for six months, who had maintained a place of business in the village for six months, and or to the duly authorized representative of such a resident or businessperson. Defendants were distributing newspapers door to door at private residences. Neither fell within the exception to the ordinance. The court stated first that the New York Court of Appeals had held that similar ordinances did not violate New York law. Furthermore, the ordinance did not violate free speech or due process because nothing prevented defendants from hiring a village resident to distribute the newspaper, mailing the newspaper, or distributing the newspaper in public places in the village. The ordinance, the purpose of which was to protect residents from having their private property entered upon, merely set forth how distribution could be legally effectuated. As for defendants' contention that the ordinance violated equal protection, the limitations imposed were germane to the purpose of the legislation. Because defendants had admitted they violated the ordinance, the court found them guilty.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motions to dismiss, then found them guilty of violating the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- distributing literature on private residential property -- Rockville Centre Village Ordinance (1.13) forbidding nonresidents' entering private residential property to distribute pamphlets was not invalid as violating legal and constitutional rights on due process of law and freedom of speech, press and expression or, because not applicable to residents, equal protection of laws (U. S. Const., 1st Amdt., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 6, 8, 11; General Municipal Law, § 80); ordinance was designed to protect residents from trespass and inconvenience; did not prevent defendants' distributing their experimental newspaper pamphlets by other means, and did not curtail freedom of speech or expression -- ordinance was as validly applicable to defendant's noncommercial venture as to commercial distribution; defendants who admitted acts charged were found guilty of violating ordinance.

1. Defenants, nonresidents of the Village of Rockville Centre, who distributed their experimental newspaper pamphlets door to door at private residences in the village by affixing them to doorknobs with rubber bands, were found guilty of violating an ordinance of the village [***2] (Ordinances of Village of Rockville Centre, ordinance 1.13) prohibiting nonresidents of the village from entering upon private residential property for the purpose, inter alia, of "distributing any * * * pamphlet" without the prior consent of the occupant thereof. The ordinance did not violate those provisions of the Constitution and laws of this State relating to Due Process of Law, Freedom of Speech, Press and Expression, and Equal Protection of the Laws (N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 6, 8, 11; General Municipal Law, § 80). ( People v. Bohnke, 287 N. Y. 154, and Watchtower Bible & Track Soc. v. Metropolitan Life Ins. Co., 297 N. Y. 339, held controlling.)

2. Moreover, the ordinance did not violate provisions of the United States Constitution (1st Amdt., 14th Amdt.) for freedom of speech and press, equal protection of the laws and due process of law. The ordinance was designed to assist the rights of village residents to privacy and to be free from trespass or inconvenience, and was in no way violative of free speech or expression. Defendants could have mailed, or hired residents to distribute, their literature, and, furthermore, nothing in the ordinance prevented [***3] their personally distributing their pamphlets at public places or on streets within the village. Accordingly, there was no curtailing of anyone's views; no curtailing of freedom of speech or expression, and no depriving defendants of due process of law.

3. The limitations of the ordinance, applicable to nonresidents but not to residents of the village for six months or more, were germane to the purposes of the legislation and did not violate the equal protection of the laws provisions of the Federal and State Constitutions and the laws of the State.

4. Defendants' noncommercial venture would not differ from a commercial distribution with respect to the applicability of the ordinance since the purpose thereof was to protect village residents' persons and property from unauthorized entry into their private residences by those unknown in the community.

5. Defendants having admitted the acts which constituted a violation of the ordinance, were found guilty of violating the ordinance.

Kenzie v. Travia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


55 Misc. 2d 1016; 286 N.Y.S.2d 965; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1759



February 6, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss the amended complaint granted, on the ground that it fails to state a cause of action (cf. Shanker v. Regents of Univ. of State of N. Y. supra).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, members of Board of Regents (New York), filed a motion to have plaintiff voter's amended complaint dismissed for failure to state a cause of action. The voter asserted that he and other voters were denied their equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, when the board met under N.Y. Educ. Law § 202, and weighed votes for the position of regent based on an equal valuation of the rights of state senators and assemblymen.


OVERVIEW: A voter asserted that members of the Board of Regents had violated his equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, when the board selected a regent. In reaching this decision, the board met under N.Y. Educ. Law § 202, and weighed votes for the position based on the voted of state senators and assemblymen. The voter asserted that the board improperly valuated the votes of those state representatives equally when it ought to have counted the votes of state senators more, as there were over two assemblymen for each senator. The court dismissed the voter's amended complaint for failure to state a cause of action and found that the principle of "one man, one vote", was not relevant to this matter because regents were not the elected representatives of the voter. In its decision, the court noted that the Board of Regents was an administrative board pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law, §§ 201, 202, 206, 208-211, 213-219, 223, 229-233, 235-237, 239, 245-250, 254, 263, 267, 269, and 292, and not a legislative body.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss the amended complaint.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- election of Board of Regents -- members of Board of Regents are validly elected by joint session of Legislature (Education Law, § 202), even though Senate has only 57 members as against 150 Assemblymen; citizen's suit dismissed.

The Education Law ( § 202) provides that members of the State Board of Regents shall be elected by the Legislature at a meeting in joint session. Thus, 150 Assemblymen cast 150 votes and 57 Senators cast only 57 votes. However, there is no constitutional requirement that each Senator's vote be weighted 2.62. This action by a citizen is dismissed.
