Uberman v. Lasner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


55 Misc. 2d 1027; 287 N.Y.S.2d 464; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1763



February 5, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, plaintiff's motion to dismiss such counterclaim is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord sought summary judgment in his declaratory judgment to exclude defendant tenant from the protection of the rent control law because the tenant was a "wealthy person." The landlord also sought dismissal of the tenant's counterclaim for money damages. The tenant sought summary judgment dismissing the landlord's complaint and for judgment on his counterclaim.


OVERVIEW: The tenant had lived in the apartment since 1936. When the rent control legislation became effective, the tenant became a statutory tenant that enjoyed all the benefits and protections of the legislation provided that he did not default on his rent payments or otherwise violate a substantial obligation of his tenancy. The landlord contended that the rent control legislation was only applicable to tenants who were not wealthy. He claimed the statute was unconstitutional because it protected wealthy tenants. Upon review, the court found that the landlord failed to establish that the rent control law made the distinction between wealthy and nonwealthy tenants. Therefore, equal protection applied to all tenants who came within the bounds of controlled housing. If the landlord wished to have the rent control law amended, he needed to seek the amendment from the legislature. The court granted the tenant's motion to dismiss the landlord's complaint for failure to state a cause of action. It also dismissed the tenant's counterclaim for money damages because there was no justiciable cause of action set forth.


OUTCOME: The court granted the tenant's motion for summary judgment to dismiss the landlord's action. The court granted the landlord's motion to dismiss the tenant's counterclaim.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- rent control law -- wealthy tenants, as well as poor tenants, are equally entitled to protection of rent control laws.

The rent control law and regulations of the City of New York protect wealthy tenants and poor tenants equally (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.). Declaratory judgment is granted accordingly to defendant, an allegedly wealthy tenant; but his counterclaim against his landlord for damages for abuse of process is dismissed.

 

2027 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Powsner v. Mills


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


56 Misc. 2d 411; 288 N.Y.S.2d 846; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1779



January 30, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff filed a motion under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211, subd. (c) to dismiss defendant's affirmative defenses of lack of personal or subject matter jurisdiction and the law that plaintiff relied upon in obtaining jurisdiction was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a New York resident and she brought an action against defendant, a New Jersey resident, for personal injuries she sustained in a car accident that occurred in New Jersey. Plaintiff obtained an order of attachment under which a levy was made against the interest of defendant on the obligation of her insurer to indemnify her under an automobile liability policy it issued to her. Defendant initially moved to dismiss the complaint for lack of jurisdiction but withdrew the motion and served her answer. Upon review, the court found it had subject matter jurisdiction over the action. Plaintiff's rights were not prejudiced by defendant's withdrawal of her motion. Because the case was based upon in rem jurisdiction, defendant's appearance did not subject her to the jurisdiction of the court because she raised an objection to the court's personal jurisdiction in her answer through an affirmative defense. Only if defendant proceeded with a defense after asserting her objection to jurisdiction would she subject herself to the court's jurisdiction. Defendant's affirmative defense on the constitutionality of the statute under which plaintiff obtained jurisdiction was stricken.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff's motion to dismiss defendant's affirmative defense of lack of personal jurisdiction. The court struck defendant's affirmative defense of lack of subject matter jurisdiction and the defense that the law upon which plaintiff relied in obtaining jurisdiction over defendant was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Appearances -- in rem -- in motor vehicle accident action against New Jersey resident, plaintiff attached defendant's interest in liability policy; defendant appeared and moved to dismiss complaint for lack of jurisdiction of her person, then withdrew motion and served answer containing same objection; since no further proceedings have yet been taken, plaintiff's motion to strike this affirmative defense is denied ( CPLR 320, subd. [c]).

This is an action by a New York resident against a New Jersey resident for damages for personal injuries suffered in an automobile accident in New Jersey. Plaintiff obtained an order of attachment under which a levy was made in New York on defendant's interest in the obligation of an automobile liability insurance company to indemnify defendant ( Seider v. Roth, 17 N Y 2d 111, followed). Defendant appeared by attorneys, and at first moved to dismiss the complaint for lack of jurisdiction over her person, but then withdrew her motion, and served an answer which, among other things, raised this same assertion that the court has no jurisdiction over her person. This tactic was her right. Since no further proceedings have as yet been [***2] taken, plaintiff's motion to strike this affirmative defense as having no merit ( CPLR 3211) is denied ( CPLR 320, subds. [b], [c]).

Greenburgh v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


55 Misc. 2d 1031; 287 N.Y.S.2d 154; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1791



January 29, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, members of the League of Women Voters, brought a motion to vacate a judgment, which approved a plan of reapportionment of respondent Westchester County Board of Supervisors.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that the original reapportionment plan was found unconstitutional. The plan challenged by the instant motion was the fourth plan, which was approved by the court from a constitutional standpoint, but was overwhelmingly defeated by popular vote. By its motion, the League of Women Voters sought to vacate the court's judgment and asked the court to order a reapportionment on the basis of single-member districts, or, to fix a time limitation within which a valid plan shall be proposed by the Board of Supervisors. The court said that it would have been a usurpation of legislative function for it to hold that the Board had to be apportioned on the basis of single-member districts. However, the court granted that portion of the request that sought a time limitation.


OUTCOME: The court fixed a time limitation within which a valid plan was ordered to have been proposed by the Board of Supervisors.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- judgment determining that plan of reapportionment adopted by Board of Supervisors of Westchester County is constitutional cannot be reversed by vote of People rejecting such apportionment -- judgment amended to provide that any party can apply at foot thereof for further relief -- Board of Supervisors directed to adopt constitutional plan of apportionment by April 15, 1968 in default of which court will create one.

1. A judgment of the court entered August 30, 1967 determining that a plan of reapportionment proposed by the Board of Supervisors of Westchester County was constitutional will not be vacated even though the People in a referendum rejected it. Such judgment cannot be reversed by popular vote.

2. The judgment is amended, however, to authorize any party to apply at the foot of the judgment for further relief.

3. The application of a group of plaintiffs is granted and the Board of Supervisors is directed to adopt a constitutional plan of apportionment subject to judicial review as to constitutionality and subject to popular referendum as to acceptability, by no later than April 15, 1968 in default of [***2] which the court will devise a plan. The submission of a plan substantially similar to the last one, so overwhelmingly rejected by the People, without providing any alternative method of reapportionment, would have to be regarded as a failure to act.

People v. Newhouse


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Ithaca


55 Misc. 2d 1064; 287 N.Y.S.2d 713; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1806



January 22, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion is denied and the case is set down for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss a ticket issued to him for violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 381(6) for not wearing a helmet while operating a motorcycle. Defendant contended that § 381(6) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's motion to dismiss alleged that § 381(6) was unconstitutional on the grounds that it was too vague and indefinite and that it unreasonably limited one's personal freedom. In denying defendant's motion, the court held (1) that the fact that the statute delegated authority to the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles to adopt regulations with respect to the type of helmet did not render the statute unconstitutional; (2) that § 381(6) was not vague and indefinite since the court of appeals had found that other statutes dealing with imprudent speed and noisy mufflers were not unconstitutionally vague; and (3) that the enactment of legislation requiring the use of helmets was a reasonable exercise of police power and did not unconstitutionally limit one's personal freedom.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- wearing helmet while operating motorcycle -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 381, subd. 6) requiring motorcyclist to wear helmet is constitutional.

Subdivision 6 of section 381 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which requires any person operating or riding on a motorcycle to wear a protective helmet, and which authorizes the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles to adopt regulations and to establish a list of approved helmets, is valid and constitutional.

 

2030 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sterling Optical Co. v. University of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Albany County


55 Misc. 2d 852; 287 N.Y.S.2d 827; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1812



January 19, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, corporate employers and registered ophthalmic dispensers, sought a declaratory judgment from the court to declare state regulations that prohibited plaintiffs from using advertisements and window displays unconstitutional. Defendant government agencies were either responsible for promulgating the rules or were in charge of their enforcement. Intervenor, licensed optometrists, sought a judgment upholding the regulations.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed an action seeking to have state regulations that absolutely banned advertising or window displays on the part of corporations employing optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers declared void and unconstitutional. The court granted a judgment in favor of plaintiffs. The court held that the regulations were unconstitutional because no reasonable basis existed for the ban. The court noted that the regulations strengthened self-employed optometrists because they were allowed to advertise and the regulations weakened plaintiffs because they were not allowed to do the same. In addition, the court found that the regulations were invalid because they were not shown to have an adequate connection with the preservation of public health, safety, and welfare. The court determined that notices mailed to plaintiffs by an administrative agency were invalid because they failed to notify the person accused of wrongdoing of his wrongful act in clear and unmistakable language. The court determined the notices improperly contained "shotgun" charges and that the regulations authorizing such notices were illegal and unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The court granted a judgment in favor of plaintiffs and declared unconstitutional state regulations that prohibited plaintiffs from using advertisements and window displays.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- regulation of optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers -- in action to declare invalid certain regulations of State Education Commissioner applicable to optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers, such regulations (8 NYCRR 66.1 [a] [9] [11]) governing window displays and advertising by licensed optometrists were not found to have any rational relation to public health, safety and welfare, but to interfere with private business permitted by statute (Education Law, § 7109) and to favor State Optometric Association, which drafted such regulations, over plaintiff corporation which employed optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers; no findings made as to validity of regulations of advertising by ophthalmic dispensers (8 NYCRR 67.1 [a] [3] [4]); other regulations (8 NYCRR 66.1 [a] [12] before amdt. of May 26, 1967; 8 NYCRR 67.1 [a] [5]), requiring optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers to cease employment upon Education Department's notice of employer's violating regulations as to advertising, also held illegal and unenforcible and notices thereunder declared null and void; court recommends legislative or departmental hearing of all parties interested  [***2] in eye care to resolve existing animosities, correct shocking disdain for public needs and provide for School of Optometry within State.

1. Regulations of the Commissioner of Education applicable to licensed optometrists which governed "Window Display" and "Advertising" (8 NYCRR 66.1 [a] [9] [11]) were not found to have any rational relationship to the public health, safety and welfare. To the contrary, evidence showed that such regulations (1) were drafted by the State Optometric Association a private membership corporation, in accord with its general policy of eliminating eyeglass dispensing by plaintiff and similar corporations which employ licensed optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers; (2) did not, when promulgated, represent the prevailing practice of the majority of optometrists; (3) undiscriminatingly banned all optometric window display and advertising in contravention of decisions of this State's courts, and (4) were approved by the Board of Regents solely upon representations made by the association's officers with no independent investigation of whether these rules would benefit the general public rather than the profession or part thereof involved. Accordingly,  [***3] in this action by plaintiff corporation to have these and other regulations applying to ophthalmic dispensers and optometrists declared unconstitutional, illegal and unenforcible, the court found that no reasonable basis existed for an absolute ban against window displays or advertising on the part of corporations employing optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers in the sale of eyeglasses, and, since these regulations were not shown to have adequate connection with preserving public health, safety and welfare, that their enforcement would deprive plaintiff of a property right in operating its business of dispensing eyeglasses.

2. Moreover, an amendment to the "Advertising" regulation, promulgated during the trial of this action and allowing self-employed optometrists to display up to five signs, gave them a competitive advantage over plaintiff and similar corporate employers of optometrists who were denied this privilege. Such regulations which aid one group against another without benefitting the general public are condemned.

3. Furthermore, the Legislature, by granting in 1928 to corporations such as plaintiff the right to employ optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers (Education [***4] Law, § 7109), by implication permitted such corporations to continue their business without interference so long as the public interests were protected, and these regulations not only interfere with the operation of a private business but also prevent the corporations from truthfully advising the public of their limited authority to examine their customers' eyes. The statute (legislation to repeal which was sponsored by the Optometric Association but vetoed by the Governor in 1967) allowing plaintiff to hire optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers, should not be emasculated step by step by the Regents' promulgating regulations based on proposals by the Optometric Association.

4. Those parts of the Commissioner's Regulations which prohibit licensed optometrists (8 NYCRR 66.1 [a] [12] before amdt. of May 26, 1967) and ophthalmic dispensers (8 NYCRR 67.1 [a] [5]) from continuing employment after receiving written notice from the Department that their employer is violating those regulations relating to conduct or avertising, are likewise illegal and unenforcible and such departmental notices served thereunder upon optometrists and ophthalmic dispensers employed by plaintiff corporation,  [***5] threatening them with disciplinary action and possible license revocation, are declared null and void. The Department here notified the optometrists that plaintiff had advertised in conflict with 8 separate paragraphs of the applicable regulations whereas there was actually only one such paragraph possibly violated. Likewise, the Department's notice to the ophthalmic dispensers charged that plaintiff's advertising violated 4 separate paragraphs of the regulations whereas no such violations had occurred. Such notices violated the fundamental legal principle that an accused person would be informed of the wrongful act charged in clear and unmistakable language without being obliged to conjecture as to the charge. "Shotgun" charges should not be leveled by anyone in authority against a professional man who should not have to determine whether he must leave his employment or continue thereat with the possibility of suspension of his license.

5. However, the Department on May 26, 1967, after this action was commenced, amended one of the regulations (8 NYCRR 66.1 [a] [12]) and since the amendment provides for a statement of the specific violations alleged on the part of the employer [***6] and fixes a date for a preliminary hearing with the right to be represented by counsel, the illegality of the original regulation has been corrected and, therefore, it is now valid.

6. With respect to plaintiff's contention that regulations governing "Price Advertising" (8 NYCRR 67.1 [a] [3]) and "Advertising Gratutitous Services" (8 NYCRR 67.1 [a] [4]) and applicable to ophthalmic dispensers, are also illegal and unenforcible, the court declined to pass on the legality of these regulations since no convincing proof was offered that plaintiff corporation engaged in either activity at the time the regulations were promulgated.

7. The court recommends that the Legislature or the Education Department should bring together at a hearing all parties interested in eye care in order to resolve existing animosities, to enact corrective legislation and regulations to overcome the shocking disdain shown for public needs, and to provide for the establishment of a School of Optometry within this State which at present has no such institution.

 

2031 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Montalvo v. Montalvo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York City


55 Misc. 2d 699; 286 N.Y.S.2d 605; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1808



January 19, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner wife sought to transfer a proceeding back to a court of criminal jurisdiction pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 816(a); respondent husband was accused of felonious assault of the wife.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that a proceeding involving truly criminal conduct should be transferred from the Family Court to a criminal court. The court also noted that a reconciliation between the parties seemed improbable. The court's difficulty in estimating whether husband was dangerous to his wife or the community was intensified by the lack of a psychiatric evaluation. The husband had no criminal record, and the wife's testimony and manner at the hearing indicated more a desire for vengeance than a fear of her husband. The court found that the retention of the proceeding would not help the family in some fundamental respects and that the husband could be dangerous, and noted that a societal interest in criminal prosecution for an extremely grave assault. The court also noted that the husband would not in any event be spared the detriment of criminal processes as he was subject to trial on a weapons charge regardless of whether the court retained jurisdiction over the assaultive acts.


OUTCOME: The proceeding was transferred to the Criminal Court of New York City.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Family Court -- jurisdiction of crimes -- indictment against husband for assault and attempted murder of his wife is void, since complaint to Criminal Court of assault between members of family must first be transferred to Family Court -- but indictment is valid as to portion which alleges unlawful possession of weapon -- Family Court determines that its own processes are "inappropriate" in this particular case, and hence transfers proceeding back to New York City Criminal Court (Family Ct. Act, §§ 811, 816, 821) -- court suggests that Family Court Act be amended so as to provide that alleged offender's statements and testimony in such transfer hearing or proceeding in Family Court shall not be admissible in any criminal prosecution against him.

1. A husband shot and wounded his wife, after she had gone to live with their married child and for six weeks had repeatedly rebuffed his attempts at reconciliation. He was arraigned in the New York City Criminal Court on a charge of felonious assault. Subsequently he was indicted by a Grand Jury for attempted first degree murder, first degree assault, and unlawful possession of a firearm. Since the Family Court has original [***2] jurisdiction of assaults and family offenses, and since the indictment was obtained without first obtaining an order of the Family Court determining "that the processes of the family court are inappropriate" (Family Ct. Act, § 816 [formerly numbered 814], subd. [a]) the indictment is void as to the alleged assault and attempted murder ( People v. Johnson, 20 N Y 2d 220, followed). However, the indictment is valid as to the firearms count.

2. After the indictment was obtained, the criminal proceeding was transferred to the Family Court. The latter has conducted a hearing, and has determined that its retention of jurisdiction would not fundamentally help this family, that this husband is possibly dangerous to the safety of his wife and of the public, that there is a societal or public interest in criminally prosecuting this crime, that anyway this husband is subject to criminal prosecution on a weapons charge, and that, in this particular case "the processes of the family court are inappropriate" (Family Ct. Act, § 816, subd. [a]; § 811; see, also, §§ 812-817; §§ 821, 822, 842, 845, 846; Family Ct. Rules, rule 8.3); and accordingly the Family Court now transfers this [***3] proceeding back to the New York City Criminal Court.

3. In view of the alleged offender's constitutional privilege against making any admissions on direct examination which might incriminate him or which might open the door to an incriminating cross-examination, and in view of his privilege of keeping his communications with physicians confidential, and in view of the requirements of due process, the Family Court is inhibited, under the present statute governing such a hearing as this, from compelling important testimony and unsworn statements and a psychiatric examination, and the alleged offender is similarly inhibited from talking freely in court and in chambers and in conference. Accordingly, the court suggests that the Legislature amend the Family Court Act so as to provide expressly that the alleged offender's testimony and statements in such a hearing or proceeding shall not be admissible in any criminal prosecution against him (cf. Family Ct. Act, § 824, and Code Crim. Pro., §§ 312-d, 913-i).

 

2032 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rockland County Builders Asso. v. McAlevey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


55 Misc. 2d 695; 286 N.Y.S.2d 431; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1869



January 18, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a membership corporation representing builders and the members of the corporation, brought an action against defendants, the Town Board of the Town of Ramapo and others. Plaintiffs sought an injunction pendente lite restraining the Town from enforcing Town of Ramapo, N.Y., Local Law No. 4.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were engaged in constructing single-family dwellings. The ordinance provided that when such a house was to be erected, the down payment would be held in escrow. The court stated that a local government had only derived powers as created either by the New York Constitution or its legislature. Here, neither the constitution nor statutes had conferred the power to enact such an ordinance upon the town. Furthermore, the town could not impinge upon freedom of contract. The lawful and permissible solution to its concern about protecting homebuyers was to ensure compliance with its building code, not to create, through its own legislation, a fund against which a homeowner could proceed to recoup damages. The court rejected defendants' argument that another town had adopted a similar ordinance. The other town's ordinance was not necessarily valid. Finally, the court stated that although the ordinance had a separability clause, the clause was of no avail because the legislation was so replete with unconstitutional provisions that its basic purpose could not be salvaged.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for an injunction pendente lite.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- local laws -- towns cannot constitutionally require builders to deposit purchasers' down payments in escrow for benefit of those purchasers who might institute suits arising out of defects in workmanship.

1. A town's local law began with a section headed "Legislative Intent", which stated that "The Town Board * * *, deeming it necessary to take adequate steps to protect purchasers of newly-constructed homes * * * and further insure that said homes have been built in accordance with * * * regulations and laws, and in a good and workmanlike manner, hereby determines that the enactment of this local law, pursuant to the powers granted by the Municipal Home Rule Law and the Town Law of the State of New York, will contribute to the public welfare and * * * the orderly development of the Town * * * and to the protection of future home buyers in the Town". The other sections (a) required that, when a builder of one-family or two-family homes contracted to sell a home, the "down payment shall be held in escrow by the attorney for either the seller or purchaser, or * * * a title company * * * or [***2] * * * the Town Clerk", but, if a building permit had already been issued, then such down payment must "be deposited in escrow with the Building Inspector", and (b) empowered the Justice Court of the town to entertain any action brought within one year arising out of any defect in construction or workmanship of such a home. At the end of this local law was a separability clause in the event of partial invalidity. However, the entire local law is unconstitutional.

2. No such power as this, over private contracts, has been delegated to the towns by Constitution or statute, even if the State itself were to be deemed to have such power. A temporary injunction shall issue against any enforcement of this local law.

 

2033 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ferguson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


55 Misc. 2d 823; 286 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1842



January 10, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An indictment charged 17 defendants with the crimes of advocacy of criminal anarchy (count one), conspiracy to commit the crime of criminal anarchy (count four), conspiracy to commit the crime of arson in the third degree (count five), and other substantive crimes. Thirteen defendants interposed demurrers to discrete counts of the indictment by which they were respectively charged with various crimes.


OVERVIEW: Six defendants demurred to the first and fourth counts of the indictment on grounds that the facts stated therein did not constitute a crime and the indictment did not conform with the requirements of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 275, 276, 278, 279. Three defendants also demurred to the fifth count of the indictment on the same grounds. Five defendants demurred to the respective substantive counts charging each with possession of firearms on the ground that the indictment as to those counts violated N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 278, 279. Finally, two defendants demurred to the entire indictment on the ground that it did not conform with the requirements of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 278, 279. The court sustained the demurrers to the first, fourth, and fifth counts of the indictment, primarily because those counts merely reiterated statutory language and contained no particulars concerning the crimes charged. The court overruled the remaining demurrers, holding (1) that the crimes charged were properly joined in one indictment, and (2) that the counts charging the substantive crimes of unlawful possession of firearms suffered no deficiency rendering them demurrable.


OUTCOME: The court sustained defendants' demurrers to the first, fourth and fifth counts of the indictment, with leave to the district attorney to represent the matters to the same or another grand jury. The court overruled defendants' remaining demurrers.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- criminal anarchy -- indictment of advocacy of criminal anarchy (Penal Law, § 161) must sufficiently allege defendants' intent to overthrow government of State of New York, nature of violence they advocated, identity of any books or papers or printed matter, and existence of clear and present danger; free speech is constitutionally protected, and Federal Government has pre-empted crime of advocating overthrow of Federal Government -- indictment charging conspiracy to advocate criminal anarchy likewise insufficient, despite allegation of nine overt acts (Code Crim Pro., §§ 275, 276) -- indictment charging conspiracy to commit arson and setting forth four overt acts, insufficient for failing to identify structures or vehicles to be burnt -- accordingly, demurrers to above 3 counts of 17-count indictment sustained -- District Attorney may resubmit these matters to Grand Jury -- other substantive, connected crimes are properly alleged in separate counts in one indictment (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 278, 279).

1. An indictment charging advocacy of criminal anarchy (Penal Law, § 161) must allege more than advocacy by speech, since free speech is constitutionally protected. The [***2] indictment must allege, further, that the defendants intend to accomplish the overthrow of the government of the State of New York by violence or other unlawful means and that a clear and present danger exists that such advocated overthrow will be attempted or accomplished. The crime of advocating overthrow of the Federal Government has been pre-empted by the Federal Government (U. S. Code, tit. 18, § 2385); and section 161 of our Penal Law does not make it a crime to advocate the violent overthrow of the government of a sister State or of a foreign nation. Further, the indictment must specify the form which the advocacy or teaching or advice took, to whom it was directed, the nature of the violence which was advocated or taught, the officials if any who were to be assassinated, and whatever other acts constituted defendants' crime (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 275, 276), and, moreover, it must identify any papers, documents, books or written or printed matter allegedly advocating such force or violence or other unlawful means.

2. An indictment charging conspiracy to commit the crime of advocacy of criminal anarchy must likewise sufficiently allege the form and the overt acts which the [***3] conspiracy took. Although nine overt acts are set forth in the indictment in this case, they do not sufficiently identify the conspiracy charged so as to inform the defendants of the acts constituting their crime (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 275, 276), to the end that a defense may be prepared and a subsequent charge for the same crime prevented, particularly so since a charge of conspiracy can be sustained by proof of commission of overt acts other than those alleged in the indictment so long as one of the alleged overt acts is proved.

3. An indictment charging conspiracy to commit the crime of arson, and setting forth four overt acts, is likewise insufficient, for failure to identify the arsons and the particular structures or vehicles intended.

4. Therefore, demurrers to the foregoing 3 counts of a 17-count indictment are sustained (Code Crim. Pro., § 323, subds. 2, 4), with leave to the District Attorney to resubmit the matters to the same or another Grand Jury.

5. The indictment properly alleges, in separate counts, other separate substantive crimes which were connected together or were parts of a common plan (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 278, 279; § 323, subd. 3).

 

2034 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ferguson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


55 Misc. 2d 711; 286 N.Y.S.2d 976; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1864



January 2, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion lacks merit and is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion for an order directing the dismissal of an indictment upon the ground that the grand jury which returned an indictment against them was unconstitutionally selected and, therefore, did not acquire jurisdiction to charge them.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that the grand jury was unconstitutionally selected and that the provisions of N.Y. Jud. Law §§ 596, 609, 662, 684(1) were unconstitutional because the eligibility provisions required a juror to be intelligent, of sound mind, and good character, well informed, and a person who had not been convicted of a misdemeanor involving moral turpitude and that such provisions set up an impermissible subjective test for juror qualification to be applied by the jury clerk. Defendants contended that the jury clerk in applying the subjective test was or might have been influenced by prejudice. Defendants further contended that most grand juries in the county consisted mostly of white people and that blacks received token representation on such bodies. The court denied the motion finding that defendants failed to make any factual showing sufficient to raise an issue that there was a deliberate and systematic exclusion of blacks from service on grand juries. The court found that defendants merely presented undocumented, unsupported conclusions and inferences, and the absence of proof of members of an eligible class or race from a particular jury or panel was not proof.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion.


HEADNOTES

Grand jury -- selection -- no proof that County Clerk discriminates against Negroes in selecting grand jurors who are "intelligent; of sound mind and good character; well informed"; further requirement, that they shall not have been convicted of misdemeanor "involving moral turpitude", has been interpreted by courts; motion to dismiss indictment denied.

The Judiciary Law ( § 596; see, also, §§ 609, 662) directs and empowers the County Clerk to select grand jurors who, among other qualifications, are "intelligent; of sound mind and good character; well informed", and who have not been convicted of "a misdemeanor involving moral turpitude." The last-quoted words have been interpreted in a number of judicial decisions; and the other quoted requirements are not so subjective as to give license to a County Clerk to discriminate unconstitutionally against Negroes and low-income groups. The defendants in this case who move to dismiss these indictments of conspiracy to commit criminal anarchy and conspiracy to commit murder are seven Negroes, and their counsel asserts in his brief that, although Negroes comprise one sixth [***2] of the population of this county, only occasionally is a "token" Negro selected to serve on a Grand Jury; but this assertion is not documented or factually supported. Moreover, the United States Supreme Court has ruled that the constitutional guarantees of due process and equal protection do not require that a jury panel contain the same political or economic or racial or religious or national-origin proportions as does the population of the county. Accordingly, these indictments will not be dismissed on the ground asserted by defendants' counsel.

 

2035 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Quintero v. Long Island Railroad


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


55 Misc. 2d 813; 286 N.Y.S.2d 748; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1865



January 2, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is in all respects granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party filed a motion for leave to file a notice of claim and for leave to amend his complaint to allege proper notice or, alternatively, for a declaration that the notice requirements of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e were met in his action against defendant railroad entity to recover for injuries incurred when he was struck by a train.


OVERVIEW: After being struck by a train, the injured party filed a personal injury action against he railroad entity involved. The railroad entity asserted a defense based on the injured party's failure to comply with statutory notice requirements. The injured party filed a motion for leave to file a notice of claim and for leave to amend his complaint to allege proper notice or, alternatively, for a declaration that the notice requirements were met. The court granted the motion in all respects and ordered that the railroad entity was estopped from asserting the notice defense. The court found that the 90-day notice requirement resulted from a "quiet" amendment to the statute, and the bar and litigants were not adequately notified of the amendment. The court concluded that because no rational relationship existed between the statute and any public policy, such a short limitation period was unconstitutional unless the court had discretion to relieve a party from nonprejudicial mistake or inadvertence under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2004.


OUTCOME: The court granted the injured party's motion in all respects, allowing him take the actions necessary to meet the statutory notice requirements to pursue his personal injury action against the railroad entity.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- notice of tort claim -- Long Island Rail Road, subsidiary of Metropolitan Transportation Authority, investigated accident to plaintiff and was served with summons and complaint less than 90 days after accident; plaintiff is granted leave to serve late notice of claim and to amend complaint subsequently to allege that 30 days have elapsed since notice of claim was served (Public Authorities Law, § 1276, subds. 1, 2, 6; General Municipal Law, § 50-e, subds. 2, 3; CPLR 2004) -- railroad is estopped from asserting lack of notice -- court has jurisdiction to grant such leave.

1. Plaintiff was severely injured in an accident on June 30, 1967 by the alleged tort of the Long Island Rail Road. Within a few days after the accident, while plaintiff was undergoing a double amputation in the hospital, the railroad's insurer interviewed witnesses and plaintiff's wife. Counsel was retained by plaintiff on July 7, 1967 and he immediately wrote to the railroad's insurer, notifying it of the accident. On September 26, 1967, which was less than 90 days after the accident, this action was commenced by service of summons and [***2] complaint on the railroad. Plaintiff is granted leave to file a late notice of claim (General Municipal Law, § 50-e; CPLR 2004) and to amend the complaint, 30 days after such filing, so as to allege that 30 days have elapsed since the notice of claim was served (Public Authorities Law, § 1276, subd. 1). The order will also provide, in the alternative, that service of a notice of claim is dispensed with and that the complaint may, if necessary, be amended to provide the specifications of section 50-e (subd. 2) of the General Municipal Law, and for service of duplicate copies under subdivision 3 of said section 50-e. The order will also provide that defendant is estopped from establishing its defense of no notice, and that such defense is stricken.

2. The Metropolitan Transportation Authority was originally created in 1965 and consisted at that time of two public authorities -- the Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority and the New York City Transit Authority. On January 20, 1966 it acquired the Long Island Rail Road Co., a private stock corporation, and thereafter subdivision 6 was added to section 1276 of the Public Authorities Law and provided that, commencing on January 20,  [***3] 1967, the notice requirements of subdivisions 1 and 2 of said section 1276 of the Public Authorities Law and of section 50-e of the General Municipal Law should apply to "a subsidiary corporation of the authority which is a stock corporation". This was an omnibus bill; it contained many amendments and it did not specifically name the Long Island Rail Road Co. (cf. N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 15, 16, 17). Prior to this amendment, a plaintiff had three years in which to commence an action against this stock corporation ( CPLR 214). This short-notice amendment might be unconstitutional if it were intended to deprive the courts of discretion to relieve an injured person from his nonprejudicial mistake. Such discretion is set forth in general terms in CPLR 2004 and it should be construed to apply to subdivisions 1, 2 and 6 of section 1276 of the Public Authorities Law. On the facts of this case, the railroad is estopped from asserting any defense of lack of notice of claim, and such defense is stricken.

 

2036 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Old Dutch Lands, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


55 Misc. 2d 384; 286 N.Y.S.2d 86; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 951



December 29, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendants are granted summary judgment ( CPLR 3212).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a purchaser and former property owners, and defendants, an assignee, a mortgagee, State of New York and City of New York, filed cross-motions for summary judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3212 in plaintiffs' action seeking a declaratory judgment that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D17-25.0 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A transfer tax lienor applied for a reconveyance of properties seized in tax foreclosure proceedings in rem under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D17-25.0. The properties were reconveyed to him after he paid the property taxes owed, and the former owners' equity in the properties was eliminated. The purchaser obtained the choses in action of some of the former owners. The purchaser and the remaining former owners filed a declaratory judgment action against the State, City and the lienor's assignee and the mortgagee. The parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment, and the court granted summary judgment to defendants. The court ruled that the purchaser's claim was precluded by res judicata due to a prior action against defendants. The court held that the enactment of § D17-25.0 gave adequate notice of the former owners' right to present a claim for purposes of due process. The court ruled that the former owners were not denied equal protection because § D17-25.0 gave them a first right to apply for a reconveyance. The court also ruled that § D17-25.0 did not constitute a gift of municipal property in violation of N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the assignee, the mortgagee, the State and the City in plaintiffs' declaratory judgment action.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax liens, tax sales and tax titles -- in rem tax lien foreclosures valid -- provisions for granting reconveyance to former owner or lienor valid -- party defendant in previous action is precluded from instituting present action -- conveyance to former lienor wipes out equity of former owners -- in interests of constitutionality and of fairness to former owners, court suggests that Administrative Code of City of New York (§ D17-25.0) be amended.

1. In rem tax lien foreclosure proceedings (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 17, tit. D) vest valid title in the city. The statute's provisions have been held constitutional ( Nelson v. City of New York, 352 U.S. 103, followed).

2. An additional section has been enacted (Administrative Code of City of New York, § D17-25.0) whereby a foreclosed-upon owner or mortgagee or other lienor may, upon his paying all taxes owed to the city and other costs, be granted a reconveyance of the property by the city. This additional, special section validly supersedes the general statutory requirements that sales of city-owned realty be at auction only [***2] (New York City Charter, § 384; General City Law, § 23). Nor is such a reconveyance a "gift" (N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 1).

3. Under said section D17-25.0, a conveyance by the city to a former lienor wipes out the equity of the former owner ( Astoria Holding Corp. v. Old Dutch Lands, 20 N Y 2d 905, followed). Said decision, and the public policy against vexatious litigation, preclude said Old Dutch Lands from instituting the present action for a judgment declaring said section D17-25.0 unconstitutional.

4. Under said section D17-25.0 of the Administrative Code, a reconveyance to the former owner reinstates the former liens; whereas, for the same price, a reconveyance to a former lienor wipes out entirely the equity of the former owner, no matter how substantial that equity was. In the interests of fairness and of constitutionality, the court suggests that the section be amended: (a) the former owner should be notified when a lienor applies for a reconveyance; (b) the four-month limitation and the two-month limitation on applications for reconveyances should be enlarged; and (c) the unjustifiable discrimination in favor of former lienors and against former owners of the [***3] property should be obviated.

Thomson Industries, Inc. v. Port Washington North


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


55 Misc. 2d 625; 286 N.Y.S.2d 187; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 957



December 28, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporate property owner filed an action for a declaratory judgment and ancillary injunctive relief against defendant village, which prohibited the corporation from using a portion of its property as a helicopter landing area under a village ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The corporation notified the Federal Aviation Agency (FAA) that it was establishing a heliport, which was accepted by the FAA. The corporation discontinued helicopter flights to and from its plant following communications from the village that directed it to do so on the ground that such operations were in violation of the village's building zone ordinance. The corporation brought a complaint for a declaratory judgment. The court dismissed the corporation's complaint. The court held that a place set aside for the landing and take-off of a helicopter was a heliport. The court found that the portion of the corporation's property was subject to the village ordinance. The court determined that the FAA only indicated its approval from an airspace standpoint, and it did not grant permission to establish a limited private helicopter landing area, therefore, the ordinance was not an unlawful attempt to legislate upon a matter pre-empted by the federal government. The court held that it could not be said that the village's determination to exclude heliports from the industrial district of the village did not promote the health, safety, morals, or the general welfare of the community.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the corporation's complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- village ordinance prohibiting "Heliport" in industrial zone validly prohibits factory from using part of its parking area for landing and take-off of private helicopter.

In the center of a small village is a 40-acre industrially zoned plot, which is surrounded by many residences and by a school, a shopping center and a theatre. The zoning ordinance forbids any "Heliport" in the industrial zone. In that industrial zone are plaintiff's factory, which covers 2 acres, and plaintiff's black-topped parking area, which covers 2 acres, and plaintiff's vacant parcel of 11 acres, and beyond plaintiff's property but within 1,100 feet of plaintiff's proposed heliport landing site are several fuel storage tanks. Plaintiff wishes to use part of its paved parking area for the landing and take-off of its helicopter for the transportation about 10 times a month of important personnel, customers and distributors. The Federal Aviation Agency has stated to plaintiff that such a "private use heliport" would be acceptable "from an airspace standpoint" but that plaintiff must apply to State and/or local authorities "for license or approval * * * including request [***2] for local rezoning if needed." The village has properly refused to grant permission. The Federal Aviation Agency publishes a "Heliport Design Guide" which defines a "heliport" as including an "off-heliport landing site". What plaintiff proposes is prohibited by the village zoning ordinance, and is not authorized or licensed by the FAA and is not exclusively regulated by Federal law. Moreover, less than a mile from plaintiff's plant and readily accessible by good roads is a seaplane base which is suitable for helicopter landings and take-offs. The village is granted judgment declaring that its zoning ordinance validly and constitutionally prohibits plaintiff's proposed helicopter site.

Yoli v. Yoli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


55 Misc. 2d 416; 285 N.Y.S.2d 470; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1021



December 6, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for separation prior to September 1, 1967, plaintiff wife moved for leave to add a prayer for judgment of absolute divorce pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(1).


OVERVIEW: All of the acts of alleged mistreatment took place prior to September 1, 1967. The proposed amendment would have rested upon the same factual allegations supporting one of the causes of action for separation, which was the cruel and inhuman treatment of the wife by the husband. The question before the court was whether the benefits conferred by the amended divorce laws were retroactive in effect. The court answered the question affirmatively and granted the wife's motion to add a prayer for absolute divorce. The court found that refusal to accord the wife the same rights as a plaintiff who complained of similar mistreatment after September 1, 1967, would be a denial of equal protection of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court granted the wife's motion. The court struck the complaint and answer and ordered a new complaint to be filed in 120 days and an answer to be served 20 days after service of the complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and wife -- matrimonial actions -- amendments to Domestic Relations Law, which made cruel and inhuman treatment basis for action for divorce, are given retroactive application -- in action, commenced prior to September 1, 1967 when amendments became effective, for separation based on cruel and inhuman treatment occurring before that date, prayer for relief is amended to include judgment for absolute divorce and plaintiff is directed to comply with conciliation procedures of statute.

1. The amendments to the Domestic Relations Law, which made cruel and inhuman treatment a basis for an action for divorce, are deemed remedial legislation and are given retroactive application to acts of cruelty occurring prior to September 1, 1967 when the amended law became effective. In the amendments there is no area which violates due process or a vested or contractual right in violation of the Constitution. To deny retroactive application would result in a denial of equal protection of the laws.

2. Accordingly, in an action commenced prior to September 1, 1967 for a separation based on cruel and inhuman treatment, plaintiff's motion to amend the complaint to add a prayer for judgment [***2] of absolute divorce based on the same factual allegations is granted and the summons is deemed amended accordingly. The complaint and answer are stricken and plaintiff is directed to comply with the requirements for conciliation pursuant to article 11-B. A new complaint shall be served 120 days after the date of entry of an order hereon or upon completion of conciliation procedures, whichever is earlier. The order for alimony and counsel fees previously granted is not disturbed.

In re Elwell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Dutchess County


55 Misc. 2d 252; 284 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1042



November 29, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action of four proceedings under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act art. 3 and four companion proceedings charging respondent parents with violating N.Y. Educ. Law art. 65, pt. 1, petitioner school attendance supervisor alleged that the parents did not adequately supply their children with education and neglected them.


OVERVIEW: The attendance supervisor claimed that the children endangered the health and safety of other minors because their parents refused to have them immunized against poliomyelitis as required by N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2164. The parents argued that the exception to § 2164 applied to them because they were bona fide members of a recognized religious organization whose teachings were contrary to the practice required in § 2164. They also argued that § 2164 violated N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11, U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. The court held that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2164 was a reasonable and proper exercise of the police power of the State, for the protection of the public health, within the ambit of constitutionally permissible legislative authority. The court further held that the record did not indicate that the parents' objections to immunization were based upon the tenets of a religious organization. Rather, the court believed that the parents were members of the Methodist Church, a recognized religious organization, whose teachings were not contrary to such immunization. Yet, the court dismissed the four N.Y. Educ. Law art. 65, pt. 1 proceedings for lack of jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court adjudicated the children to be neglected. However, the court dismissed the four companion proceedings charging the parents with violating N.Y. Educ. Law art. 65, pt. 1.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- immunization against poliomyelitis -- Public Health Law ( § 2164) requiring immunization of children against polio as condition to attendance in school is constitutional -- parents of four children of school age who have refused to have them immunized are not entitled to rely on the exemption in the statute since the church of which they are members does not disapprove of such immunization; children are adjudged to be neglected children -- Family Court lacks jurisdiction of proceedings against parents of children charging them with violation of Education Law since such proceedings are criminal in nature.

1. Section 2164 of the Public Health Law which provides that no person in charge of a school shall permit any child to attend who does not have a certificate or other evidence of the child's immunization against poliomyelitis and which further provides that it does not apply to children whose parents are members of a religious organization whose teachings are contrary to the practice of immunization is a valid and constitutional exercise of the police power for the protection of the public health.

2. The parents of four children of school age have refused [***2] to have them immunized against poliomyelitis on the ground that it is against their religious beliefs. The court finds that they are members of the Methodist Church, a recognized religious organization, whose teachings are not contrary to immunization and that their objections are based on their personal beliefs. They do not qualify for the exemption contained in the section. Accordingly, each of the four children is adjudged to be a neglected child within article 3 of the Family Court Act.

3. The Family Court lacks jurisdiction of proceedings against the parents charging them with a violation of part 1 of article 65 of the Education Law. The Constitution does not give the court jurisdiction of such proceedings which are penal in nature because section 3233 of the Education Law provides penalties of imprisonment for violations thereof. The proceedings are dismissed.

New York State Employees Council 50, etc. v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


55 Misc. 2d 250; 284 N.Y.S.2d 803; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1062; 57 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,828

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner union filed an application for a preliminary injunction and temporary restraining order, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6301. The union sought to restrain respondents, state officials and the governor, from entering into or conducting negotiations for a collective agreement and from signing the agreement.

Thereafter, the union sought to restrain the city officials and the governor from entering into or conducting negotiations for a collective bargaining agreement, until the Board made a final determination with respect to their petition for certification. In support of its restraining order, the union contended that the governor did not follow the intent of the Taylor Law when he publicly recognized the Civil Service Employees Association as the representative of all the state employees. The court denied the union's petition for a stay. According to the court, to grant a stay of the executive process would be an abortive attempt to disrupt the orderly process as designed by legislative process.

 [***1] State -- Public Employment Relations Board -- court will not issue preliminary injunction against Governor's certification of representative for State employees.

The Governor of the State of New York has certified the Civil Service Employees Association, "on the basis of dues deduction", as the representative of practically all the employees of the State for the purpose of negotiations before the Public Employment Relations Board ( Civil Service Law, § 207, subd. 2). An AFL-CIO union has filed objections with said board against such all-inclusive certification, as having been made without sufficient evidence and without an election. Such administrative proceeding before the board is pending. Said union has meantime commenced a judicial proceeding in the nature of prohibition against the Governor and a State negotiating committee which the Governor has appointed. At this stage, the court will not issue a preliminary injunction against the Governor and the committee.

People v. Big Apple Supermarket, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


55 Misc. 2d 139; 284 N.Y.S.2d 970; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1098



November 13, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendants' motions addressed to the sufficiency of the informations herein, as well as their motions to dismiss upon the merits are in all respects denied, with exceptions in each instance.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated trial, defendants, two supermarkets, filed motions to dismiss the informations against them for selling shortweighted butter in violation of N.Y. Agric. Mkts. § 189. Defendant two was also charged with not having a scale available to customers in violation of N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. § 193(5).


OVERVIEW: Inspectors checked over 35,000 pounds of butter and found more than 10 percent of them to be short weight. The State argued that a seller of foodstuffs was liable when a short-weight item is exposed and offered for sale to a consumer. The State urged that a lack of knowledge or intent on the part of the seller would not enable him to avoid conviction for violation of this nature. Defendants argued that N.Y. Penal Law § 189 required the State to plead and prove intent beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that the legislature did not intend and would not have intended to make § 189 a statutory enclave in the Agriculture and Markets Law by constituting it the only section, which required intent to be pleaded and proven. The court further held that whether or not knowledge was to be imputed to defendants beyond a reasonable doubt was for the trial court to determine based upon the testimony and exhibits adduced during trial. The court found that such scienter was abundantly established by the State in this case. The court noted that the matter of falsity of weights was brought to defendants' attention and that they were given a reasonable opportunity to correct the problem.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motions and fined them each $ 200. The court also fined defendant two $ 100.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- short-weight sale of food -- retail supermarkets were fined $ 200 for offering for sale one-pound packages of butter which weighed only about 15 3/4 ounces (Agriculture and Markets Law, § 189) -- intent and knowledge may be shown by circumstances; immaterial that supermarkets had purchased one-pound packages from wholesalers -- $ 100 fine imposed for not keeping scale "easily available to customers" (Agriculture and Markets Law, § 193, subd. 5).

1. The Agriculture and Markets Law ( § 41) makes it a misdemeanor to violate its provisions, and declares that its provisions "should receive a liberal interpretation" (§ 3), and that "intent * * * is immaterial in any prosecution" (§ 43). Section 189 is entitled "False labels", and prohibits any false indication of weight or measure "with intent to defraud", and any sale or offering for sale of an article "which to his knowledge" is thus falsely labeled. These provisions were originally in the Penal Law ( § 435, subds.  [***2] 1, 2); but, when they were transposed into the Agriculture and Markets Law ( § 189) they were intended to be interpreted by the standards of the latter law. Accordingly, "intent" and "knowledge" under section 189 can be proved by the circumstances, as under any other penal section of the Agriculture and Markets Law.

2. Two retail supermarket corporations are found by the court to be guilty beyond a reasonable doubt (Agriculture and Markets Law, § 189) of offering for sale 343 packages of butter, each marked as weighing one pound, and which may have weighed one pound when wrapped and marked at the packer's plant or when stored in the warehouse of the wholesaler, but which, perhaps because of moisture shrinkage, weighed only about 15 3/4 ounces when sold or offered for sale to the ultimate consumer. Each retail corporation is fined $ 200.

3. The Agriculture and Markets Law ( § 193, subd. 5) validly and constitutionally requires that "Wherever food or food products are packaged or wrapped for sale by a retailer * * * an accurate computing scale shall be maintained so placed as to be easily available to customers." Such a scale must front to the public. Furthermore, in a supermarket,  [***3] a scale which is 40 feet away from the dairy department is not "easily available to customers" in the dairy department. For this violation, the court fines one supermarket $ 100.

 

2042 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Turner v. Deegan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


55 Misc. 2d 261; 285 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1100



November 13, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The writ is dismissed and the relator is remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the validity of every step in the procedure and proceeding under which he was presently held after he absconded three days after his parole and was declared delinquent.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner absconded, he was arrested and returned to New York. The court dismissed the writ and remanded petitioner. The court held that it was assumed petitioner was arrested under a parole violation under N.Y. Correct. Law § 216 and the State contended that the proceedings were correct and proper. The court concluded that petitioner as a paroled prisoner was not regarded as a "free man" nor was he a prisoner. Accordingly, a parole violation was not a "criminal prosecution" to which the Sixth Amendment applied. Therefore, the returning of petitioner without extradition or without waiver thereof was proper because there was compliance with N.Y. Correct. Law §§ 216-218. The court concluded there was no proof that there was noncompliance with the pertinent provisions of §§ 216-218.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writ and remanded the petitioner.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- violation -- New York parolee absconded and was arrested in Mississippi and was returned to New York; Correction Law ( §§ 216-218) was complied with; writ of habeas corpus dismissed.

A parolee absconded from New York State. Two years later, he was arrested in Mississippi and was arraigned before a United States Commissioner; and subsequently he was returned to New York, where our Parole Board ordered him incarcerated. It appears that the provisions of our Correction Law ( §§ 216-218) were complied with. Those provisions are constitutional. A parole violation is not a criminal prosecution to which the Sixth Amendment applies. Writ of habeas corpus is dismissed.

People v. Williams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


55 Misc. 2d 774; 286 N.Y.S.2d 575; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1111



November 9, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] All 41 cases are dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Forty-one defendants were charged with loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were alleged to be engaged or about to engage in prostitution. For some defendants it was also alleged that upon inquiry by a police officer failed to give a reasonably credible account of their conduct and purposes. The informations were based upon observations of police officers in which they stated that they saw defendants, known or suspected prostitutes speak to two or three men while walking or standing on a public street. The officers did not overhear the conversations and did not know who the men were but they arrested defendants upon the ground that they were engaged or about to engage in prostitution. The court held that the § 240.35 was also suspect on the ground that it was not sufficiently explicit to inform those who were subject to it what conduct on their part would render them liable to its penalties. The court found that it was the judgment of the officer and not the activity of a defendant, which constituted the crime. The court also found that the provision of § 240.35, which required a defendant to explain herself was unconstitutional, because an accused may remain silent and no penal conclusions flowed from that silence.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed all 41 cases.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- arrests of defendants, women suspected of being about to engage in prostitution, which is "violation", were improper under Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6) which defines "loitering" as justifying suspicion that person is about to engage in "crime".

Informations charging defendants, all alleged to be prostitutes, with loitering under subdivision 6 of section 240.35 of the Penal Law are dismissed. Such subdivision provides for arrests where the loitering is suspected to be preparatory to engaging in "crime". Under section 230.00 of the Penal Law prostitution is a "violation". Since the loitering statute does not permit arrests for "violations", defendants were improperly arrested.

Claudio v. Greenport


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


55 Misc. 2d 371; 284 N.Y.S.2d 965; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1155



October 24, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner and third-party defendant realty company filed a motion for summary judgment in the owner's action to determine a claim allegedly made by defendant village adverse to his title to certain land under the water of a bay. The village was also a third-party plaintiff. Other third-party defendants joined in the motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The owner placed barricades up on the wharf at issue, and the village forced the owner to remove them. The village contended that the granting of state land to the owner's predecessor via 1954 N.Y. Laws 335 was unconstitutional, such that the owner had not entitled meant to the land at issue. The court disagreed. The court found that although it was questionable that the village had standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute, because it admitted to not having a right to the land but rather was defending the publics right to use said land, the court would determine the issue because the owner agreed that the issue should be resolved. The court determined that although the Commissioners of the Land Office were restricted by the Public Lands Law to grant state land only to adjacent proprietors, it was lawful for the State to make the grant to others than the adjacent proprietors for the public good. Unless positive proof to the contrary was offered, the court as bound by the strong presumption that a statute was validly enacted. Chapter 335 did not restrict the state's power to grant land, only the Commissioners, who were not responsible for the original grant of land.


OUTCOME: The court held that the transfer of title from the State as valid and a judgment was awarded against the village.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Waters and watercourses -- grant of land under water -- special act and grant of land under water to wharf corporation which does not own upland, valid and constitutional -- if necessary, court will take notice that act which bears heading "Passed, three-fifths being present", was actually passed by more than two thirds -- accordingly village has no title or claim to said property.

1. The State of New York owned the land under the waters of Greenport Harbor, adjoining the Village of Greenport, in Suffolk County, which harbor is part of Peconic Bay. By a special act (L. 1954, ch. 335), the State validly and constitutionally authorized the Commissioners of the Land Office to convey -- and they did convey -- to the Greenport Wharf Company -- even though said grantee did not own the upland (cf. Public Lands Law, art. 6) -- the title to the land under the waters of said Greenport Harbor upon which the predecessors of said corporation, pursuant to earlier special legislative acts, had for many years built and maintained a dock or wharf. Pursuant to said earlier enactments, this special act provided that "Such conveyance shall be subject to any public or private rights to a landing [***2] on the easterly portion of the premises to be conveyed hereunder and subject to the right of ingress and egress thereto." This is the only extent to which the village has any rights in said property. There is no evidence that the village has ever acquired any prescriptive rights in said property.

2. If one of said earlier acts (L. 1926, ch. 45) was essential to the validity of the corporation's present title, and if said act required a two-thirds vote (cf. N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 17, 20, 23), the court will take notice that, though said act as published bears a headnote which recites "Passed, three-fifths being present", actually it was passed by a vote of 138 to 0 in the Assembly, and by 40 out of a total membership of 51 in the Senate, which is far more than two thirds.

3. Accordingly, summary judgment is granted to the successors in interest of said Greenport Wharf Company, determining that the village has no claim to said property.
