In re Telephone Communications


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


55 Misc. 2d 163; 284 N.Y.S.2d 431; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1170



October 19, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: By personal affirmation of petitioner district attorney, supported by an affidavit of a detective of the special investigating unit of the narcotics bureau containing details, the district attorney applied for an order authorizing the interception of telephone communications over a specified telephone for a period of 20 days.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of the United States held that N.Y. Code Crim. P. § 813-a governing eavesdropping and interception of telephone communications was unconstitutional on its face. The question posed by the application for a wiretap order was whether, absent any wiretapping and eavesdropping statute, the court could nevertheless grant a wiretap order. Despite the holdings, at least so far as eavesdropping by trespass was concerned N.Y. Const. art. I, § 12 remained in full force and effect and could serve as a basis for a constitutionally valid wiretap order if the guidelines set down in previous caselaw were contained in the papers upon which it was granted.The court found that the affirmation and the affidavit submitted in support of the proposed wiretap order fully complied with the requirements.


OUTCOME: The court signed the order submitted by the district attorney authorizing the placing of a wiretap on and the interception of telephone communications over the telephone specified.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- wiretap orders -- section 12 of article I of Constitution providing for wiretap orders is self-executing and is in full force and effect despite invalidation of implementing statute (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-a) by Berger v. New York (388 U.S. 41) -- affidavit in support of wiretap order meets guide lines of Berger case and order is signed.

1. Despite the invalidation of section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure by Berger v. New York (388 U.S. 41) on constitutional grounds, section 12 of article I of the New York State Constitution which provides for ex parte orders for the interception of telephone communications upon oath or affirmation that there is reasonable ground to believe that evidence of crime may be obtained, and identifying the telephone and describing the person whose communications are to be intercepted, is self-executing. Even if all the requirements needed for a constitutionally valid wiretap order are not set forth therein, reliance can be placed on the presumption that the courts in carrying out State constitutional mandates, will do so with full awareness and recognition of all Federal due process requirements. Such constitutional [***2] provision remains in full force and may serve as a basis for a valid wiretap order if the guide lines set forth in Berger v. New York (supra) are contained in the papers on which it is granted.

2. An affidavit submitted in support of a proposed wiretap order meets these guide lines and the order is signed.

 

2046 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


300 West 154th Street Realty Co. v. Department of Bldgs.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


55 Misc. 2d 37; 284 N.Y.S.2d 203; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1186



October 16, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord sought to have the New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 564-15.0 et seq., and a resolution of respondent city's Board of Health under the city's Emergency Repair Program, declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: One of the landlord's tenants discovered a stoppage in her toilet that caused it to overflow. The landlord refused to repair it, so the tenant reported it to the city. The toilet was repaired by a contractor hired by the city, which sought reimbursement from the landlord. The landlord claimed that the emergency repair program and the provisions of the administrative code upon which it was based worked a denial of equal protection of the laws, an unconstitutional impairment of his contractual rights, and a deprivation of property without due process. The resolution designated as public nuisances those dwellings lacking certain services such as running water, effective sewage disposal facilities, electricity, or heat. It ordered the immediate abatement of such nuisances by owners, and in the event they failed to do so, authorized the city to take the steps required to secure such abatement. The court dismissed the landlord's petition, holding that the statutes challenged were reasonably directed toward the correction of the problem of substandard housing and should not be set aside because the means devised to accomplish that result impaired the obligation of the landlord's contract.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landlord's petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- health nuisances in multiple dwellings -- public health nuisance, such as stopped toilet in apartment in multiple dwelling, may be repaired by contractor hired by city, on summary notice to landlord and without any judicial proceeding; city may then have lien on rents and may require landlord to repay city's expenses (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 564-15.0 et seq.).

1. A tenant in a multiple dwelling in New York City discovered a stoppage in her toilet which caused it to overflow. She notified the landlord. The landlord denies this, and asserts that the tenant herself willfully or negligently caused the stoppage and that in such case the lease does not require the landlord to repair the condition. In any event, after four days the tenant reported the condition to the New York City Department of Buildings. On that day, the department inspected the toilet and notified the landlord and the city's Department of Health; and the latter at once certified that this condition constituted a nuisance. On the next day, the Buildings Department hired a contractor, who repaired the toilet, for $ 58.10. Subsequently,  [***2] the Buildings Department lawfully obtained reimbursement from the landlord. Otherwise the city could have validly and constitutionally filed a lien on the building and all its rents (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 564-15.0 et seq.) and declared the nuisance to be "a rent impairing violation" (Multiple Dwelling Law, § 302-a) and accordingly could have notified all the tenants to pay their rents meanwhile to the city.

2. A municipality may abate a public health nuisance summarily and without any judicial proceeding, and may then properly require reimbursement from the property owner. Pursuant to the Administrative Code of the City of New York (§ 564-15.0 et seq.), the city's Board of Health had passed a resolution providing for summary notice to a landlord when a nuisance presents an immediate danger to the life or health of the occupants of a building. The Department of Buildings' manual of procedure for such emergency repairs sufficiently provides for such summary notice, and this landlord was properly given such notice. These provisions are valid and constitutional.

 

2047 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bartlett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


56 Misc. 2d 185; 287 N.Y.S.2d 850; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1222



September 29, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] All motions as to which decision was reserved upon the trial are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with speeding while operating a private ambulance over a road under the jurisdiction of a New York park commission.


OVERVIEW: Defendant testified that at the time the violation allegedly occurred, he was responding to an emergency call; specifically to transport a patient to a local hospital. At the close of the case defendant moved to dismiss the information upon the ground that his vehicle was exempt from the operation of the ordinance by reason of the provisions of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1104(b)(3). The statute permitted the driver of an authorized emergency vehicle to exceed maximum speed limits, under certain conditions, so long as he did not endanger life or property. The People asserted that defendant however was required to operate his vehicle in conformity with a further section of the park commission ordinance, which required all vehicles on park commission land, including emergency vehicles to obey the speed limit. Emergency vehicles could use the parkways, as an exception to the class of vehicles to which the highways were generally restricted, but the vehicles had to nonetheless obey the maximum speed limits fixed for all users of the highway. The court found defendant guilty.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt of speeding while operating a private ambulance over a parkway.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- speeding -- emergency vehicles -- ambulance cannot speed on Long Island State Park Commission's highways, even for emergency.

State park commissions and parkway authorities may regulate traffic and establish speed limits for their highways (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1630); and such regulations validly and constitutionally supersede general provisions permitting emergency vehicles to exceed speed limits (cf. Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1104). An ordinance adopted by the Long Island State Park Commission permits licensed ambulances, when on emergency service, to use the parkways "but subject to all the provisions of these ordinances regulating traffic including limitations of the rate of speed". Accordingly, the driver of a private ambulance was found guilty of speeding on a Long Island State Park Commission highway, though he was responding to an emergency call.

People v. Schmidt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Erie County


54 Misc. 2d 702; 283 N.Y.S.2d 290; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1229



September 28, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a trial court (New York) of violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 381(6), which required the use of a protective helmet while operating or riding a motorcycle. Defendant appealed, challenging the constitutionality of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the helmet requirement, claiming that it was vague and uncertain and that in regulating individual conduct, it was outside the scope of the State's police power. The argument that the subdivision was vague and uncertain was directed at the mandate that the helmet be approved by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles. The court held that the use and nature of protective helmets in industry, in athletics and in the military services was known generally to the public. For motorcycle riders as a class the Commissioner approved the types of helmets which lawfully could be sold by commercial dealers. The section was not so vague and uncertain as to render it unconstitutional. The court also found that the danger of flying stones or other objects from the wheels of moving vehicles was a real one and a blow on the head of a cyclist not only could endanger himself, but be the cause of injury or death to other users of the public highways. To prevent such possible occurrences was a valid objective for legislative action under the general police power of the State.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- wearing helmet while operating motorcycle -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 381, subd. 6) requiring motorcyclists to wear reflectorized, protective helmet is constitutional -- requirement is within police power and does not regulate individual conduct.

1. Subdivision 6 of section 381 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law rendering it unlawful to operate a motorcycle unless the operator wears a reflectorized protective helmet is not unconstitutional as vague and uncertain.

2. Requiring the wearing of such helmet to protect the cyclist and other users of the public highways is within the police power of the State and is not an unconstitutional regulation of individual conduct.

Lampert v. Berman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


55 Misc. 2d 99; 284 N.Y.S.2d 657; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1237



September 22, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord filed an action for a writ of mandamus against respondent, the New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Administrator, to compel the Administrator to schedule a public hearing to consider an order abolishing rent and eviction controls within the City of New York and to enter an order of decontrol pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin Code § Y51-12.0.


OVERVIEW: New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y51-12.0 required the Administrator to enter an order of decontrol after a public hearing whenever studies and investigations established that there were vacancies in a class of housing accommodations of five percent or more. The United States Bureau of the Census disclosed that there was more than a five percent vacancy in residential housing in the City of the New York. The landlord requested a hearing by the Administrator for an order of decontrol. The Administrator refused the landlord's request on the ground that the central factor for consideration was the net vacancy rate, which was indisputably less than five percent. The landlord filed a petition for a writ of mandamus against the Administrator to conduct a public hearing and to enter an order of decontrol. The court dismissed the petition on the ground that the New York City Council had previously determined that the criteria for application of § Y51-12.0 was based upon the net vacancy rate and that the landlord was not entitled to a writ of mandamus based upon his disagreement with the Administrator's finding and interpretation of the appropriate vacancy rate factor.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landlord's petition to compel the Administrator to schedule a public hearing to consider abolition of rent and eviction controls within New York City and to enter an order of decontrol.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- New York City Council in 1967 having extended duration of housing rent controls, upon finding of 3.19% net vacancy rate in city, landlord's petition for order of mandamus to require City Rent Administrator to hold public hearing and to decontrol rents, dismissed.

The New York City Council having passed a resolution in 1967 extending the duration of the city's local emergency housing rent control law, after public hearings at which the City Council (a) found that the percentage of housing vacancies in the city is 3.19% and (b) considered and rejected the contention that this was a net figure from which certain types of vacancies had been excluded and that the true gross figure is more than 5%, no justiciable issue remains upon which a court may (a) direct the City Rent and Rehabilitation Administrator to hold public hearings on the true vacancy rate and (b) direct him to order the decontrol of rents and evictions in the city (Local Laws, 1967, No. 60 of City of New York). This application by a landlord for such an order in the nature of mandamus is dismissed.

People v. Colozzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


54 Misc. 2d 687; 283 N.Y.S.2d 409; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1254; 56 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,750



September 18, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, 10 individuals, a union, and 34 employers, demurred the indictments against them, contending that the facts therein did not state a crime.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 29, which criminalized the performance of any act prohibited by statute. The State alleged that defendants had violated 1953 N.Y. Laws ch. 882, § 8, as amended, which prohibited a union from having a convicted felon as its agent or employee. That section, however, did not contain a criminal penalty. Defendants filed demurrers to the indictments, which the court granted. The court held that the legislature did not intend to criminalize defendants' conduct.


OUTCOME: The demurrers were granted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- dues checkoff -- Waterfront Commission Act (L. 1953, ch. 882, as amd., § 8) forbids collection of union dues if agent or employee of union is felon; section and act do not make violation thereof criminal; indictment charges defendants, employers and unions, with violation because employee of union was felon; section 29 of Penal Law, which makes misdemeanor out of prohibited act for which no penalty is provided, is vague and unconstitutional -- even if constitutional, application of section 8 to defendants without knowledge of union employee's prior convictions, violates due process -- even assuming constitutionality of both sections, section 29 of Penal Law is inapplicable to section 8.

1. Section 8 of the Waterfront Commission Act (L. 1953, ch. 882, as amd.) provides that no person shall collect or receive dues for any labor union if any officer, agent or employee has been convicted of a felony. The section and the act do not make violation of such provision criminal. The indictment charges that one defendant had been convicted of felonies and was employed as an agent for a labor union to collect dues and that all defendants, employers of union help, an international [***2] union, two locals thereof and their officers, violated section 8. Section 29 of the former Penal Law, which is applicable because the violation occurred before September 1, 1967, and which makes a misdemeanor out of the performance of an act prohibited by statute for which no penalty is provided, is vague, does not inform the public as to which statutes it applies, lacks standards and lacks due process of law in violation of the Federal and State Constitutions.

2. Even assuming section 29 to be constitutional and applicable to section 8, the application of section 8 to these defendants, checking off dues because they are required to do so by their labor contracts, without knowledge of the union employee's prior convictions, is repugnant to the Due Process Clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions.

3. Even assuming the constitutionality of section 8 and section 29, section 8 is not one of the statutes to which section 29 of the Penal Law is applicable. Section 29 was intended to apply to the Penal Law and not to any other statutes.

People v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Genesee County


54 Misc. 2d 902; 283 N.Y.S.2d 594; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1257



September 18, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding before the court, defendants moved to inspect the grand jury minutes or in the alternative, to dismiss their indictments for the crime of attempted robbery in the first degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 2120, 2124 and 2. The second defendant also moved to sever his trial from that of the first defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with attempted robbery. Defendants moved to inspect the grand jury minutes, contending that the 1967 Grand Jury was illegally constituted in that it was not selected and impaneled pursuant to the procedural requirements of N.Y. Jud. Ct. Law art. 18; and that certain provisions pertaining to a $ 250 property requirement were unconstitutional. The court found that the granting of a motion to inspect the grand jury minutes was a matter of discretion that was not to be exercised arbitrarily nor without good reason shown by the moving party based on fact and not mere assumption. The court noted that defendants had failed to show facts from which there was reason to believe that the evidence before the grand jury was insufficient or illegal. The court, in considering the matter before it and after reviewing the grand jury minutes, was of the opinion that the indictment was founded on legal and sufficient evidence as to both defendants. The court also held that, in the sound exercise of its discretion and in fairness to the second defendant, his motion to sever should have been granted and separate trials ordered.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for inspection of the grand jury minutes and granted the second defendant's motion to sever.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Grand jury -- impanelling -- indictments handed up by traditional type of Grand Jury are valid, despite procedural irregularities in impanelling of Grand Jury -- requirement that jurors own $ 250 worth of property constitutional -- defendants' motion to inspect Grand Jury minutes denied -- but defendant who did not confess may, in court's discretion, have separate trial.

1. By a resolution of the Genesee County Board of Supervisors and an order of the Appellate Division, Fourth Department, appointing a Genesee County Jury Board, the county elected in 1966 to be governed by article 18 of the Judiciary Law. However, there is no record that said Jury Board ever met or made any appointments. The County Clerk acted as the Commissioner of Jurors and selected trial jurors and grand jurors. Existing jury lists were used, plus names of additional persons who were examined and found to be qualified, and a Grand Jury was drawn from the master list of some 8,000 jurors without first drawing 300 names. Despite these procedural irregularities, the Grand Jury conformed to the traditional concept of a Grand Jury and consisted of legally qualified persons who represented a fair cross section [***2] of the impartial citizens of the county. Motion of indicted defendants to dismiss the indictment, on the ground that the Grand Jury was not legally constituted, is denied.

2. The requirement that a juror own at least $ 250 worth of realty or personalty is valid and constitutional.

3. After reading the Grand Jury minutes, the court denies defendants' motion to inspect the minutes or in the alternative to dismiss the indictment.

4. However, since it appears that only one of the two jointly indicted defendants has made a confession, the court in its discretion directs that separate trials be had.

People v. Brady


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Suffolk County


54 Misc. 2d 638; 283 N.Y.S.2d 175; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1258



September 15, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] This motion is accordingly denied, and this matter is hereby ordered transferred to the Family Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss an assault in the third degree complaint brought by his wife.


OVERVIEW: Defendant moved for an order dismissing an assault in the third degree complaint brought by his wife contending that the Family Court had exclusive original jurisdiction over an intrafamilial assault case, and that the court was therefore devoid of jurisdiction. The court rejected this contention, finding that the authorities did not intend that an act as defined and set forth in the Penal Law was not to be considered a crime simply because it was perpetrated by one spouse upon the other. The court held that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 813, 814, in their entirety, appeared to be inconsistent with N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 811, 812 of the same article, and further appear to violate the constitutional provision setting up the Family Court. Accordingly, the court held that it had original jurisdiction of all misdemeanors committed any place within the territorial jurisdiction of the court and this fact, taken together with the transferral provisions contained in N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 813 and 814 of the Family Court Act, precluded a dismissal of the complaint except on the grounds of legal insufficiency.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss and transferred the matter to family court.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- jurisdiction as between Family Court and criminal court -- criminal court has original jurisdiction of assault charge between spouses and complaint should not be dismissed; upon taking jurisdiction such court must transfer jurisdiction to Family Court.

The District Court does not lack jurisdiction of an assault in the third degree complaint made by a wife against her husband. Section 811 of the Family Court Act provides that family offense proceedings are civil in nature. Section 812 grants to the Family Court exclusive original jurisdiction over family offense proceedings. Section 813 provides that any criminal complaint charging assault between spouses shall be transferred by the criminal court to the Family Court. Section 814 directs the criminal court, after entertaining an assault charge between spouses, to transfer the proceeding, to hold defendant, admit him to bail or parole defendant for hearing before the Family Court. While sections 813 and 814 appear to be contrary to the acknowledged purpose of section 811, the District Court has jurisdiction [***2] of the assault charge and such jurisdiction precludes dismissal. Upon taking jurisdiction, the charge must be transferred to the Family Court.

People v. Krebs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 578; 282 N.Y.S.2d 996; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1265



September 11, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding before the court, defendant was charged with violations of two ordinances, selling leaflets, magazines, booklets, and newspapers, without a peddler's license and obstructing a sidewalk.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating two ordinances when she sold leaflets, magazines, booklets, and newspapers supporting the Viet Cong and protesting the Vietnam War. The first ordinance alleged a violation for selling the materials without a peddler's license. The second ordinance related to obstruction of the sidewalk. Defendant contended that the material she sold were manifestations of communication of thoughts and ideas implicit in the protections afforded to free speech. The court agreed with this contention. On the second charge, the court held that based on all the evidence, and recognizing the unusually wide width of the sidewalk, the mid-afternoon time of the alleged offense, the conflicting testimony as to the density of the pedestrian traffic, and the mighty strength of defendant's constitutional right to speak out her views, defendant was not guilty of obstructing the sidewalk.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaints against defendant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- free speech -- ordinance requiring peddler to obtain license was inapplicable to housewife selling literature and buttons with legends all opposing war in Vietnam; housewife was exercising right of free speech and she is not guilty of violating ordinance -- sidewalk obstruction ordinance not construed so as to infringe on constitutional right of freedom of speech -- while defendant had right to place table on sidewalk for sale of literature and butttons in exercise of free speech, proof establishes that her conduct did not go beyond exercise of right of free speech; she is not guilty of violating sidewalk obstruction ordinance.

1. Defendant housewife selling leaflets, magazines, booklets and newspapers, all of them urging support of the Viet Cong and opposing the war in Vietnam, and also selling flags and buttons containing legends supporting the Viet Cong and opposing the Vietnam war, is not guilty of violating a section of the New York City Administrative Code requiring a peddler to obtain a license. Her sale of the articles was for the purpose of communicating her ideas and convictions. The peddler license ordinance was not intended to strike down [***2] the right of free speech.

2. Section 692h-1.0 of the Administrative Code is construed to make unlawful those acts committed on public streets which so substantially impede or delay the convenient and safe passage of pedestrian traffic as to curtail seriously and without any legal justification the use thereof by others but cannot infringe on First Amendment rights. So construed, the section is not unconstitutional.

3. While defendant had a right to place a table on the sidewalk incidental to her distribution, and incidental sale, of literature, buttons and flags, her conduct did not go beyond a clear and proper exercise of her right of freedom of speech. The sidewalk was wide, the time was 3:30 in the afternoon and there was conflicting testimony as to the density of pedestrian traffic. Defendant is not guilty and the complaint is dismissed.

 

2054 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Long Island College Hospital v. Catherwood


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 712; 283 N.Y.S.2d 249; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1275; 66 L.R.R.M. 2360; 56 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,743



September 5, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] It follows, therefore, that petitioner's application to stay arbitration is in all respects denied. Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a nonprofit hospital, filed an application to stay compulsory arbitration between it and respondent union as ordered by respondent New York Industrial Commissioner. The hospital sought to restrain the New York State Board of Mediation, which was designated by the Industrial Commissioner as arbitrator, from proceeding to arbitrate the dispute between the hospital and the union.


OVERVIEW: The hospital argued that it had a right to judicial review of the union's certification as the bargaining representative; and that N.Y. Lab. Law § 716 was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power to the Industrial Commissioner and the New York State Mediation Board. The court disagreed and dismissed the petition. A certification by the Labor Board as to the proper bargaining representative under N.Y. Lab. Law § 705 could not be judicially reviewed. The claim to stay arbitration on that ground was denied. The claim that § 716 was unconstitutional because it improperly delegated power to the State Board of Mediation and the Industrial Commissioner without providing legislative standards to guide them in the exercise of those powers appeared equally without merit. The statute and its amendments were designed to avoid labor disputes in voluntary hospitals by coupling the extension of collective bargaining rights with a prohibition against strikes. The Industrial Commissioner had the experience which justified reliance upon his expertise in determining which remedy was most likely to effect the statutory purpose in each particular case.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition to stay arbitration.


HEADNOTES

Labor unions -- collective bargaining in nonprofit hospitals -- arbitration directed by court will not be stayed on ground hospital employer is entitled to judicial review of certification of union as collective bargaining representative -- Labor Law ( § 716) not unconstitutional.

1. A voluntary, nonprofit hospital's refusal to acknowledge a union's certification as collective bargaining representative for maintenance employees is a flouting of a mandate of the Appellate Division which directed it to begin collective bargaining and to seek judicial review as to the propriety of the certification upon an appeal from a final order in an enforcement proceeding (26 A D 2d 543). Arbitration will not be stayed on the ground that petitioner is entitled first to judicial review of the certification.

2. Section 716 of the Labor Law, which seeks to avoid labor disputes in voluntary, nonprofit hospitals by forbidding strikes and requiring compulsory collective bargaining, fact finding and binding arbitration in its stead, grants discretion to the Industrial Commissioner [***2] to determine which remedy is most suitable and such discretion is not an improper delegation of legislative authority rendering the section unconstitutional.

 

2055 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cohen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


54 Misc. 2d 873; 283 N.Y.S.2d 817; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1279



August 31, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the indictments is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion seeking to dismiss various grand jury indictments, contending that the grand jury selection process violated the Due Process and Equal Protection clauses of the U.S. and New York Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contested the various grand jury indictments against him, contending that members of low income groups, and particularly Negroes, Puerto Ricans, and persons of Italian and Jewish origin were deliberately and systematically excluded from the jury pool. The court upheld the validity of the indictments, however. The court held that defendant has failed to rebut the presumption that the grand jury was properly empanelled and acted with propriety, validating the indictments. The court further held that defendant had failed to demonstrate that he had been prejudiced by the alleged irregularities in selection of grand jurors, or that any of the grand jurors was incompetent and should have been disqualified.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Grand jury -- selection of jurors -- sections 596 and 609 of Judiciary Law providing qualifications of grand jurors and manner of their selection are constitutional -- use of questionnaires to prospective jurors to determine their qualifications is not extension of powers of County Clerk -- no evidence of arbitrary exclusion from panel of grand jurors of any ethnic or racial group -- presumption that Grand Jury was properly impaneled was not rebutted and defendant's indictment was proper.

1. Section 596 and section 609 of the Judiciary Law which provide, respectively, the qualifications to be possessed by grand jurors and the manner in which they are to be selected within the City of New York are constitutional.

2. The use of questionnaires to prospective jurors to determine if they meet the qualifications set forth in section 596 of the Judiciary Law is not an unwarranted extension of the powers of the County Clerk who by section 609 is authorized to make special investigations of grand jurors.

3. There is in the record no evidence of any effort by the County Clerk arbitrarily to exclude persons of a particular classification [***2] from the panel of grand jurors. In the absence of clear proof to the contrary, the County Clerk is presumed to have performed his duty and defendant's rights are presumed not to have been violated. There is no proof that the Grand Jury which indicted defendant was not composed of a cross section of the community. The County Clerk fairly and honestly discharged his duties and did not discriminate against Negroes, Puerto Ricans, and persons of Italian and Jewish origin and extraction. There was no willful and planned discrimination and exclusion in the selection of grand jurors based on race, color, creed or economic standing in the years 1958 through 1962.

4. Defendant has failed to rebut the presumption that the Grand Jury was properly impaneled and acted with propriety and that the indictments are valid. Defendant has not offered any proof as to the financial or ethnic makeup of the panel of jurors which indicted him or how he was prejudiced by their selection as grand jurors.

 

2056 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Millstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach


54 Misc. 2d 493; 283 N.Y.S.2d 353; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1280



August 30, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, conceding the sufficiency of the prosecution's prima facie proof of their violation of the relevant section of the zoning law, relied for acquittal upon the law's asserted unconstitutionality.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with zoning law violations, including over-occupancy of their residential property. While admitting that the prosecution had made out its prima facie case, defendants challenged the laws' constitutionality because they had been rarely enforced in the years since their enactment. In addition, defendants alleged that the laws were not validly enacted. The court upheld the ordinances' constitutionality and validity. The court held that the breakdown in enforcement was not so widespread, so long continued, and so persistently ignored as to convert a constitutional and lawful enactment into one that could not any longer be enforced. There was insufficient evidence that the 1922 ordinance was published in the local paper within seven days of enactment, but there was insufficient evidence that it was not. Because the ordinance had been in force so long, there was a presumption in favor of its legality. The court also upheld the 1930 version, deciding that the variances from the ordinance as published and as enacted were insignificant and did not require striking down the law.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the constitutionality and validity of the local zoning laws.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- change of conditions warranting invalidation of zoning ordinance limiting number of families in dwelling to two need not be of legal character -- no breakdown of enforcement of ordinance shown -- that defendants' property is worth more as three-family house is irrelevant -- evidence as to defendants' deliberate acquisition of property bears on degree of guilt -- 1922 zoning ordinance not invalid because publication may have been eight, rather than seven, days after passage -- 1930 ordinance not invalid because changes made after publication -- defendants are guilty of violating ordinance.

1. To strike down a zoning ordinance limiting to two the number of families in a dwelling a change in conditions subsequent to enactment need not be of a legal character.

2. While disregard of an ordinance may become so widespread as to make the statute a dead letter and enforcement discriminatory as to those selected for prosecution, where 108 dwellings are located within a three-block area in which the subject premises are located, and there were violations in 28 of them, 18 owners having been either convicted or having offered guilty pleas and are now [***2] complying, and the remaining 10 possibly being legal by reason of variances or prior nonconforming use, there has been no breakdown in enforcement so widespread and continued as to convert a constitutional enactment into one which can no longer be enforced. There was no breakdown in enforcement even if the original 28 violators be considered.

3. That defendants' property is worth more as a three-family house is irrelevant and immaterial in determining whether this prosecution is maintainable.

4. Evidence as to defendants' deliberate acquisition of the property with actual or implied knowledge of the restrictions was properly received on the question of the degree of their guilt rather than on the issue as to their criminal responsibility.

5. The zoning ordinance originally enacted in 1922 and sustained in 1938 may not be declared invalid for insubstantial reasons, such as that in 1922 the ordinance may have been published eight rather than seven days after passage, especially since thousands of transactions have been consummated in reliance on the validity of the ordinance. The presumption of nonpublication arising from the absence of the required certificate ( CPLR 4532) has been [***3] overcome. The 1922 zoning ordinance was validly enacted and remained legally in force until replaced by the 1930 ordinance.

6. Although the 1930 ordinance as proposed and published and as adopted differed because of changes made after publication, the changes being limited and having no reference to the maximum permissible occupancy, did not require republication or invalidation. Insignificant error in passage or promulgation does not justify upsetting the thousands of transactions consummated on basis of the 1930 ordinance.

7. Defendants are found guilty of violating the ordinance.

People v. Bielmeyer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


54 Misc. 2d 466; 282 N.Y.S.2d 797; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1282

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding before the court, defendants challenged the charges against them for violating N.Y. Veh. & Traffic Law § 381(6), which made it unlawful for any person to operate or ride upon a motorcycle unless he wears a protective helmet of a type approved by the commissioner.

The court held that under any interpretation of the concept of police power, the law was constitutional. The court found that because defendants had admitted all the factual allegations made against them and because they raised only the constitutional question, all the defendants were found guilty as charged.

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- wearing helmet while operating motorcycle -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 381, subd. 6) requiring motorcyclist to wear helmet is constitutional.

Subdivision 6 of section 381 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which requires any person operating or riding upon a motorcycle to wear a protective helmet, is constitutional as a proper exercise of the police power.

 

2058 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Shilbury v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Sullivan County


54 Misc. 2d 979; 284 N.Y.S.2d 124; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1290



August 25, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen brought an action against defendants, the board of supervisors of the county, the State of New York, and two towns after the board of supervisors offered a proposed local law that eliminated the designation of the county board of supervisors and allowed county legislators, one from each town, to be elected in the general elections beginning in 1967.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. County Law § 411 prohibited county judges, and the district attorney, sheriff, county clerk, or any elective county officer to be eligible to hold at the same time any other elective county or town office, or that of city supervisor. The board of supervisors proposed to disband and allow 15 county legislators to be elected at the general election. The proposal allowed all supervisors of the towns in the county to be eligible for election to the county board of legislators. The proposed local law was to be effective only if it was approved by the affirmative vote of a majority of the qualified electors voting upon the law at the general election of November 7, 1967. The board argued that it could enact the law pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 10 and its power of home rule. The court held that: (1) under the proposed law it was mathematically possible for every member of the legislative body to cast the decisive vote on legislation in substantially the same ratio that the population of his constituency bore to the total population; (2) the board could not change the law under home rule; and (3) the voters were entitled to a referendum.


OUTCOME: The court gave the board of supervisors an extension to enable it to submit one or more constitutionally valid plans of reapportionment. The court adjourned the matter for five days.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- mathematically weighted voting powers of different legislators on County Board of Legislators might meet constitutional requirements -- but referendum on such proposed local law is mandatory -- regardless of referendum, no Town Supervisor can be member of County Board of Legislators.

1. A proposed local law of the existing malapportioned Board of Supervisors of a county, creating a County Board of Legislators which would still consist of only one from each town regardless of the town's population, and which would allow Supervisors to be elected as legislators also, but which would so differently weight the voting power of the different legislators on the board as to give each legislator a decisive vote on proposed legislation in substantially the same ratio which his town's population bears to the county's population, would comply with the constitutional principle that each legislator must represent substantially the same number of electors as any other legislator. But a referendum thereon is mandatory (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 23, subd. 2, pars. f, g; Town Law, § 20, subds. 1, 5).

2. But, regardless of any [***2] local law or referendum, a Town Supervisor cannot be a member of the County Board of Legislators (County Law, § 411; N. Y. Const., art. IX, §§ 2, 3; Municipal Home Rule Law, §§ 2, 10).

 

2059 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dobish v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Wayne County


54 Misc. 2d 367; 282 N.Y.S.2d 791; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1305



August 17, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prior suit, the court held that the apportionment of defendant county board of supervisors was unconstitutional, and it refused to approve a proposed voting plan submitted by the board. Subsequently, the Court of Appeals (New York) considered the propriety of an adjusted weighted voting plan and a fractional-weighted voting plan. On remand, the court heard testimony from an expert on those types of plans.


OVERVIEW: At the hearing, the expert testified that it was possible to determine the number of times when the "yes" or "no" votes of a legislator having a certain number of votes would pass or defeat a measure by a mathematical formula. The expert testified that it was possible to program that function into a computer so that it would produce results in 10 or 15 minutes depending on the number of towns in the county. The court found that the expert's analyses fairly represented adjusted weighted voting plans where it was mathematically possible for every member of the legislative body to cast the decisive vote on legislation in the same ratio which the population of his constituency bore to the total population of the county. The court concluded that either of the plans on which it heard testimony satisfied the "one person, one vote" principle and that both were constitutional. The court then addressed how the county could take action to recognize the voting power of each supervisor. The court held that under home rule the board had to enact a local law that provided for a constitutional voting plan like those considered by the court.


OUTCOME: The county board of supervisors had to promptly enact a local law in conformity with the court's opinion, without the need for a referendum, in order to proceed with a proper voting plan. The county had to adopt the law no later than January 1, 1968.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- Wayne County Board of Supervisors is directed to adopt either of two plans of weighted voting by members of board, which have been analyzed by digital computer and which court finds will satisfy principle of "one person, one vote".

The Wayne County Board of Supervisors is directed to correct the present unconstitutional apportionment of the board by enacting a local law, which shall be effective no later than January 1, 1968, and which will provide for adjusted weighted voting by the members of the board according to either of two plans which have been analyzed for the court by an expert with digital computers. The court finds that, under either of the two plans, each Supervisor may cast a decisive vote in substantially the same ratio as the population of his town bears to the total population of the county, and that therefore either plan will satisfy the principle of "one person, one vote". Since Wayne County has not adopted any "alternative form of county government", no mandatory or permissive referendum is necessary. (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 1, subd. [h], par. [2]; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd.  [***2] 1, par. [ii], subpar. a, cl. [1]; County Law, § 153, subd. 4.)

 

2060 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re James


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 514; 283 N.Y.S.2d 126; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1306



August 17, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner alleged narcotic addict, detained involuntarily pursuant to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 206, sought his release on the grounds that the statute in question was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Alleged narcotic addict was ordered committed, pursuant to a warrant, for an examination and possibly for rehabilitative treatment. He sued, claiming that the statute authorizing his arrest, detention, and involuntary rehabilitation was unconstitutional. The statute provided that the alleged addict could be arrested upon an ex parte application alleging that he was an addict, then subjected to an examination regarding his possible addiction at which he could be obliged to answer a physician's questions, and if found to be an addict ordered to undergo treatment. After that determination, he was granted counsel and could file a challenge to the compulsory treatment order. The court held that this procedure was unconstitutional and it ordered the alleged addict's release. Alleged addict needed to be provided with legal counsel prior to the examination, so as to protect his self-incrimination privilege.


OUTCOME: Alleged addict's application for release on grounds of statutory unconstitutionality was granted. The detention statute was unconstitutional since it failed to provide an alleged addict with counsel prior to a mandatory physical examination at which the alleged addict was required to respond to a physician's questions.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Mental health -- drug addiction -- involuntary civil commitment of drug addict under Mental Hygiene Law ( § 206) violates addict's constitutional rights; he is ordered released.

Section 206 of the Mental Hygiene Law, which deals with narcotic addicts, provides the procedures for the rehabilitation of addicts including an involuntary civil commitment. In the instant case, a warrant for the apprehension of the addict was signed on May 2, 1967, the warrant stating that it was issued upon the petition of the addict's mother. The addict first became aware of the proceeding when he was apprehended by an officer, detained and delivered to a reception center for a medical examination. On May 3, 1967 he was examined by a doctor who took a history, the report of the doctor giving an opinion that the addict should be certified as an addict and indicating that the addict had admitted that he had taken heroin. On May 5, 1967 the addict was brought into court and he then requested a hearing and the assignment of counsel. The procedures of section 206 of the Mental Hygiene Law deprived the addict of his constitutional rights to remain silent and to the advice of counsel. The doctor [***2] by whom the addict was examined was a State doctor and the usual privilege between doctor and patient was ignored. The addict's rights under Miranda v. Arizona (384 U.S. 436) were violated. It is immaterial that the mother initiated the proceeding since the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission then became the prosecutor. His detention by the warrant made him a prisoner and his examination by the doctor without an attorney violated his constitutional rights. It is immaterial that he is charged with a sickness. The penalty is detention for up to three years. The law should be amended to provide that an order or warrant be returnable before the court after service on the addict of all papers so that he could be advised of his constitutional rights.

 

2061 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Abelove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Utica, Traffic Court


54 Misc. 2d 306; 282 N.Y.S.2d 611; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1327



August 3, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Hence, the information filed against him is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was issued a traffic ticket on February 22, 1967, when he parked his car at a space regulated by a parking meter without inserting a coin as required by Utica, NY, Traffic Ordinance § 17-209. Defendant asked the court to dismiss the information filed against him on the grounds that it was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Utica, NY, Traffic Ordinance § 17-209 required payment while the owner of a car parked a vehicle at a meter unless it was a holiday. The ordinance did not designate February 22 as a holiday. In contrast, N.Y. Gen. Constr. Law § 24 designated February 22, or Washington's birthday, as a legal holiday. Defendant contended that the municipality had to post suitable signs before the ordinance could be effective. The prosecution argued that the city had a right to adopt the ordinance under N.Y. Const. art. §§ 1 and 2. The court held that: (1) the city had a right to select the holidays on which the parking meters were to be in operation; (2) the failure of the city to designate on the parking meters the various holidays as defined by the city ordinance was confusing; therefore, the ordinance was unenforceable; and (3) until the city clarified the days when payment was not required, the term "holiday" as appearing on the parking meters would be interpreted to mean all of the holidays as defined in N.Y. Gen. Constr. Law § 24. Therefore, defendant did not have to insert a coin in the parking meter on February 22, 1967.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the information filed against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- parking -- no coin is required on February 22 in parking meter which reads that Sundays and "holidays" are excepted, even though city's traffic ordinance omits February 22 from list of "holidays" (General Construction Law, § 24; Vehicle and Traffic Law, §§ 1600, 1683); information dismissed.

A parking meter in the City of Utica bore a sign stating that a coin was required during certain hours but not on Sundays and "holidays". Defendant motorist rightly omitted to deposit a coin on February 22, Washington's birthday, a "public holiday" (General Construction Law, § 24). The city's traffic ordinance defined "holiday" as including only 6 holidays, of which February 22 was not one; whereas the State statute lists 11 holidays, including February 22. Without a suitable sign, the city's ordinance is not enforcible in contravention of the State statute (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1600; § 1683, subd. [a], par. 8; cf. N. Y. Const., art. IX, §§ 1, 2). The information filed against defendant is dismissed.

 

2062 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Style Rite Homes, Inc. v. Zoning Bd. of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Monroe County


54 Misc. 2d 866; 283 N.Y.S.2d 623; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1332



August 1, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation brought a proceeding against respondent, zoning board of appeals (board), for certiorari review of the board's upholding of the denial of the corporation's application for a permit to construct a multiple-family garden court apartment; the corporation also filed an action seeking declaratory judgment that defendant town's zoning ordinance was unconstitutional. The court consolidated the cases and rendered its decision.


OVERVIEW: The corporation sought approval to construct an apartment court. It adduced evidence that the property could not be advantageously used for any purpose permitted by Town of Chili, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance para. 19-7 (1947). A building inspector denied the corporation a building permit. The board upheld denial of the permit. The corporation filed a petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for review of the board's decision and filed a complaint seeking declaratory judgment that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The corporation argued that separation resulting from appropriation of part of the tract for an interstate highway completely frustrated use of the severed portion in conformance with the ordinance, and as such amounted to legal confiscation rendering the ordinance invalid. The court denied the petition and dismissed the complaint. The board's denial of an application for a variance was not so unreasonable, arbitrary, and capricious as to amount to an abuse of discretion as a matter of law. The ordinance was not unconstitutional. Any loss suffered by the corporation was caused not by any act of the town, but by state action, and the severance was not without just compensation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the corporation's petition for review of the board's decision upholding the denial of a building permit and dismissed the corporation's complaint seeking declaratory judgment that a town zoning ordinance was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- hardship variance was properly denied to corporation which had already reaped large profit; corporation's difficulties were caused, not by town's zoning ordinance, but by State's severance of corporation's acreage, for which State had paid corporation consequential damages -- zoning ordinance is not confiscatory and unconstitutional as applied to such remaining acreage -- since court is dismissing article 78 proceeding, court has properly before it, also, corporation's plenary action for declaratory judgment of unconstitutionality.

1. A corporation purchased approximately 31 acres in a one-family zone, and built one-family dwellings on the small part which fronted on a street, and sold all the dwellings, and thereby recouped the price it had paid for all of the 31 acres. Then the State appropriated, for a limited access highway, a 100-foot-wide strip comprising 9.3 acres, which diagonally divided the rest of this acreage into a 17-acre parcel and a triangular 4.02-acre parcel. The State paid the corporation $ 40,000. This was almost four times the price which the corporation had paid for the whole 31 acres six years earlier; that is, this [***2] was at about ten times the original price per acre. A reasonable inference is that the $ 40,000 included severance or consequential damages. Then the corporation combined the 17 acres with other land and sold it for between $ 35,000 and $ 40,000, and sold topsoil for $ 8,900. Thus, the corporation's profit on its original small investment totaled about $ 84,000, and it still had 4.02 acres left. Then the corporation applied to the town's Zoning Board of Appeals for a hardship variance to allow it to build a 60-family garden court apartment development on the 4.02 acres. The corporation's asserted hardship is that this remaining 4.02-acre parcel is triangular, and that, since the zoning ordinance permits only one-family dwellings on 25% of a minimum lot area and requires setbacks, the corporation can build only seven dwellings, and that the corporation's total expenses for streets, sewers, utilities and sales promotion would average about $ 6,000 a house, and that therefore the corporation cannot economically offer seven dwellings for sale at the $ 18,000 to $ 22,000 price range of other house in the neighborhood, and furthermore, that the topography and soil are not advantageous [***3] for a farm or nursery or school or any of the dozen other uses which the zoning ordinance allows in a one-family zone. The board properly denied the application for a variance.

2. The board's refusal to modify the zoning plan and change the character of the neighborhood, in order that the corporation might reap a further profit, was not arbitrary or unreasonable. The corporation's difficulties were caused, not by the ordinance, but by the State's severance of the 31 acres, and for those consequential damages the corporation has already received full compensation.

3. Since the court is now dismissing the corporation's petition under article 78 of the CPLR to review the board's determination, the court has properly before it now the corporation's plenary action for a judgment to declare that the zoning ordinance is confiscatory and unconstitutional as applied to these 4.02 acres. The zoning ordinance is not thus confiscatory and unconstitutional; and the court will render a judgment so declaring and a judgment dismissing the article 78 proceeding.

 

2063 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Volpe v. Fireman's Fund Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


54 Misc. 2d 212; 282 N.Y.S.2d 69; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1359



July 20, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner administratrix, in a special proceeding, sought (1) a compromise order approving the settlement of an action for conscious pain and wrongful death in which she, as administratrix of her husband's estate, was plaintiff, and (2) an order fixing distribution of the proceeds of settlement. Respondent compensation carrier opposed the application.


OVERVIEW: The administratrix's husband died from a heart attack less than two months after he was injured in a car accident. There were conflicting doctor's reports as to whether the heart attack was a result of the car accident. The administratrix brought an action against the compensation carrier seeking a compromise order approving a settlement of an action against a third party defendant for conscious pain and wrongful death in which she, as administratrix of her husband's estate, was plaintiff, and an order fixing distribution of the proceeds of settlement of that case. The compensation carrier opposed the application on the grounds that the settlement offer was grossly inadequate. The court approved the settlement as reasonable in amount, finding that there was a great probability that a jury would not have found a causal relationship between the accident and the heart attack. Unless a causal relationship of the death was established, the administratrix could have recovered only lost earnings and medical and drug expenses. The court fixed distribution of the settlement to the administratrix and to each of the deceased's three children.


OUTCOME: The court issued a compromise order approving the settlement of the administratrix's action for the conscious pain and wrongful death of her husband and fixing distribution of the proceeds of settlement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Workmen's compensation -- third-party action -- compromise -- despite objection of workmen's compensation insurance carrier, court approves compromise for $ 20,000 of action for conscious pain and wrongful death (Decedent Estate Law, § 135; Workmen's Compensation Law, § 29, subd. 5) -- statute authorizing court approval of compromise does not violate any constitutional right of compensation insurance carrier -- court directs allocation of $ 3,500 to cause of action for conscious pain, and $ 16,500 to cause of action for wrongful death, and awards attorney's fees, and determines percentages of distribution of $ 10,013 net recovery for wrongful death among widow and three children (Decedent Estate Law, § 133).

1. Decedent's car, while stopped for a traffic light, was struck in the rear by a car operated by another person. Decedent was treated by his family doctor for a contusion of the knee and for a sprain of the cervical and lumbar spine. Six and a half weeks later he died of an acute myocardial infarction which the autopsy showed to be a "massive cardiac tamponade due to ruptured aorta, ascending". The probabilities are that a jury, presented with all the evidence, would [***2] find that his death was not causally related to the injury. He was 49 years old, his average weekly wage was $ 173, his lost earnings for the six weeks were $ 1,113, and his medical expenses were about $ 204. A verdict of $ 3,500 to $ 4,000 seems probable. His administratrix is granted an order approving, as reasonable in amount, a settlement for $ 20,000 of her action for his conscious pain and his wrongful death (Decedent Estate Law, § 135; Workmen's Compensation Law, § 29, subd. 5, as amd.).

2. Subdivision 5 (as amd.) of section 29 of the Workmen's Compensation Law is authorized by the State Constitution (art. I, § 18) and does not violate the workmen's compensation carrier's rights of fair trial and due process of law or deprive said carrier of any vested property right, even though, when this accident occurred, said subdivision 5 did not provide for court approval, over the carrier's objection as to the amount, of a compromise of the third-party action for an amount less than the compensation payable by the carrier.

3. Of the $ 20,000 settlement, the court allocates $ 3,500 to the cause of action for conscious pain, and $ 16,500 to the cause of action for wrongful death.  [***3] The fee of the attorney is fixed, on said first cause of action at $ 1,100, and on said second cause of action at $ 5,500, both inclusive of disbursements. The funeral expenses were $ 987. Thus, $ 10,013 of the wrongful death recovery remain for distribution. At death, decedent was 49 and had a life expectancy of 23.6 years. His wife was 43 and had a life expectancy of 32.9 years. Thus their joint expectancy was 23.6 years. His 16-year-old daughter would reach her majority in 4 years, 8 months, and 23 days; his 12-year-old daughter in 8 years, 10 months, and 26 days; and his 8-year-old son in 12 years, 9 months and 13 days. Using the formula in Matter of Kaiser (198 Misc. 582), and there being no evidence that any of decedent's three children will require support beyond majority, the courts fixes distribution (Decedent Estate Law, § 133) as follows: to the widow 47.2%; to the older daughter 9.5%; to the younger daughter 17.8%; and to the son 25.5%.
