People v. Cline


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Rensselaer County


54 Misc. 2d 172; 282 N.Y.S.2d 318; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1364



July 18, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who were indicted for gambling and conspiracy to commit gambling separately, joined a motion for the dismissal of their indictments.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted for gambling and conspiracy to commit gambling. They joined in the motion to dismiss after a ruling that declared N.Y. Code Crim. P. § 813-a unconstitutional. That holding made even court-authorized wiretapping illegal. The court noted that the prosecution conceded with commendable candor that the indictments were based on evidence from court-authorized wiretapping orders and that all the evidence of guilt flowed from such source. The court concluded that the evidence was illegally procured and inadmissible even for grand jury purposes and, therefore, all the indictments were dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictments of defendants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- wiretapping -- indictments based on evidence obtained by court-authorized wiretapping dismissed.

Indictments based on evidence which was obtained by tapping telephone wires must be dismissed, even though such wiretapping was authorized by ex parte orders of court (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 249, 813-a; CPLR 4506; Berger v. New York, 388 U.S. 41; People v. Grossman, 20 N Y 2d 346).

 

2065 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Spadafora


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


54 Misc. 2d 123; 281 N.Y.S.2d 923; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1372



July 14, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent resident challenged the issuance of a warrant pursuant to which he was taken into custody and taken to a facility under the jurisdiction of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission (NACC). Petitioner mother filed an application for the civil certification of her son to the NACC pursuant to the N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 206.


OVERVIEW: Prior to the issuance of a warrant, the mother testified that she was a diabetic and that on a number of occasions the resident had taken her insulin syringe. The mother also testified that the resident asked her to ask their family doctor to give him a prescription for methadone, which she did. A physician examined the resident and certified that he was dependent on narcotic drugs. The resident challenged § 206 on constitutional grounds. The court determined that the testimony of the physician and the mother established that the resident was dependent on narcotics within the definition of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 201(2). Further, it was not error for the trial court to deny the mother's request to withdraw the application and discontinue the proceeding because under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 3217, the mother asserted no claim against the resident for which he was liable to her. Additionally, the court rejected the resident's claim that compulsory confinement constituted cruel and unusual punishment. Likewise, the resident's right to due process was not violated by the medical interview and examination, which occurred prior to a full hearing in the question of addiction.


OUTCOME: The court deemed the resident to be a narcotic addict and certified the resident pursuant to the Mental Hygiene Law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Mental health -- drug addiction -- civil procedure for certification and compulsory treatment of narcotic addicts is constitutional.

The Mental Hygiene Law (art. 9, entitled Drug Addiction) validly exercises the police power of the State to provide a civil proceeding, instituted by a petition, for the compulsory medical and judicial examination and certification of persons who are dependent upon narcotic drugs, and for the civil custody and treatment of such persons in a closed setting for not exceeding three years (Mental Hygiene Law, art. 9, §§ 200, 201, 204, 206, 206-a, 206-b). The statute's definition of such persons is not unconstitutionally vague, and the statute's procedure for certification of such persons does not contravene their rights under the United States Constitution (4th, 5th, 6th, 8th, 14th Amdts.).

 

2066 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smiley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 826; 284 N.Y.S.2d 265; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1373



July 14, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for a pre-trial hearing to determine whether a police lineup or showup was unnecessarily suggestive and conducive to irreparable mistaken identification and that, a fortiori, a courtroom identification would be tainted as to thus deprive defendant of due process of law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for robbery in the first degree, grand larceny in the second degree, assault with intent to commit a felony, and possession of a dangerous weapon. Defendant applied by motion for a pretrial hearing challenging the police lineup. The court denied defendant's motion for a pre-trial hearing, holding that no pretrial hearing was necessary. But once the in-court identification of defendant was sought to be introduced, and the court was apprised that the identification was questioned on the basis of a prior police station identification, and defense counsel filed a motion for a hearing to be held outside of the presence of the jury, the trial would be suspended until the completion of the evidence on the hearing and the ruling thereon by the court. Furthermore, if the procedures in the police station were violative of due process, the People were precluded from offering any evidence of the prior identification, but the witness could still identify defendant as the perpetrator of the crime if that identification could be sufficiently distinguishable to be purged of the primary taint.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for a pre-trial hearing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- identification -- hearing required to determine whether police station identification of defendant was violative of due process of law -- determination of issue by court without jury is neither post-trial nor pretrial but during trial if defendant raises issue.

1. A hearing is required to enable the court to determine, as a matter of law, whether the police station identification of defendant was or was not violative of due process of law.

2. The determination of the issue is not a post-conviction remedy nor a pretrial remedy. During the trial before the jury, once the "in court" identification of defendant is sought to be introduced and the court is apprised that the identification is questioned on the basis of the prior police station identification, then upon motion of defense counsel a hearing will be held outside the presence of the jury, the trial being suspended until the evidence is complete and the court's ruling made. Defendant's motion for a pretrial hearing is granted to the extent of permitting a hearing sometime during the trial if the issue of identification is raised.

 

2067 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Vaccaro v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


54 Misc. 2d 206; 282 N.Y.S.2d 881; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1389



July 10, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff provisional employee filed an action against defendant city to recover for vacation pay following his termination.


OVERVIEW: The provisional employee had accrued vacation pay prior to his termination by the city, which he filed an action to recover. The city contended that certification of an eligible list prevented it from granting the provisional employee his accrued vacation pay in that his salary line was taken over by the new employee and that there was no other line or authorization to predicate payment to him. The court did not question the city's right to dismiss the provisional employee. However, the court awarded judgment to the provisional employee for recovery of his accrued vacation pay. The court held that the provisional employee had a vested right to receive compensation for work actually performed, which included salary and accrued vacation pay in accordance with Rule 5.17 of the Rules and Regulations for Administrative Employees of the Board of Education of the City of New York. Thus, the language of Rule 5.17 providing for such payment "unless certification of eligible list prevents" violated the provisional employee's substantive due process rights, pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. Therefore, the court declared that portion of Rule 5.17 void.


OUTCOME: The court awarded judgment to the provisional employee in his action for recovery of vacation pay following his termination by the city.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- provisional employees -- provisional employee, who served for eight and one-half years until eligible list was certified, is awarded judgment for his accrued vacation pay.

A provisional employee (appointed under Civil Service Law, § 65) of the Board of Education of the City of New York has "the same annual leave benefits as regular employees, unless certification of eligible list prevents" (N. Y. City Bd. of Educ. Rules and Reg. for Administrative Employees, rule 5.17). This rule means that he is entitled to his accumulated vacation pay when his appointment is thus terminated. Any other construction of said rule 5.17 would unconstitutionally deprive him of an earned, vested right. To pay him what he has earned would not be a gift of public funds (cf. N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 1). He received interim appointments as a provisional employee for eight and one-half years. He is now awarded judgment for his accrued vacation pay.

 

2068 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gillmore v. J. S. Inskip, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


54 Misc. 2d 218; 282 N.Y.S.2d 127; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1390



July 10, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant car companies sought dismissal for lack of jurisdiction of the buyer's action for negligence and breach of warranty.


OVERVIEW: The car companies were incorporated outside of New York. The buyer purchased a car manufactured by the car companies which caught fire. The buyer filed a claim for negligence and breach of warranty against the car companies. The car companies filed a motion to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction. The court denied the car companies' motion. The court held that pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(a) the court had jurisdiction over car companies because they failed to show that they did not obtain substantial revenue from goods or services rendered in New York. The court held that there was no constitutional prohibition in applying N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(a) retroactively to a claim filed after its effective date based on events before its effective date. The court further held that the "substantial revenue" provision of N.Y. C.P.L.R.302(a) was not unconstitutional because it was predicated on the concept that a defendant who derived substantial revenue from goods used or consumed in the state had sufficient contact with the state to justify jurisdiction over the defendant in an action for damage to property which occurred in the state as a result of acts of the defendant outside the state.


OUTCOME: The court denied the car companies' motion to dismiss the buyer's action for lack of jurisdiction.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service on nonresidents -- plaintiff in New York purchased English automobile from New York distributor, which bought it from Pennsylvania servicer, which imported it from English manufacturer; allegedly because of defective manufacture and service, automobile caught fire in New York and was destroyed; motion of Pennsylvania importer and English manufacturer to dismiss plaintiff's action, for lack of jurisdiction over them, is denied, since said defendants' affidavits fail to show that they do not derive "substantial revenue from goods used or consumed or services rendered, in the state, or * * * substantial revenue from interstate or international commerce" ( CPLR 302, subd. [a], par. 3, cls. [i], [ii], as amd. eff. Sept. 1, 1966) -- retroactive application of amendment constitutional.

1. An English corporation manufactures Aston Martin automobiles. A Pennsylvania corporation imports them and services them and resells and delivers them in Pennsylvania to dealers. A domestic dealer gets them in Pennsylvania and resells them in New York and the Eastern seaboard. About April 29, 1966, plaintiff purchased one from said [***2] New York dealer, who delivered it to plaintiff in New York. On May 20, 1966, the automobile caught fire and was destroyed, allegedly because of defective manufacture and service. In 1967 plaintiff commenced this action, for negligence and breach of warranty, against the dealer and the Pennsylvania importer and the English manufacturer. Motion of the latter two defendants for a dismissal for lack of jurisdiction over them is denied; however, this denial is without prejudice to their asserting the same defense in their answers.

2. Neither the Pennsylvania corporation nor the English corporation "regularly does or solicits business" in New York" or engages in any other persistent course of conduct" in New York ( CPLR 302, subd. [a], par. 3, cl. [i]), despite their exhibition at the 1967 New York Automobile Show. Their New York distributor is not shown to be their agent, but merely an independent contractor to whom a distributorship has been granted.

3. However, this is an action for allegedly committing "a tortious act" -- defective manufacture or service -- "without the state causing injury to person or property within the state" ( CPLR 302, subd. [a], par. 3); and each of [***3] the moving defendants has failed to sustain its burden of showing that it does not come within the further provisions which read "(i) * * * derives substantial revenue from goods used or consumed or services rendered, in the state, or (ii) expects or should reasonably expect the act to have consequences in the state and derives substantial revenue from interstate or international commerce" (as amd. by L. 1966, ch. 590). The "substantial revenue" provisions do not require any connection between the tortious act and the revenue from goods or services or commerce; and the "reasonably expect" provision refers to forum consequences in general and not to the specific event which produced an injury within this State. Defendants' own affidavits show that six automobiles, valued for customs purposes at $ 55,586, moved in international commerce from the English corporation to the Pennsylvania corporation, and they also state facts which raise inferences that the Pennsylvania corporation derives all of its revenue from interstate or international commerce.

4. However, it may be possible for either or both of said defendants to prove that they do not derive "substantial revenue", within the [***4] meaning of the above-cited statute; and therefore they are granted leave to raise the jurisdictional issue in their answer or answers.

5. There is no constitutional objection to applying CPLR 302 (subd. [a], par. 3, cls. [i], [ii], as amd. by L. 1966, ch. 590, eff. Sept. 1, 1966) to this 1967 action which is based on events which occurred in April and May of 1966. The amended statute itself appears to be constitutional, and the moving defendants have not shown that they justifiably relied on the old statute. They may, if so advised, set forth in their answers any facts which will establish that they justifiably relied on the old statute.

 

2069 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carbone


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 762; 283 N.Y.S.2d 468; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1400



July 7, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with a violation of N.Y Veh. & Traf. Law § 380(3) for transporting by motor vehicle over the highways within the state a dangerous article without conspicuously marking or placarding the motor vehicle. More specifically, defendants were charged with transporting 15 drums of ethylene oxide weighing 10,038 pounds. Defendants rested after their motion for acquittal on the prima facie case was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were a driver and the corporation he worked for. Defendants were caught transporting a hazardous liquid without the proper warning labels and in amounts in excess of what was legally permitted. Defendants contended that § 380(3) should be interpreted to require a criminal intent, which neither allegedly had, or that if no criminal intent was need the statute was unconstitutional as an inconsistent invasion of a sphere pre-empted by the Federal government, which had a similar regulation. Defendants also contended it was error for the State not to test the liquid to determine its composition. The court convicted defendants. It was not unconstitutional for § 380 to eliminate the prescribed element of mens rea and create thereby a public welfare offense because the exclusive occupation of a sphere of regulation was not to be implied unless the act of Congress was in actual conflict with law of the State. And, because defendants rested without putting on any evidence from which to contradict the State's case, defendants could not complain that a test was not performed.


OUTCOME: The court found both defendants guilty.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- dangerous freight -- tractor-trailer which carried flammable liquid was not placarded or marked "dangerous" or with name of hazard; freight corporation and driver are found guilty of violating Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 380, subd. 3) -- not necessary for People to make chemical test -- New York regulation similar to Interstate Commerce Commission regulation, but New York statute validly and constitutionally omits to require any proof of intent.

A tractor-trailer, carrying more than 2,500 pounds of ethylene oxide, which is a flammable liquid as defined in section 380 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law and as defined in the regulations of the Interstate Commerce Commission (Code of Fed. Reg., tit. 49), was driven into the Holland Tunnel to the New York side without any placards or markings on the vehicle stating "dangerous" or the name of the hazard (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 380, subd. 3; 15 NYCRR 67.6 [a] [1]). The driver and the freight transportation corporation are found guilty of violating said New York statute. It was not necessary for the People to make a chemical test of this dangerous liquid. The vehicle's lack of marking or placarding violated [***2] also the very similar regulation of the Interstate Commerce Commission (Code of Fed. Reg., tit. 49, § 77.823). The New York statute and regulation do not differ, in this particular requirement, from the Federal regulation and do not unduly burden interstate commerce. The United States Code (tit. 18, § 834, subd. [f]) provides that "Whoever knowingly violates any such regulation shall", etc., whereas the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 380, subd. 3) simply provides that "It shall be unlawful to transport" etc.; however, since this is a public welfare offense, there is no unconstitutionality in not requiring proof of intent.

 

2070 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hinckley v. Hinckley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 1; 281 N.Y.S.2d 165; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1420



June 29, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing disposition, application for counsel fee is denied except that if it be determined that the child is in need of psychiatric therapy and respondent is directed to pay the cost thereof, in accordance with the terms of the agreement, counsel fee will be fixed at $ 500.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother applied for an order modifying upward an out of state divorce decree, which incorporated by reference, but did not merge within, a separation agreement entered into with respondent father. The mother's application was based on grounds that the child was older, that the child's needs had increased, that he needed psychiatric care, and that the father's income was now four times as initially reported.


OVERVIEW: The terms of the agreement contemplated the possibility that the father's income might increase over time and accordingly provided for an escalation in support payments. The father contended that the court lacked jurisdiction to modify and out of state decree, that the alimony increase lacked merit, and that there were no substantial change of circumstances. The court ruled against the father on the jurisdiction issue and ruled in his favor on the other issues. The court held that because N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 7(c), empowered it to hear new classes of actions, it had jurisdiction to hear the current action because enforcement or modification of out of state divorce decrees were a new class of action pursuant to §§ 461, 466, of the Fam. Ct. Act. The court further held that the mother was in no position to request an increase in alimony because the initial agreement was adequate when made and that there was no showing that she was unable to support herself or in danger of becoming a public charge. As for the child support, the court held that there was no substantial change of circumstances because the increased salary was anticipated and provisions were made in the agreement.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the mother's application with respect to increased alimony, denied the application insofar as it sought an increase in support for the child, and retained jurisdiction to determine if the child was in need of psychiatric therapy.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- support -- modification of out-of-State divorce decree -- Family Court has jurisdiction to modify support provisions of out-of-State divorce decrees; but, if decree incorporates separation agreement which provides that it shall not merge in decree, and if support agreement for wife was valid and adequate when made, it cannot be increased subsequently unless she comes in danger of becoming public charge -- however, support provisions for child can be increased if child's needs or father's means increase substantially -- but, in present case, percentage provisions of separation agreement reasonably and adequately provide such increase for child -- however, on issue of whether child needs psychotherapy, Family Court will require study and report by court's psychiatric consultation service; if court determines that child needs such therapy, it will direct father to pay therefor  [***2] according to separation agreement and will award $ 500 counsel fee; otherwise no counsel fee will be allowed.

1. The Family Court has jurisdiction to modify the support provisions of an out-of-State divorce decree (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 7, subd. c; art. VI, § 13, subd. c; Family Ct. Act, § 461, subd. [b]; § 466, subd. [c]). But, if the decree incorporates by reference a separation agreement which contains a provision that it shall not merge into a divorce judgment, and if the support agreement for the wife was valid and adequate when originally made, then the support provisions for the wife cannot be increased subsequently unless she comes in actual danger of becoming a public charge ( McMains v. McMains, 15 N Y 2d 283, followed).

2. However, the agreement's support provisions for the child can be increased if they were improvident or inadequate when made, or if the child's needs or the father's means have substantially increased.

3. But, in the present case, the separation agreement provides, for the support of the wife and child, a minimum payment plus percentages of the husband's income. Thus the agreement fairly, reasonably and adequately provides for [***3] the child according to the father's means. There is no proof that the child needs an increase. Nor is there any proof that the child's welfare is being impaired by the visitation provisions of the agreement.

4. A separation agreement dated January 1, 1963 was incorporated by reference into a Nevada divorce decree in March, 1963. However, the agreement provided that it should not merge into the decree. The agreement provided for the support of the wife and child, annually, 1/3 of the first $ 15,000 of the husband's adjusted gross income, plus 30% of the next $ 9,000 plus 25% of the next $ 12,000 plus 20% of the next $ 14,000, but such support was never to be less than $ 5,000 nor more than $ 13,500; and, in addition, he was to pay all hospital, surgical and dental expenses of the child which exceeded 5% of the sum thus paid to the wife in any calendar year; and he was to determine whether and which Summer camp the child should attend, and he was to pay the reasonable expenses thereof; and he was to have extensive rights of visitation, and to have exclusive control of the child's education, and he was to pay all charges for tuition, board, lodging and expenses of the child at such [***4] school or college as he selected; and, except in emergencies, he was not to be liable for any extraordinary expenses for orthodontures or surgery or psychiatry to which he had not consented in advance. The wife's application to the Family Court, New York County, three and a half years later, to increase the support provisions and to decrease the visitation and Summer camp provisions, is denied.

5. However, if the parties cannot reconcile their views as to whether the child needs psychotherapy, the Family Court will refer the child to the court's psychiatric consultation service for a study and report to the court. If the court determines that the child needs such therapy, the court will direct the father to pay therefor as provided in the separation agreement, and will further direct that he pay a $ 500 counsel fee; otherwise, no counsel fee will be allowed.

 

2071 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York County Lawyers' Asso. v. Dacey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 564; 282 N.Y.S.2d 985; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1431



June 28, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Consequently, the motion is granted adjudging the Dacey respondents in contempt and injunction is granted against all of the respondents and the application is otherwise denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bar association instituted a summary proceeding seeking an order adjudging respondents, the author, publisher, and distributor of a book titled "How To Avoid Probate," guilty of criminal contempt of court for unauthorized practice of law in violation of N.Y. Jud. Law § 750(B) and imposing a permanent injunction, along with other injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The author had been enjoined for the unauthorized practice of law in Connecticut, where he had confronted the public directly, offered his advice as an expert estate planner and distributed trust forms. The book made the same representations enjoined in Connecticut and included printed forms for use by the public. The author asserted his U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The court found the author in contempt for violation of § 750(B) and enjoined his unauthorized practice of law. The court ruled that the author was engaged in the practice of law, which included the drafting of forms and giving advice as to their use. The court held that the author's unauthorized practice, already prohibited in Connecticut, did not attain the security of a constitutional umbrella through the device of transferring that unauthorized practice to a book. The court found that there was no inherent right to the practice of law, which the state had the power to license, regulate, and control. The court found that the violation was clear with regard to the author, but not the publisher and distributor, and, thus, denied the injunction with regard to those respondents.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of the bar association to adjudge the author in contempt for unauthorized practice of law and for an injunction against the author, but denied that motion with regard to the publisher and distributor, and otherwise denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- unlawful practice of law by author of book -- author of book entitled "How to Avoid Probate" which contains detachable forms of trusts and wills with instructions on how to fill in blank spaces is guilty of unlawful practice of law in violation of Judiciary Law ( § 750) -- statute not vague -- nor does it unconstitutionally abridge freedom of speech and press -- State's power to control practice of law is limited to State and interference with interstate commerce is not involved -- publishers and distributors are not guilty of unlawful practice of law but they are enjoined from publishing and distributing.

1. Respondent author of book entitled "How to Avoid Probate", which contains forms including 23 trust instruments and 12 wills capable of being detached, with instructions as to how to fill in the blank spaces, and an order blank for additional instructions represented as being sufficient to accomplish specific purposes, is guilty of practicing law without admission to [***2] the Bar. Through the book the author in effect "confronts" the client and fits and fashions his advice to individual need. He also practices law by sale and further solicited sale on request of forms to fit a precise need.

2. Section 750 of the Judiciary Law prohibiting the practice of law by other than lawyers is not vague.

3. Section 750 of the Judiciary Law is not unconstitutional as abridging freedom of speech and press. The violation of the law is the author's. Unauthorized practice of law cannot be transferred to the book and by that device grant the author constitutional protection.

4. The State's power to control the practice of law is limited to the State of New York and, therefore, interference with interstate commerce is not involved.

5. The publishers and distributors of the book may not be found guilty of practicing law but they are enjoined from publishing and distributing.

 

2072 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bunge Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 325; 282 N.Y.S.2d 576; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1442



June 22, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defrauded investors filed a motion to intervene and an order for a writ of mandamus to compel the attorney general to appoint a receiver of defendant corporation's property pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 353(a) in the state's action against defendant for fraud.


OVERVIEW: The state filed an action under the Martin Act (Act), N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law art. 23-A, against defendant for fraud involving alleged wrongful manipulations of warehouse oil receipts. Several defrauded investors filed a motion to intervene and order a writ of mandamus to compel the state to appoint a receiver of defendant's property, which the state opposed. The court held that the state could not be compelled to make such an appointment. The court found that the attorney general, in enforcing the Act, did not become a securities commissioner who was subject to the directions of the court, as the investors claimed. The court noted that the performance of an administrative function did not convert the attorney general into an administrative officer. The court stated that the actions taken by the attorney general were as the chief legal officer of the state, that the prosecution of defendant was wholly within his discretion, and that the exercise of that discretion could not have been hampered by the courts. The court found that because intervention was permitted only when the court appointed a receiver, and the attorney general declined to do so, the moving parties could not intervene.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the investors' petition to compel the state to appoint a receiver of defendant's property, and it denied the investors' motion to intervene.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Attorney-General -- Martin Act -- court cannot reopen consent judgment in Martin Act action, for purpose of compelling Attorney-General to exercise his discretion as to whether or not to apply for appointment of receiver for benefit of assertedly interested persons who wish to intervene, and cannot compel Attorney-General to let them have evidence he collected in course of his investigation.

The Martin Act (General Business Law, art. 23-A, § 352 et seq.) provides that, if the Attorney-General "believes it to be in the public interest * * * he may in his discretion" investigate fraudulent transactions in respect to stocks, bonds and other securities (§ 352) and bring an injunction action (§ 353), and in such an action the court may appoint a receiver of the property and may then allow intervention by persons who seek to prove that they have an interest in the property held by the receiver (§ 353-a). It is entirely within the discretion of the Attorney-General, the chief legal officer of the State (Executive Law, § 63), whether or not to initiate any of these steps. In such an action the court cannot, of its own volition or on motion of assertedly interested persons [***2] or of members of the public, appoint a receiver or direct the Attorney-General to apply for a receiver. In this case, the Attorney-General conducted a three-year investigation, and then served a complaint without filing it, and then entered a consent judgment and filed the complaint with the judgment. Shortly thereafter, a bank and 21 insurance companies asked the court to vacate the consent judgment and reopen the action and permit them to intervene, and to appoint a receiver; and they also petitioned the court for an order under CPLR article 78 directing the Attorney-General to apply for a receiver and to make available to them the evidence which the Attorney-General collected in his investigation. The motions are denied and the petitions are dismissed.

 

2073 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Prestige Sewing Stores of Queens, Inc. v. Lefkowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


54 Misc. 2d 188; 281 N.Y.S.2d 689; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1453



June 15, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to vacate or modify the subpoenas is denied, and the motion to compel compliance with them is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a corporation and others, and respondent state Attorney General filed two motions: one to quash or modify subpoenas issued by the Attorney-General, and the other to compel compliance with the subpoenas.


OVERVIEW: The corporation contended that, under the wording of N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12), the Attorney-General could not issue the subpoenas authorized therein without a showing that there had actually been a repeated and persistent commission of fraudulent or illegal acts, and that the statute did not authorize the Attorney-General to investigate whether or not such acts were committed or to act upon mere suspicion or surmise. The court held that this claim was without merit. Section 63(12) did not contain any language which made the Attorney-General's authority to issue a subpoena dependent upon the prior existence of proof that repeated fraudulent or illegal acts had in fact been engaged in. On the contrary, the statute empowered the Attorney-General to make a determination of the relevant facts, and it was for the purpose of determining the relevant facts prior to applying for injunction or cancellation of a certificate that he was authorized to issue subpoenas. The court held that if the Attorney-General's demands were to become oppressive, coercive, arbitrary, or capricious, and there was no such showing here, the court had power to grant appropriate relief.


OUTCOME: The motion to vacate or modify the subpoenas was denied, and the motion to compel compliance with them was granted.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- subpoena -- proof of repeated fraudulent acts in transaction of business committed by petitioners need not be shown by Attorney-General prior to valid issuance of subpoenas (Executive Law, § 63, subd. 12) -- statute constitutional -- subpoenas not arbitrary or oppressive -- commission of similar practices by competitor is no justification for petitioners to continue.

1. Proof of actual commission of "repeated fraudulent or illegal acts * * * in the * * * transaction of business" is not a prerequisite to the valid issuance of a subpoena under subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law by the Attorney-General.

2. There is no merit to the claim that the statute, if construed as authorizing the Attorney-General to issue subpoenas in aid of an investigation aimed at ascertaining whether illegal acts had been committed, is unconstitutional.

3. Claims that the subpoenas are arbitrary and oppressive and that they will cause financial loss and hardship and disruption of business in order to comply with them are overruled.

4. The alleged commission [***2] of similar practices by a competitor constitutes no justification for permitting petitioners to continue their own practices if they continue to defraud the public.

 

2074 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schneiderman v. Shenkenberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


54 Misc. 2d 66; 281 N.Y.S.2d 459; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1457



June 14, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner's application granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner brought before the court (New York) a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to review and annul the decision of the respondent zoning board denying his application to use a municipal parking lot to satisfy the off-street parking requirements of the Town of Huntington Building Zone Ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The landowner was the owner of a parcel of land situated in a town. On the parcel was situated a building consisting of two floors. The ground floor had been used for retail purposes and the second floor has been used for storage and residential purposes. The premises had been zoned "general business." The landowner sought to convert the second floor for retail purposes. The court was presented with the unusual proposition of the right of a landowner to convert a nonconforming use to a conforming use. The court held that it had always been loathe to reverse a decision of the zoning board of appeals if it could find some basis for their decision in the record. But the court was required to reverse a decision of the board which in effect told the owner of a land blocked building, situated within 250 feet of a municipal parking lot, that he would be required to produce a parking lot or he would not be able to convert a nonconforming use to a conforming use, and which was so arbitrary, so capricious, so unreasonable, so contrary to public policy and the generally accepted standards of zoning, that it violated all principles of fairness and outraged every sense of justice.


OUTCOME: The landowner's application was granted.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- permission to convert prior nonconforming residential use to conforming business use cannot be denied on ground that owner has no space on which to provide off-street parking, since municipal parking lot is nearby.

Petitioner owns a lot on which stands a two-story building. The ground floor is used for retail business purposes, and the second floor is used for storage and for residential purposes. The premises are in a business zone, and the residential use constitutes a prior nonconforming use. Petitioner's recent application for a building permit to convert the second floor for retail purposes -- a conforming use -- was denied, on the ground that the proposed conversion would constitute an intensification of the business use and therefore comes within the scope of a recent ordinance requiring the owner to provide off-street parking; and, since he has no space available for parking, the Zoning Board of Appeals unreasonably denied his application to satisfy the off-street parking requirement by use of a municipal parking lot which is about 250 feet from his building. A zoning ordinance should be [***2] interpreted in accord with its public policy of gradually eliminating nonconforming uses. Petitioner and all other taxpayers in the town are paying for the bond issue for said municipal parking lot. The other businesses which use said parking lot have no monopoly of it, even though it is generally full. Petitioner cannot be required to find a tenant who will generate no traffic. The decision of the board is annulled.

People ex rel. Goldfinger v. Johnston


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Dutchess County


53 Misc. 2d 949; 280 N.Y.S.2d 304; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1477



June 1, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator sought a writ of habeas corpus to effect his transfer to a civil mental institution so further proceedings could take place under N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 124. After initially pleading guilty to attempted assault, relator was confined to a psychiatric institution after violating parole.


OVERVIEW: The relator sought to effect his transfer to a civil mental institution and to declare N.Y. Correct. Law § 432 unconstitutional. The court granted relator's writ. The court held that it was unnecessary to rule upon the constitutionality of N.Y. Correct. Law § 438. The provision for notice and for a hearing prior to confinement could be read into the section. The court further held that N.Y. Mental Hygiene Law had to be construed in pari materia, thus making applicable the procedural safeguards prescribed in N.Y. Correct. Law § 135. The court found that the distinction between civil and criminal commitment was an unnecessary one. Both were confinement and required procedural safeguards such as notice to the person to be confined as well as a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the writ.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- sentence as mental defective -- person who pleaded guilty in 1956 of attempted assault, second degree, and was sentenced to indeterminate term as mental defective (Correction Law, § 438), is granted habeas corpus order in 1967 for proceedings and hearing pursuant to Mental Hygiene Law ( §§ 124, 135).

A person who in 1956 pleaded guilty of attempted assault, second degree, and was sentenced to serve an indeterminate term in the Eastern Correctional Institution at Napanoch for mental defectives (Correction Law, § 438) and who was transferred in 1966 to the Beacon Institution for Defective Delinquents is granted an order sustaining his writ of habeas corpus in 1967, so that proceedings and hearing may be had pursuant to the Mental Hygiene Law ( §§ 124, 135). A provision for notice and hearing may thus be read into section 438 of the Correction Law so as to sustain its constitutionality ( Baxstrom v. Herold, 383 U.S. 107, and Specht v. Patterson, U.S. 605, followed).

People ex rel. Hahn v. Solkoff


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach


53 Misc. 2d 893; 280 N.Y.S.2d 455; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1486



May 29, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss is denied and counsel directed to appear before this court on Tuesday, June 6, 1967, to fix a trial date.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the criminal prosecution of defendant for violation of the city's zoning ordinance. She challenged the enforcement of the ordinance as discriminatory and violative of the equal protection clause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that she was being singled out for prosecution. She alleged that three criminal prosecutions had been filed against her by the city's building department within less than a year, while others violators went unchallenged, unprosecuted, and unpunished. The court rejected defendant's contention, concluding that grounds supporting a finding of a denial of equal protection were not alleged and that defendant failed to sustain the "heavy burden" of proof of that degree of discriminatory action required to justify the implication-loaded finding of unconstitutionality. In so holding, the court observed that essentially, defendant demonstrated little more than mere nonenforcement of the statute against others similarly situated. The conscious exercise of some selectivity in enforcement was not in itself a federal constitutional violation. The court further observed that even though the statistics might have implied a policy of selective enforcement, it was not stated that the selection was deliberately based upon an unjustifiable standard such as race, religion, or other arbitrary classification.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- discriminatory enforcement -- defendant failed to sustain her burden of proving clearly intentional or invidious discrimination by city's Building Department in prosecuting her for zoning violation; lack of systematic enforcement against other violators is not unconstitutional; defendant's motion to dismiss prosecution denied.

Preliminarily to any trial of this prosecution of defendant for using her one-family residence as a two-family residence in violation of the city's zoning ordinance, the court has held a hearing to enable defendant to sustain her burden of proving her allegations that the city's Building Department has unconstitutionally discriminated against her in instituting three prosecutions against her in less than a year while most other violators have remained immune from prosecution or have been allowed time by the department to comply while this defendant has been given no such consideration by the department, and that therefore her motion to dismiss this prosecution should be granted. The court [***2] finds, after a three-day hearing, that, although the Building Department has no system, plan or pattern for detecting and prosecuting such violations, there has been no invidious, arbitrary or clearly intentional discrimination against this defendant. She shall stand trial.

Defendant's demurrer is overruled.

Di Maggio v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


53 Misc. 2d 1036; 281 N.Y.S.2d 152; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1504



May 24, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the mayor and others, made a motion for an order granting reargument and vacating the prior judgment in favor of petitioner ferry worker.


OVERVIEW: A ferry worker was dismissed from his position under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 108. The ferry worker brought an action against respondents and they subsequently brought a motion for reargument and to vacate the court's prior judgment. The court granted the motion and amended the decision to reflect the subsequent enactment of N.Y. Laws chap. 394, 395. The court found that the effect of the new legislation was to masculate in application here the mandatory operation of the enforcement provisions of the N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §108 and permit an exculpatory course, permissive to the public officials, as to the striking employees of the New York City Housing Authority and the Department of Welfare. The court found that, recognizing the over-all legislative policy, it was a matter of discretion and the court declined its continuing directive, to avoid confusion and disruption. The court found that the ferry worker had been afforded a trial of the constitutional issues and if he was successful there, it was inapparent how a holding of unconstitutionality of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 108 would affect the result.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for reargument and vacated the prior judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- strikes by public employees -- New York City Department of Welfare and New York City Housing Authority are now authorized (L. 1967, chs. 394, 395) to continue to employ, without penalties (cf. Civil Service Law, § 108), employees who struck in January, 1967; accordingly, employee of Department of Marine and Aviation who struck in 1965 and who was penalized and not restored despite similar, subsequent, exculpatory statute for his department (L. 1966, ch. 807), has no standing to petition for judicial review of city's payments of compensation to said welfare and housing employees, and such judgment which he was granted is now vacated.

The Department of Welfare of the City of New York and the New York City Housing Authority are now authorized (by L. 1967, chs. 394, 395, enacted April 21, 1967) to continue in employment all employees who absented themselves from work during certain days in January, 1967, and not to impose on them any of the penalties prescribed by section 108 of the Civil Service Law. Accordingly, a citizen who was employed by the Department of Marine and Aviation of the city and who was penalized, with other employees of that department, for [***2] absenting themselves from work in 1965, and who was not restored despite the subsequent enactment of a similar exculpatory statute for their strike (L. 1966, ch. 807), now has no standing to petition for an order under CPLR article 78 to restrain the city for three years from paying the afore-mentioned welfare and housing employees any increase of compensation (under Civil Service Law, § 108); and such a judgment under CPLR article 78 which the court granted to him on March 27, 1967 (53 Misc 2d 209) is now recalled and vacated and his petition is dismissed. The city's filed notice of appeal from that judgment does not bar it from making this motion for reargument and vacatur of that judgment.

 

2079 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nieke


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Port Jervis, Orange County


53 Misc. 2d 872; 280 N.Y.S.2d 187; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1507



May 23, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss the information is granted, defendant's parole custody is vacated and she is released.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a motion to vacate the issuance of a warrant of arrest and to dismiss, as jurisdictionally defective, an information charging her with violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 1898(5), 1897(4) for selling a BB gun to a person under 16 years of age.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a stock clerk, was substituting for a counter clerk when she turned over the package based on a telephone order. She contended that the information was defective, and that N.Y. Penal Law §§ 1897(4), 1898(5) were unconstitutional. The court granted the motion to dismiss. The court determined that while the information was not so defective as to deny the issuance of a warrant, the supporting affidavit was insufficient to sustain the arrest of defendant. The court found the information defective in that it specified a particular BB gun, but did not indicate it coming within the statute. The court noted that the information alleged the sale by defendant but neither it nor the supporting affidavit sustained a sale, that nothing in the statute indicated that it was a misdemeanor to sell a BB gun, and that the information was based on the affidavit of the purchaser, who was at best an accessory in pari delicto. The court concluded that there was no basis in law or fact to sustain the information, that it was jurisdictionally defective, and that §§ 1897(4), 1898(5) insofar as related to each other and defined in N.Y. Penal Law § 1896(3), (6), were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the information charging her with selling a BB gun to a minor, vacated her parole custody, and ordered her released.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- weapons -- BB gun -- information alleging that defendant, clerk in store, sold slide action BB gun to boy under age 16, dismissed -- boy is accomplice, hence his deposition cannot support information -- sale was made by store, not by clerk -- Penal Law ( § 1897, subd. 4; § 1898, subd. 5) unconstitutionally vague -- information sufficient for arrest but not for trial.

1. Defendant, who is employed as a store clerk and substitute counter clerk by Sears, Roebuck & Co., a mail-order department store, is accused of selling "a Sears & Roebuck slide action BB gun" to a person less than 16 years of age, to wit, a 13-year-old boy, in violation of the Penal Law ( § 1898, subd. 5; § 1897, subd. 4). The information by a State Police investigator is sworn "upon information and belief, the basis for which being police investigation, oral statement of defendant and attached deposition of" the boy. The boy's deposition states that he ordered the gun by telephone and called for it in person and paid this clerk $ 11.98 and that she gave him a box containing the gun and [***2] did not ask him his age. The information is dismissed, on the following grounds.

2. The boy is an accomplice of the defendant (Penal Law, § 1897, subd. 4) and hence his deposition cannot support the policeman's information (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 148, 399).

3. The information alleges, in the language of the Penal Law ( § 1898, subd. 5), that this clerk "disposed of" the gun to the boy. That phrase is defined as including a "sale" (Penal Law, § 1896, subd. 6). The boy's deposition alleges that she "sold me the gun" in "a box". It cannot be said that defendant clerk -- rather than Sears, Roebuck & Co. -- "sold" the gun.

4. The information and deposition do not allege that this was an "air-gun, spring-gun, or other instrument or weapon in which the propelling force is a spring or air", nor do they allege that this was a "weapon in or upon which any loaded or blank cartridges may be used" or that the defendant delivered to the boy any "cartridges or ammunition therefor" (Penal Law, § 1897, subd. 4). The abbreviation "BB" means merely that a projectile is 0.18 inch in diameter; the projectile itself may be paper, cotton, water, cork, rubber, plastic, etc. The statute refers to [***3] section 1896 (subd. 3) of the Penal Law, which defines "Firearm" as including one "of a size which may be concealed upon the person"; whereas this BB gun was a rifle 40 inches long. Moreover, minors between the ages of 14 and 16 may be licensed to hunt with a gun if accompanied by parent or guardian (Conservation Law, §§ 219, 238). As applied to this case, the penal provisions (Penal Law, § 1897, subd. 4; § 1898, subd. 5) are unconstitutionally vague.

5. The information was sufficient to justify the issuance of the warrant of arrest (Code Crim. Pro., § 150, subd. 2) but it was not sufficient for a trial.

 

2080 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dobish v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Wayne County


53 Misc. 2d 732; 279 N.Y.S.2d 565; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1536



May 10, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town citizen instituted an action seeking a declaratory judgment that the apportionment of defendant county board of supervisors was unconstitutional under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 and in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: One supervisor represented each town on the board. The court declared that the apportionment of the county supervisors was unconstitutional as the eight smallest towns representing approximately 30 percent of the county's population constituted a majority on the board. Recognizing that the board's malapportionment, the board instituted a "weighted voting plan" directly proportional to each town's population. The court also found this plan unconstitutional as it did not provide that the vote of any citizen was approximately equal in weight to that of any other citizen. In so holding, the court observed that a block of 125 votes assigned to the most populous town under the plan gave it the opportunity to cast the decisive vote 19.61 percent of the time. As the plan overweighted the town's vote inequitably over that of the other towns, the plan constituted invidious discrimination in violation of the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court declared the board's apportionment and weighted voting plan unconstitutional. The court permitted the board to function as such until a plan was approved by the court.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- plan of weighted voting analyzed by digital computer and found to be very unequal and therefore unconstitutional -- mental defectives in Newark State School are ineligible to vote (Election Law, § 152, subd. 6).

1. Wayne County consists of 15 towns. Their populations, according to a special 1966 census, range from 1,570 (Town of Huron) to 12,544 (Town of Arcadia); yet each town elects one Supervisor on the 15-man Board of Supervisors. This is unconstitutional inequality. Each resident of the county is entitled to be equally represented on the county's legislative body (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11). The present Board of Supervisors proposes to weight each Supervisor's vote on the board according to the population of his town, so that, for example, the Supervisor from Huron would cast 16 votes on the board, the Supervisor from Arcadia 125 votes, etc., and thus the 15 Supervisors would cast 713 votes in all. A digital computer analysis of this proposal shows that the Supervisor from Arcadia could cast the decisive vote in 19.61% of different voting situations on the board, and the Supervisor [***2] from Huron only 2.148%, etc. This plan is unconstitutional.

2. In one of the towns is the Newark State School, consisting of 2,620 patients, only 115 of whom are residents of this county. All of the 2,620 patients have been duly committed as mental defectives and are therefore ineligible to vote (Election Law, § 152, subd. 6). None of them should be added to the population of the town for the purpose of electing and apportioning town Supervisors.

Trustees of Sailors' Snug Harbor v. Platt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


53 Misc. 2d 933; 280 N.Y.S.2d 75; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1547



May 8, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the application is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner trustees brought N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceedings to vacate the determination of respondent landmark preservation commission, which found that certain of the trustees' buildings, were landmarks.


OVERVIEW: The commission had designated buildings maintained by the trustees for retired seamen landmarks. The trustees had made plans to demolish the old buildings to construct new, modern facilities. The trustees challenged the constitutionality of the commission's actions and the enabling legislation found in New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 207-8.0(2). The court found that the commission's designations, under the authority of § 207-8.0(2), imposed a great burden on the trustees. The undue burden amounted to an unlawful taking of the property without just compensation. The location of the building on the harbor was a good area for retired seamen. The restraint placed on the trustees prevented them from using the property to its best advantage. The court further found that there was no relation between the designation and the public health, safety, and general welfare.


OUTCOME: The court granted the application.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- unlawful taking -- landmarks -- designation as historic landmarks of old buildings occupied by retired mariners on property of "Sailors' Snug Harbor", imposed burden upon Trustees amounting to unlawful taking of property without just compensation -- landmarks designation set aside.

1. Petitioner, trustees of a charitable corporation operating, under a testamentary trust, a home for elderly mariners on Staten Island called "The Sailors' Snug Harbor", has made plans to construct new modern buildings to replace four ancient dormitory buildings presently occupied by about 200 retired seamen. However, the Landmarks Preservation Commission has designated the Harbor's buildings as landmarks, thus prohibiting petitioner from altering or demolishing them without Commission approval (Administrative Code of City of New York, §§ 207-5.0, 207-6.0), and requiring petitioner, at the risk of criminal sanctions, to maintain them in good repair. To obtain a certificate of appropriateness permitting demolition petitioner, as a tax-exempt owner, must establish execution of an agreement to sell the buildings or to lease them for [***2] a term greater than 20 years, contingent on approval of demolition by the Commission, (Administrative Code, § 207-8.0, subd. a, par. [2]). Petitioner also would have to show that the buildings were not suitable for carrying out the purposes of the Harbor and would not yield a reasonable return if not tax-exempt. Although the State and city have power to take reasonable steps to prevent destruction of buildings having a civic, educational or cultural value, the burden imposed upon petitioner exceeds the limits of reasonable regulation, prevents petitioner from utilizing the land to its best advantage, and amounts to an unlawful taking of property without just compensation.

2. The designation of the buildings as landmarks is set aside.

People v. Radich


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


53 Misc. 2d 717; 279 N.Y.S.2d 680; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1550



May 5, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an action that charged him with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 1425(16).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, an art dealer, publicly exhibited for sale a number of three-dimensional constructions. The constructions were different representations of the American flag. One structure was a flag stuffed and shaped to suggest a human body hanging with a noose around its neck. The State prosecuted defendant under N.Y. Penal Law § 1425(16). After trial, defendant filed a motion to dismiss. Defendant argued that the statute violated among other things his constitutional right to free speech. Defendant argued that the exhibited art was known as "protest art," and that there was no intent of contempt for the flag. The court held that the structures constituted a contemptuous use of the flag. The court further held that defendant had cast contempt upon the flag by exhibiting the structures. The court held that the structures were not protected. The court further found that the statute was not so vague as to violated the due process clause of the 14th Amendment. The court also found that the statute did not constitute a malum prohibit, thus there was no intent element prerequisite to convict.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss and found him guilty.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- American flag -- art dealer who displayed so-called "protest art constructions" using American flag is found guilty of violating Penal Law ( § 1425, subd. 16, par. d).

At an art gallery, 13 three-dimensional so-called "protest art constructions" were displayed, two of which "publicly mutilate, deface, defile * * * or cast contempt upon" the American flag, in violation of the Penal Law ( § 1425, subd. 16, par. d). The art dealer is found guilty of violating said statute. One "construction" is an American flag stuffed in a shape suggesting a human body hanging from a noose; the other is a cross with a bishop's mitre on the headpiece and with ecclesiastical flags wrapped around the arms and, on the vertical portion, an American flag wrapped around a form suggesting a phallus. This is not an "ornamental picture" within the exceptions in the statute. The statute is not unconstitutionally vague, and, as thus applied, does not violate defendant's rights of free speech or due process of law or equal protection of the laws.

 

2083 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zogby v. State


Claim No. 39904


Court of Claims of New York


53 Misc. 2d 740; 279 N.Y.S.2d 665; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1551



May 5, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant property owner sought review of the decision issued by the appellate court (New York), which reversed a decision rendered in favor of the property owner in an action concerning appropriation of the property owner's land.


OVERVIEW: This was a claim for the appropriation of the property owner's land. A ruling was issued for the property owner, the state appealed, and the judgment in favor of the property owner was reversed and a new trial was granted. The appellate court, in reversing the original decision, did so in part on the lack of evidence relative to a probability of zoning change, residential to industrial. The property owner contended that the zoning amendment, which changed the subject property from unclassified to residential, was invalid and null and void. The court held that after examining the zoning maps, it had to conclude that the subject property was in an unclassified zone in 1955 that permitted an industrial use. The court also stated the property owner's property could have been valued with an enhancement for probable industrial use. However, the court also stated that the proof before the court was that the property owner's right of way was restricted to farm use. In the interest of justice, the court directed that the trial be reopened on testimony pertaining to farm value and the practice of railroad companies, particularly the company in ownership of the right of way.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the state in proceedings concerning the appropriation of the property owner's land. The court held that the property owner's land was used for at least 35 years for farm purposes and all of the evidence supported this conclusion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent domain -- compensation -- claimant's land properly valued on basis of industrial use where zoning change to residential use was held invalid -- State could not, by taking more land than needed for highway, prevent enhancement of value of property by proximity of land to Thruway interchange -- moreover, chapter of law which authorized greater taking than needed for highway may be unconstitutional -- trial reopened for purpose of considering value based on industrial use and for considering probability that railroad over whose tracks claimant has right of way in nature of farm crossing, may make agreement with claimant to increase use of crossing for industrial purposes -- despite long interval between filing of map and service thereof on claimant, interest is limited to six months after filing of map -- judicial notice not taken of expert testimony shown in another trial as to amount of borrowed fill to be taken from claimant's land.

1. Claimant's property, when taken by the State by the filing of an appropriation map in 1955, was in an unclassified zone which permitted an industrial use. The 1954 zoning amendment which changed the subject property from unclassified [***2] to Residential B was held null and void in Soron Realty Co. v. Town of Geddes (23 A D 2d 165). Accordingly, claimant's property could be valued with an enhancement for probable industrial use. It is proper to consider sales for industrial purposes in this area in relation to its fair market value before the appropriation.

2. Claimant's land was appropriated pursuant to section 30 of the Highway Law as amended by chapter 797 of the Laws of 1953. An examination of such statute and an exhibit indicates that the State never intended to use claimant's property for highway purposes but intended to make it available to the county for park purposes. Moreover, the State's improved highway does not come close to the subject property which was declared surplus and conveyed in 1960 to the county. By lumping together in one statute a present public highway use and a future public park use, the State seeks to prevent the landowner from obtaining fair market value predicated upon the proximity of claimant's land to an important interchange of the Thruway. Value may be considered as enhanced by the property's proximity to the Thruway.

3. Chapter 797 of the Laws of 1953 which premeditated [***3] taking more land than needed may be unconstitutional and violative of section 7 of article I of the Constitution.

4. Claimant's property was landlocked and access to it came from a farm crossing across a railroad right of way. Claimant's right of way across the railroad tracks was restricted to a farm use. The reasonable probability, if any, of obtaining a private agreement from the railroad to increase the crossing user from farming to industrial is a subject of proof about which none was given. In the interests of justice, the trial is ordered reopened for testimony on (1) farm value, predicated on comparable sales, and damage; and (2) the practice of railroad companies, particularly the railroad which owns the land, relative to the possibility of an increased user, by private agreement, from farm use to industrial use of land adjacent to railroad tracks.

5. Despite the long interval between the date of filing of the appropriation map on January 6, 1955 and personal service of said map on claimant on December 7, 1959, she is not entitled to interest during that period but only for the six-month period from January 6, 1955 and the period after December 2, 1961 to the date of [***4] entry of the judgment herein.

6. Judicial notice may not be taken of testimony of an expert witness in Winkelman Co. v. State of New York (17 Misc 2d 418) as to the amount of borrowed fill which claimant there could have removed from the property of claimant here. Claimant's land was intact in January 1967 and claimant could easily have obtained expert testimony relative to borrowed fill. In any event it is academic since claimant's appraiser did not seek to testify to the enhancing value of borrow deposits and this prior expert testimony was proffered after said appraiser had completed his testimony.

 

2084 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fenster v. Leary


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


53 Misc. 2d 774; 279 N.Y.S.2d 743; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1554



May 4, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is disposed of awarding judgment of dismissal to the defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen filed a motion for summary judgment in his action seeking a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. 887(1) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was arrested and acquitted on a charge of vagrancy on three separate occasions. Previously plaintiff instituted an action against the criminal court to prohibit it from hearing or determining the vagrancy case. The action was dismissed. Subsequently, plaintiff brought an action in federal court to enjoin the enforcement of the vagrancy statute, found in N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 887(1), and to declare it unconstitutional. The action was dismissed. Plaintiff then brought an action in state court on the same ground. The court found that the statute was not vague. The court further found that plaintiff had failed to state a cause of action and that his complaint was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion and dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- vagrancy -- subdivision 1 of section 887 of Code of Criminal Procedure defining vagrancy is constitutional; complaint dismissed.

Subdivision 1 of section 887 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, which provides that a person is a vagrant who not having visible means to maintain himself lives without employment, is constitutional, is a lawful exercise of the police power and is a valid exercise of legislative power. Due process and equal protection of the law are not denied. The phrase "visible means of support" does not require resort to unlawful means prohibited by the Thirteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution. Neglect to seek employment is one of the conditions for charge and conviction but the compulsion to be free from neglect does not produce involuntary servitude in the context of the Thirteenth Amendment. Defendant is granted judgment dismissing the complaint.

 

2085 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Widziewicz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Dutchess County


53 Misc. 2d 813; 279 N.Y.S.2d 857; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1565



April 28, 1967 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an indictment charging defendant with the crimes of murder in the first degree and grand larceny in the first degree. Defendant claimed that the indictment was void as the grand jury finding the indictment had no legal authority to inquire into the crimes charged, that the grand jury was improperly constituted and convened, and that the July grand jury was not extended by court order into August.


OVERVIEW: In November of 1965, the county judge submitted an order to the Appellate Division of the Second Department designating and appointing the 1966 criminal and grand jury terms. The appellate division kept the order and filed it in the county clerk's office in February 1967. The court held that N.Y. Jud. Law § 190-c was complied with for the year 1966 as filing was an administrative function resting with the appellate division. Advance filing under § 190-e was no longer necessary to create jurisdiction in the court as long as there was compliance with § 190-c and N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 45, and as long as the appointment and designation of terms was subsequently filed pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 190-e. Even if the late filing deprived the court of jurisdiction when the July grand jury was convened, that did provide a basis for a dismissal of the indictment. Accordingly, defendant's claim that the indicting grand jury was not a grand jury because there was no July term of county court was denied. As § 190-c(3) authorized a county judge to continue each term as long as deemed necessary, the July grand jury was properly in session in August when the indictment was found and presented.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for an order dismissing the indictment against him.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury -- Terms of County Court -- late filing of designation of Grand Jury Terms of County Court immaterial -- July Grand Jury did not recess; it properly indicted defendant in August.

The Dutchess County Judge in November, 1965, filed in the office of the Appellate Division, Second Department, for approval and filing, an order which designated and appointed 12 Criminal Terms of the court for 1966, including terms commencing on July 6, August 1, and September 6, 1966, and 6 Grand Jury Terms, including one commencing July 5 and one commencing September 6, 1966. This order was filed in the Dutchess County Clerk's office in February, 1967 -- properly even if late. The July Grand Jury did not recess; it properly found indictments in August and early September. Defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment which the Grand Jury found against him on August 15, 1966, on the asserted ground that the Grand Jury was not then properly in session, is denied. (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 45, 244; Judiciary Law, §§ 190-c, 190-e, 214).

 

2086 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Knox


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Monroe County


53 Misc. 2d 889; 280 N.Y.S.2d 65; 1967 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1567



April 27, 1967 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent juvenile objected to the admission of a purported confession the grounds that a previous interrogation of the juvenile was inadmissible and tainted the subsequent interrogation which the State sought to introduce.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was questioned by a police officer in the office of the school principal with the principal and a teacher present. He did not notify the juvenile's parents of the interrogation, and he did not advise the juvenile that anything he said might be used against him at the time of the court hearing or that he might have an attorney represent him at the time of the questioning. Three days later, the juvenile was questioned again, this time in the presence of one parent. At trial, the juvenile's counsel objected to the admission of the second purported confession. The court granted the motion and excluded both confessions. Denying the juvenile counsel and protection of his parent made his first statement inadmissible whether or not they were made voluntarily. The first confession violated the requirements of due process guaranteed by the Fourteenth Amendment of the Federal Constitution as set forth in Miranda. In the mind of a 12-year-old, there was no difference between the circumstances and the subject matter of the questions asked at the first confession and the second confession. The relation was so close that the one confession controlled the other.


OUTCOME: The juvenile's motion to exclude both confessions made by the juvenile was granted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- juvenile delinquents -- policeman's questioning of schoolboy in presence of school principal without notifying parents violated due process; similar confession obtained from boy three days later in presence of his mother after reading off list of boy's rights, likewise invalid; both confessions inadmissible.

A 12-year-old schoolboy was questioned by a police officer in the presence of a teacher and the school principal in said principal's office, without any prior notification to the boy's parents. The boy admitted that he had broken about 53 windows of the school on the day before. Three days after this improper questioning (Family Ct. Act, § 724; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.) the boy signed a similar confession in the presence of his mother after the police officer read to the boy a full list of his rights. This made an empty form of due process. Undoubtedly the boy thought that he was being called in with his mother to repeat the same story he had told earlier under unconstitutional pressures. Both confessions shall be excluded from evidence.
