Phalen v. Theatrical Protective Union, etc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


51 Misc. 2d 334; 273 N.Y.S.2d 248; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1675; 62 L.R.R.M. 2689; 54 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,532



July 19, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons, petitioners' motion is granted as prayed for, and respondent's cross motion to dismiss is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, petitioners, stagehands and stage maintenance workers, filed a motion in their action, seeking to compel respondent union, the sole collective bargaining agent for stagehand and stage maintenance workers employed in two counties, to admit them to membership. Petitioners' membership eligibility ceased when they failed to earn a specified amount in a calendar year. The union cross-moved to dismiss the suit.


OVERVIEW: Since the union operated under a closed shop arrangement with the employers, petitioners were required to pay a percentage of their gross earnings as an assessment for the privilege of pursuing this employment. Also, petitioners were beneficiaries of the union pension fund. The court held that petitioners were entitled to the relief they requested. While the general rule was that a union was free in the absence of special legislation to the contrary, to choose its members and to reject applications without judicial interference, many jurisdictions had refused to extend this rule to situations where a labor union held a monopoly in the supply of labor in the locality, or attempted to maintain both a closed shop and an arbitrarily closed union. Here, the union had engaged in arbitrary admission and monopolistic practices. Also, its arbitrary refusal to admit petitioners into membership threatened to effectuate a divestment of their pension fund benefits. This was tantamount to an unconstitutional usurpation of property. Finally, the practice of giving preference to applicants with filial ties to union members was illegal in light of constitutional and legislative guarantees.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioners' motion as prayed for, and denied the union's cross motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Labor unions -- membership -- "closed shop" union admits only close relatives to membership and requires nonmembers to pay 4% of their earnings, and, if their earnings fall below $ 4,000 annually they cease to be eligible for union pension fund benefits: petitioners' earnings have been thus curtailed in favor of union members; union is directed to admit petitioners to membership.

Respondent union is the sole collective bargaining agent for stagehand and stage maintenance workers employed in the counties of New York and Bronx. It operates under a "closed shop" arrangement with the employers. Membership in the union is open only to sons or close relatives of existing members. Petitioners have been working as stagehands for many years but they are not members, and, because of their age, they are not eligible for the union's apprenticeship program, which is the only other route to membership. The union requires nonmembers, such as petitioners, to pay 4% of their earnings to the union. They are made beneficiaries of the union's pension fund, but,  [***2] if a member fails to earn at least $ 4,000 a year from contributing employers his pension eligibility ceases. Petitioners' employments have been curtailed or terminated from time to time in favor of members of the union, to such an extent that now they have ceased to be eligible for pension fund benefits. In this inequitable and monopolistic situation, petitioners are granted an order ( CPLR, art. 78) directing the union to admit them to membership (cf. Civil Rights Law, § 43).

 

2130 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Orlando v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Genesee County


53 Misc. 2d 377; 279 N.Y.S.2d 42; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1676



July 19, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3212, plaintiff citizens sought a declaratory judgment attacking the apportionment of defendant county board of supervisors as being violative of the Equal Protection Clause and comparable sections of the state constitution. Among other items of relief, the citizens additionally sought a declaration that N.Y. County Law § 150 and N.Y. Town Law §§ 29(14), 41(4) were unconstitutional as they applied to the county.


OVERVIEW: The citizens contended that because of the gross disparity in population among the towns and wards in the county, there resulted an arbitrary impairment of the votes of plaintiffs with respect to the apportionment of the board and that such an impairment deprived them of equal protection of laws. The court noted that the guarantee of "one person, one vote" had equal application to elective legislative bodies below the state legislature. Thus, the board had been required to insure that the vote of each citizen was approximately equal in weight to that of every other citizen. Although the court declined to prepare an apportionment of the board which would comply with the federal and state constitutions, as that relief was primarily a matter for the legislature, the court did grant the declaratory relief sought by the citizens. The court, however, believed that it was in the interest of orderly government to permit the board additional time to comply with the constitutional requirements. The court took into account the fact that the board had a committee in being which was attempting to prepare a permanent plan of reapportionment which would comply with federal mandates.


OUTCOME: The court granted the citizens' petition for declaratory relief, declined to prepare its own order of reapportionment, and granted the board additional time in which to prepare its own reapportionment plan, with no costs allowed to any party.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- Genesee County Board of Supervisors is directed to reapportion itself constitutionally and promptly; weighted voting meantime not required.

Judgment was granted on July 19, 1966 declaring that the existing apportionment of the Board of Supervisors of Genesee County violated the equal protection provisions of the United States Constitution (14th Amdt.) and of the State Constitution (art. I, §§ 1, 11) but that the existing board should continue to function until the terms of office of its members expired on December 31, 1967; and directing the existing board to reapportion itself constitutionally and promptly so that any necessary referendum could be held at the general election in November, 1966 and so that candidates could be nominated at the usual time in 1967. The court declined to impose any interim plan of weighted or fractional voting.

 

2131 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Iroquois Gas Corp. v. Gernatt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Erie County


50 Misc. 2d 1028; 272 N.Y.S.2d 291; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1677



July 19, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff public utility brought an action against defendant property owners in the Supreme Court of New York for Erie County (New York) pursuant to N.Y. Conserv. Law. art 3-A, § 86 to acquire gas storage rights in the property owners' land. The property owners challenged the public utility's right to condemn their property.


OVERVIEW: The property owners presented several arguments to challenge the public utility's action to condemn the property belonging to the property owners for purposes of acquiring gas storage rights. The court rejected all of the property owners' arguments and granted the public utility's petition to condemn the property and set in motion the procedure to ascertain the compensation to be paid to the property owners. The court also pointed out that the statute under which the public utility sought condemnation of the property owners' land was not deny the property owners' due process right to equal protection of the law. The court noted that the property owners were not within any class covered by the statute and, thus, had no standing to object. Even if the property owners had standing to object, the fact that the statute operated in favor of an existing use did not make it unconstitutional. Further the language of the statute concerning "commercially reasonable" and "otherwise lawfully operating an underground reservoir" were not so indefinite as to render the statute unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the public utility was entitled to take and hold the gas storage rights in the property owners' land upon payment of just compensation therefor. The court also ordered that commissioners of appraisal be appointed to ascertain the compensation to be paid for the property taken.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent domain -- underground gas storage facility -- in proceeding by gas corporation to acquire gas storage rights in defendants' land, court determines as fact that subject property is located within Quaker Pool -- subject property sufficiently described in petition -- Conservation Law (§ 86) does not require that petition allege presence of commercially recoverable native oil and gas -- failure to designate, as property to be acquired, formations below Medina formation does not render petition insufficient -- plaintiff's operation of present underground pool is not unlawful -- plaintiff shows public necessity to acquire defendants' land -- defendants lack standing to challenge constitutionality of Conservation Law (art. 3-A) -- in any event, statute's favoring of existing use is not unreasonable classification -- Conservation Law (§ 86) not unconstitutional because compensation not provided for native gas in place -- question of damage rather than constitutionality is presented by defendants' claim to formations below Medina formation -- statute is not unconstitutionally vague.

1. In a special proceeding by a public utility engaged in the storage and sale of gas, to acquire [***2] gas storage rights in land owned by defendants, the court determines on the papers as a question of fact that the subject property is located within the Quaker Pool.

2. The subject property is sufficiently described by metes and bounds to identify it and the petition seeks storage rights in the Medina formation conceded to exist under the property.

3. Section 86 of the Conservation Law does not require that the petition allege the presence or nonpresence of commercially recoverable native oil and gas. It only requires that if such oil or gas is present, compensation be paid for it.

4. The petition is not insufficient for failure to designate as property to be acquired all the geological formations below the Medina formation. Defendants may offer proof to show that their use and enjoyment of the geological formation below the Medina will be damaged by plaintiff's acquisition of storage rights in the Medina formation and if damaged they are entitled to compensation.

5. Section 86 of the Conservation Law authorizes a corporation empowered to store and distribute gas which is "otherwise lawfully operating" an underground storage reservoir, to acquire such rights by condemnation.  [***3] The contention that plaintiff's operation of the Quaker Pool is unlawful because it does not have leases authorizing it to store under the entire surface acreage within the Quaker Pool is rejected. It would render the statute meaningless since the only gas companies that would be "lawfully operating" a pool would be those already having leases on 100% of the storage area -- those having no need for condemnation.

6. While private property cannot be taken for public use unless it is necessary for such public use, the necessity need only be a reasonable one. Plaintiff already has storage rights in most of the acreage where the Quaker Pool is located. The public necessity to acquire the remainder has been adequately shown by plaintiff.

7. An attack on the constitutionality of article 3-A of the Conservation Law under which plaintiff seeks to condemn the property cannot be made by defendants who are not in either of the classes covered by the statute and their rights have not been invaded.

8. Assuming that defendants could object, the fact that the statute operates in favor of an existing use does not make it unconstitutional. Classifications between existing and new enterprises [***4] are permissible so long as the classifications are not arbitrary or unreasonable. The classification here is not unreasonable or arbitrary.

9. Section 86 of the Conservation Law is not unconstitutional because it does not provide for compensation for native gas in place. Native gas not commercially recoverable has no value to defendants.

10. A question of constitutionality is not presented by the claim that defendants will be deprived of the value of or access to geological formations below the Medina formation. If defendants lose the use and enjoyment of other strata, they are entitled to compensation. A question of damages is presented.

11. The phrases in the statute (§ 86) "commercially recoverable" and "otherwise lawfully operating an underground storage reservoir" are not unconstitutionally vague.

 

2132 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Morris v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Herkimer County


50 Misc. 2d 929; 273 N.Y.S.2d 453; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1692



July 13, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, Herkimer County residents and electors, challenged a reapportionment plan submitted by defendant Board of Supervisors of Herkimer County, which was ordered by the court in a prior decision to propose a constitutional reapportionment plan.


OVERVIEW: The Board appointed a reapportionment committee that considered the redistricting problem. By resolution, the Board adopted by the committee's Plan B that provided for permanent weighted voting. The court rejected the plan because it did not meet the one person, one vote test and was, therefore, unconstitutional, A mathematical analysis indicated the grave disparities under weighted voting as proposed in Plan B. For example, one town had approximately 10 percent less than the mean or unweighted average of the voting power based on its population, but more than one 100 percent more voting power than the residents of another town. Weighted voting could also diminish the power of the most powerful personality in the group in cases where he had a relatively small vote. To plunge into a mathematical quagmire understood only by experts using computers, was not the path to equal representation of citizen voters in local legislative bodies.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the proposed plan and directed the Board to draft a permanent plan of reapportionment to submit to the electorate for approval or disapproval.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- weighted voting is not constitutional; such plan for members of County Board of Supervisors is rejected by court.

Reapportionment of a county Board of Supervisors by means of weighted voting, that is, by giving the Supervisors from the more populous towns or districts 2 votes or 3 votes or 9, 10 or 12 votes, etc., according to the population of their respective towns or districts, does not fulfill the constitutionally required principle that each resident of the county should not have more or less than one vote. The court rejects such a plan of weighted voting, and directs the present Board of Supervisors to submit to the court promptly a proper and constitutional plan which shall be submitted to a referendum at the general election which takes place four months from now.

 

2133 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Greenburgh v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


51 Misc. 2d 168; 272 N.Y.S.2d 906; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1711



July 6, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county officials moved for an order declaring that a local law regarding membership and voting strength of the county board of supervisors complied with a prior court order and was consistent with U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. Plaintiff citizens cross-moved for an order nullifying the law as unconstitutional and enjoining defendants from placing the law for referendum. Two towns intervened to object to the law.


OVERVIEW: The burden was on the citizens to show that the local law denied to them the right to an effective vote in the election of the county board and that the discrimination was invidious. Although the court noted the board's efforts to adhere to the standards provided by the court's prior order, the question to be decided was whether the local law complied with the constitutional requirements of substantial equality in voting power. In other words, at issue was whether the citizens had shown that the weight of their votes had been substantially diluted when compared with the votes of those living in other political subdivisions of the county. The court found that it appeared from the figures submitted that the local law resulted in the vote of each citizen not being as equal as might be practicable to the vote of any other. Population could not submerged as the controlling consideration of the apportionment of voting strengths. The court declined to issue permanent relief in the case, allowing the board to conduct further consideration of the matter, and granted interim relief disapproving the local law and its application through the referendum process.


OUTCOME: Although the court declined to prescribe a plan of permanent reapportionment, it remitted the matter to the board for further consideration, with the court retaining jurisdiction for all purposes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- reapportionment plan submitted by Westchester County Board of Supervisors found to contain disparities which result in substantial dilution of voting strength of certain subdivisions when compared, on population basis, with that of others -- such dilution could be avoided by further application of method of fractional or weighted voting utilized elsewhere in plan -- court does not reach determination as to whether said method is unconstitutional per se but decides only that its limited use in plan under consideration is unconstitutional -- matter remitted to board for further consideration -- interim plan prescribed.

1. A reapportionment plan submitted by the Westchester County Board of Supervisors provides, among other things, for an increase in the board membership from 45 to 54 members, an increase in the total voting strength of the board from 45 to 48 votes, nine additional representatives from specified towns with large populations, fractional votes for representatives of specified towns with small populations and specified cities, and a continuance of the representation of the other town and city supervisors.  [***2] It combines fractional voting in both single and multi-member districts with voting in single and multi-member districts in which each representative has a single unweighted vote. This might be constitutionally permissible if, as a result, the voting power of each legislator was substantially equal in the ratio that the population represented by him bore to the total county population. However, the plan fails to accomplish that result and the matter must be remitted to the board for further consideration.

2. The plan uses a numerical basis of 18,000 persons for each vote and assumes that the "one person, one vote rule" permits a single legislator with an unweighted vote to represent a unit of population which contains 18,000 persons plus or minus 2,700 persons, an approximate 15% variation from the norm of 18,000, and that all subdivisions having populations of less than 15,300 persons should be given fractional votes and all subdivisions having populations of more than 20,700 persons should be represented by more than one legislator, on the basis of one for each 18,000 persons and each remaining fraction greater than .15. There should be no quarrel with the selection of the 18,000 [***3] figure as a norm or standard for population comparisons where a 48-vote board is the objective since, if the county were divided into 48 districts of equal population, each district would have a population of approximately 17,775 votes, according to a 1965 special census. However, the plan contains disparities which result in a substantial dilution of the voting strength of certain subdivisions when compared, on the basis of population, with that of others. For example, the Town of Greenburgh, with a population of 82,882, was assigned only four legislators and the Town of Yorktown, with a population of 22,044, was assigned one legislator, while other subdivisions having populations of less than 16,000 were each assigned one legislator. Such dilution could be avoided by a further application of the method of fractional or weighted voting utilized elsewhere in the plan. For example, the legislator from the Town of Yorktown could be assigned a 1.25 vote.

3. Since no party to the action has put it in issue, the court reaches no determination as to whether fractional or weighted voting is, per se, unconstitutional but decides only that its limited use in the plan here under consideration [***4] is unconstitutional under the circumstances and with the results here disclosed.

4. The use by the board of the population figures of a 1965 special census, rather than those of the 1960 decennial census, was not erroneous.

5. The court prescribes an interim plan to be followed pending submission by the board of a permanent reapportionment plan.

Dente v. Mt. Vernon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


50 Misc. 2d 983; 272 N.Y.S.2d 65; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1727



June 30, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner sought an order from the court declaring unconstitutional the Building Code of the City of Mount Vernon, N.Y., art. 7, § 17.03 in an action against respondent city.


OVERVIEW: The city enacted an ordinance that required the installation of a bathtub or shower and a wash basin in each water closet of every apartment such as those owned by the property owner. The property owner filed an action against the city to declare the ordinance unconstitutional. The court granted judgment in favor of the property owner because the costs to the property owner of making the renovations required under the ordinance were unreasonable. Furthermore, the court ruled that the ordinance was not a lawful exercise of police power because it did not impose upon the property owner expenses that were reasonable. Finally, the court determined that the city was not permitted to abuse its police power and that improvements required by ordinances enacted through the exercise of police power had to be reasonable, proper, and fair when considered with reference to the object to be attained.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of the property owner.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- Building Code -- installation of bathtub and washstand -- amendment of Building Code to require installation of bathtub and washstand in each of 11 apartments in plaintiff's premises is unconstitutional.

An amendment to the Building Code of the City of Mount Vernon which would require plaintiff to install, in each of the 11 apartments in his building, a bathtub or shower and a wash basin, in addition to the water closets already existing, is unconstitutional as unreasonably depriving plaintiff of his property.

 

2135 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Horton v. State


Claim No. 45812


Court of Claims of New York


50 Misc. 2d 1017; 272 N.Y.S.2d 312; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1735



June 29, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband, individually and as administrator of his wife's estate sued defendant state for the wrongful death of his wife when her vehicle was struck by a vehicle owned by the state and operated by its employee while he was backing down the highway.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the state employee was negligent in driving backwards without a clear field of vision. There was no evidence that the wife was negligent. Having decided that the husband was entitled to recover damages from the state, the court had to consider the amount of the award. The amount of damages were to be such sum as was deemed to be a fair and just compensation for the pecuniary injuries resulting from the wife's death. The amount of damages depended upon the value of the reasonable expectation of pecuniary benefits from the continuance in life by the decedent to the husband. The court held that the services rendered by a domestic and homemaker fell far short of the true loss of a wife and mother. It also took into consideration her potential earnings. The fact the vehicle had not yet been repaired did not preclude recovery for the cost of repair or reasonable rental while the car was out of service. The court also considered the present value of the dollar.


OUTCOME: The court found the state liable for the negligence of its employee and awarded various elements of recovery, including funeral expenses, ambulance service, and loss of the vehicle.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- collision with payloader -- driver for State of payloader which collided with vehicle driven by decedent was negligent in operating it backwards on highway -- in determination of damages for wrongful death of wife and mother, cost of securing substitute must be considered even though not obtained -- award of $ 60,000 made for wrongful death.

1. The driver for the State of an earth-moving piece of equipment known as a payloader was negligent in operating it backwards in a southerly direction on the highway and striking the northbound vehicle which decedent was driving, where the driver's view of the roadway ahead was blocked in part, his view was obtained by looking backwards over his shoulder, the operating manual for the vehicle did not recommend driving in reverse and the steering mechanism, when the vehicle was driven in reverse, also operated in reverse, i.e., a left turn of the wheel produced a right turn of the vehicle.

2. In the determination of damages sustained by the wrongful death of a wife and mother, the cost of securing a substitute wife and mother, even though not obtained because the family could not afford it, must be considered.

3. The [***2] factors affecting the amount of damages with respect to decedent are stated and the court awards for her death $ 60,000, plus funeral and ambulance expenses and the loss of decedent's vehicle.

 

2136 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Manhattan Club v. Landmarks Preservation Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


51 Misc. 2d 556; 273 N.Y.S.2d 848; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1749



June 23, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the application is denied and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner filed an application to review and annul a determination of respondent Landmarks Preservation Commission of the City of New York that designated a building it owned as a landmark.


OVERVIEW: The property owner held title to a building that the commission had designated as a landmark. The property owner objected because the person who sought to purchase the building wanted to tear it down. The court denied the property owner's application for review of that decision. The court stated that there was no merit in the property owner's contention that the determination was unsupported by substantial evidence. A hearing was held and the issues thoroughly aired. The architectural, historical, and aesthetic value of the improvement was fully established, and the court could not substitute its judgment for that of the commission. The court held that the property owner's contention that the designation had impaired rights vested in the existing contract of sale was not supported in the record. There was no showing that its vendee has refused, or would refuse, to take under the contract or that it had suffered or was about to suffer any loss under the contract because of the designation. The court held that the zoning law as it applied to the property owner was not confiscatory because the property owner was free to do as it pleased with the interior of the building.


OUTCOME: The court denied the property owner's application to review and annul a determination of the commission, which had designated a building it owned as a landmark, and dismissed the property owner's petition.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- Landmarks Preservation Commission of New York City -- Local Laws of 1965 (No. 46) of City of New York (adding ch. 8-A to New York City Charter) validly created Landmarks Preservation Commission -- after Manhattan Club contracted to sell its building to purchaser who planned to demolish it and erect 33-story office building, commission held hearing and validly designated building as landmark of historical or aesthetic interest -- effect of such designation is that owner may alter interior but not exterior, but, if owner cannot obtain reasonable return, commission must permit demolition or building must be acquired by purchase or eminent domain -- such local law is not confiscatory and is valid exercise of police power.

1. The General City Law ( § 20, subd. 25-a) empowers cities to provide, subject to the Constitution and the general laws of the State, for the protection and perpetuation of buildings and places of historical or aesthetic interest. By Local Law No. 46 of 1965, the City of New York added chapter 8-A to the New York City Charter. This article established a Landmarks [***2] Preservation Commission. In the city is a building which is a distinguished example of French Second Empire style of architecture and which was erected in 1859 for a lady who became the mother of the late Winston Churchill. It has been owned and occupied since 1900 by the Manhattan Club. In recent years the club has been losing members and money; and so the club contracted to sell the property for $ 600,000 to a purchaser who plans to raze this historic building and erect a 33-story office building. Thereupon the Landmarks Preservation Commission notified the seller and the buyer of a hearing, at which both appeared and opposed the commission's proposal; and then, upon substantial evidence, the commission validly designated the building as a landmark. Under the local law, the effect of such designation is that the exterior of the building cannot be altered or demolished without the commission's approval but the owner may do as it pleases with the interior, and, if the owner cannot obtain a reasonable return on its investment and the commission cannot devise a satisfactory plan for the preservation of the building, either the commission must permit demolition or the building is [***3] to be acquired by purchase or eminent domain.

2. The local law is a valid exercise of the police power. It is not confiscatory, since it does not preclude all uses for which the property is reasonably adapted. Contracts are subject to the State's police power; moreover, this seller has not shown that its contract vendee has refused to take or that it will suffer any loss.

 

2137 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Joy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Nassau County


50 Misc. 2d 690; 271 N.Y.S.2d 15; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1750



June 23, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plea of guilty is stricken, information dismissed and license restored.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, entered a guilty plea to a second offense of operating a motor vehicle while holding a junior operator's license. The court reviewed the revocation of his license.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted a statute which entitled minors to operate a motor vehicle under a junior operator's license in all counties of the state except one. N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 501(1)(b). Defendant possessed a junior license and operated a motor vehicle within the restricted county. When defendant was prosecuted for the offense, he pled guilty. Since it was defendant's second offense, the plea constituted a misdemeanor crime, rather than an offense. The court held that the legislature was entitled to enact a statute that treated minors differently if there was a rational reason to do so. The court found that although there was a rational reason for restricting minor to operating a motor vehicle under a junior license, the further restriction of not allowing such an operation within one county in the state was not reasonable because the county had no special circumstances to warrant such a restriction. The court ruled that because the statute was unreasonable, defendant could not be convicted of a violation.


OUTCOME: The court struck defendant's guilty plea, dismissed the information against him, and restored defendant's junior license.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- junior operator's license -- issuance of junior operator's license to resident of Nassau County and then prosecution of him for using it in Nassau County (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 501, subd. 1, par. b) held unreasonable and unconstitutional; information dismissed.

The Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 501, subd. 1, par. b), which provides for issuance of junior operators' licenses, is unreasonable and unconstitutional insofar as it provides that the holder of such a license cannot use it in Nassau County. A plea of guilty by a 17-year-old licensed junior operator who resides in Nassau County, to an information charging that he operated a motor vehicle in Nassau County, is recalled and stricken by a Judge of the Nassau County District Court and the information is dismissed and the license is ordered restored.

Grove v. Chemung County Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Chemung County


50 Misc. 2d 418; 270 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1810



June 6, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city, 11 towns, and a board of supervisors, filed a motion for summary judgment in the cause of action brought by plaintiffs, residents, taxpayers, and qualified voters of a county, to have the then-present representation and voting procedure of the board of supervisors of the county encompassing the 11 towns declared in violation of the Equal Protection Clauses of the U.S. and New York State Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: One town admitted the inequality of the voting in relationship to the population each supervisor represented. The court found that the disparity of population between that town and any other town or city district was unjustifiable and unreasonable. A majority of the board supervisors representing 23.9 percent of the total population of the county could, by their vote, determine the fate of legislation favored or opposed by the remaining board of supervisors members representing 76.1 percent of the total county population. The court entered a judgment declaring that the then-present districting and apportionment of the members of the county board of supervisors violated the equal protection provisions of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 11. The court also declared that N.Y. Town Law § 20(1)(a)-(b), N.Y. County Law § 150, and the Elmira City Charter §§ 2, 4 violate the same constitutional provisions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- make-up of Board of Supervisors of Chemung County whereby Supervisors representing 23.9% of total population of county control passage of legislation is unconstitutional -- Town Law ( § 20, subd. 1, pars. [a], [b]), County Law ( § 150) and Elmira City Charter (§§ 2, 4) violate Constitution in application to make-up of Board of Supervisors -- court's direction to Board of Supervisors to apportion itself fills legislative void and enables board to enact apportionment plan.

1. The Board of Supervisors of Chemung County consists of one member from each of 11 towns and 1 member from each of the 12 wards of the City of Elmira. The towns vary in population from 17,808 to 735 and each town and ward has one vote on the board. A majority of 12 supervisors representing 23.9% of the total population controls the passage of legislation. Such plan is unconstitutional.

2. When viewed in the light of the 1960 Census, Town Law ( § 20, subd. 1, pars. [a] and [b]), County Law ( § 150) and sections 2 and 4 of the Elmira City Charter violate the Federal and State constitutional guarantees of equal protection and are unconstitutional [***2] in their application to the representation on the Chemung County Board of Supervisors.

3. The redistricting of Chemung County is a legislative function to be performed by its Board of Supervisors. The Legislature has not given it the authority to do so. The direction of this court constitutes authorization for the Board of Supervisors to do all that is proper and necessary to secure a constitutional plan of supervisory representation on or before September 6, 1966.

 

2139 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Police Justice Court of New York, Rockville Centre


51 Misc. 2d 209; 273 N.Y.S.2d 181; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1808



June 6, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the complaint is denied and I find the defendant, Joseph C. Kern, guilty as charged, and I also find the defendant, Luenetta L. Miller, guilty as charged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants charged with disturbing the peace in violation of Incorporated Village of Rockville Centre, N.Y., Ordinance 1.3(b) sought dismissal of the charges against them arising out of their failure to leave a village office when requested to do so by its director and two police officers. They claimed that the court had no jurisdiction of the subject matter or of their persons and that their actions were constitutionally protected.


OVERVIEW: Defendants and 18 others went to the village's urban renewal office late on a Friday afternoon, demanded to speak to its director, handed him more than 20 complaints, and demanded that he answer immediately. The director accepted the documents, stated he could not analyze them then and that he would answer on Monday, and asked them to leave because it was closing time. The director and a police officer instructed the group to leave, and all except defendants complied; officers carried them out of the office and arrested them upon the director's instruction. The court found that N.Y. Village Law § 180 gave the court jurisdiction, that N.Y. Village Law § 338 did not require trial within five days of arrest, and that even an illegal arrest did not affect jurisdiction over the person. The court found that although a State could not criminalize peaceful expression of unpopular views, people wishing to exercise free speech could not engage in disorderly conduct, that there was no discrimination practiced against defendants, and that the director and the officers behaved properly in the situation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the complaint and found both defendants guilty as charged.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- village ordinance -- two defendants who refused to leave village office after closing hours unless their lengthy written petition was answered were found guilty of violating village ordinance against disorderly or disturbing conduct -- Police Justice Court of village did not lose jurisdiction by consensual adjournment for over five days (cf. Village Law, § 338).

1. The two defendants in this case were the only ones of a group of about 20 individuals who, after presenting a written petition or series of complaints, some 20 to 25 pages long, to the director of the urban renewal department of an incorporated village at office closing time on a Friday at 4:30 p.m., refused to accept his verbal assurance that he would study the petition and answer it on Monday, and insisted on staying in the office until he did answer. Shortly after 5:00 p.m., the director, with the assistance of village police, arrested the two defendants, and he executed informations charging the defendants [***2] with violating an ordinance of the village which provides that: "No person shall indulge in disorderly, or noisy or disturbing conduct within the Village. Any person who commits any of the following acts will be considered in violation of this provision * * * (b) acts in such manner as to annoy, disturb, interfere with, obstruct or be offensive to others". The Police Justice Court of the village (Code Crim. Pro., § 74; Village Law, § 180) tried the defendants and now finds them guilty.

2. The provision of section 338 of the Village Law against adjourning a summary disorderly conduct hearing for more than five days seems inapplicable; in any event, the adjournment for more than five days in this case was requested by defendants' counsel at the arraignment and was consented to by the prosecution. The court did not lose jurisdiction by such consensual adjournment.

 

2140 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


House of Seagram, Inc. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


51 Misc. 2d 157; 272 N.Y.S.2d 839; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1818



June 2, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition in the proceeding brought by Knickerbocker Liquors Corporation is also dismissed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the New York Liquor Authority and the New York Comptroller, moved to dismiss an action by petitioner liquor wholesalers, which sought a refund from respondents for the taxation of certain imported alcoholic beverages sold.


OVERVIEW: The liquor wholesalers purchased certain brand label liquor outside the state and resold the liquor, still packaged. Pursuant to N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 99-a(3), respondents assessed an excise tax on the brand label liquor. The liquor wholesalers requested a refund of the taxes paid from respondents and, when the request was denied, brought an action against respondents for the refund. The court found that once the liquor wholesalers resold the brand liquor, regardless of whether the liquor was still in its original packaging, the brand liquor was no longer considered an imported product. The tax was thus on the privilege of selling a particular brand of liquor in the state. Thus, the court ruled that because the taxation was proper, the liquor wholesalers were not entitled to a refund.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the liquor wholesalers' petition for a refund of taxes paid to respondents.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- fee for brand label use permit -- fee for brand label use permit under Alcoholic Beverage Control Law ( § 99-a) is not tax on imports forbidden by United States Constitution but charge for privilege of selling brand of liquor in State.

Under the Original Packages Act (U. S. Code, tit. 27, § 121), the import process as far as liquor in original packages is concerned, ceases upon the arrival in a State, and the product becomes subject to State regulation whether or not in the original package of importation. Accordingly, a fee for a liquor brand label use permit as authorized by section 99-a of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law is not in violation of clause 2 of section 10 of article I of the United State Constitution forbidding a State from laying a duty on imports. Moreover, section 99-a clearly indicates that the brand label use fee is not a tax on imports but rather a charge for the privilege of selling a particular brand of liquor in this State. Measurement of the privilege fee by the volume of brand label liquors sold is a valid [***2] exercise of regulatory power by the State, for the fee is not imposed on liquor as such but for the privilege of using a brand name. The fee does not contravene section 432 of the Tax Law which, with respect to taxes on liquor sold or used within the State, provides that no such tax shall apply to foreign or interstate commerce.

 

2141 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Allen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


51 Misc. 2d 181; 272 N.Y.S.2d 947; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1883



May 13, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, boards of education, commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking to set aside the determination of respondent, the State Commissioner of Education, refusing to make an enumeration of the inhabitants of their school districts.


OVERVIEW: The boards claimed that they were entitled to an enumeration as a matter of right and that an enumeration of less than a minimum population in each district was the only limitation on their powers to appoint district superintendents pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 1711(1) and (3). The Commissioner refused to make an enumeration. The court dismissed the boards' petitions, holding that the Commissioner was given discretionary power under § 1711(3) to veto the power of a district board to appoint a superintendent. The court found that the Commissioner's refusal to direct an enumeration was not an unreasonable exercise of his discretion because it had a rational basis to support it. The court determined that the appointment of two local superintendents over districts which did not constitute a complete school system would have resulted in no educational advantage. The court noted that the appointment would have eliminated the jurisdiction of the previously appointed superintendent over such local districts.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the boards' petitions.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- enumeration of inhabitants of school district -- Commissioner of Education has discretionary power to make enumeration of inhabitants of school district -- board of education not entitled, as matter of right, to such enumeration -- refusal of Commissioner to make requested enumeration is not without rational basis where neither of district boards involved has control over complete school system comprising kindergarten through twelfth grade.

1. Under section 1711 of the Education Law, a board of education of any union free school district may appoint a superintendent of schools if the population of the district is 4,500 or more, "which fact shall be determined by the commissioner of education". Prior to 1947 this section provided that, upon request by the district board for an enumeration of the inhabitants of the district, the Commissioner of Education "shall" cause such an enumeration to be made. In 1947 the section was amended to substitute the permissive word "may" for the mandatory word "shall". The intent and effect of that amendment was to give the Commissioner discretionary power with respect to the making of an enumeration. Accordingly, a board of education [***2] is not entitled to one as a matter of right.

2. As the basis for his refusal to make requested enumerations in certain districts, the Commissioner cites certain standards for enumerations, to be applied in determining whether a district may employ a superintendent of schools, including "Evidence of enrollment in grades K-12 exceeding 2000 pupils" and "Evidence that instruction is provided for all grades K-12". Neither of the district boards whose requests for enumerations were refused has control over a complete school system comprising kindergarten through twelfth grade. No educational advantage would result from appointing two local superintendents, responsible only to the local boards, over such districts. Accordingly, it may not be said that the Commissioner's refusal to make the requested enumerations was without a rational basis.
