State Com. for Human Rights v. Harvey Properties, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


50 Misc. 2d 672; 271 N.Y.S.2d 365; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1916



May 6, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application for a temporary injunction is denied for the reasons above stated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State Commission for Human Rights filed an application pursuant to N.Y. Exec. Law § 297 to enjoin respondent apartment owners from renting a specific apartment to anyone other than complainant.


OVERVIEW: The complainant brought a matter of discrimination before the Commission. The complainant contended that she had been denied the lease on an apartment by the apartment owners due to her race and color. The commission sought to enjoin the rental of the apartment to another party until the issue was resolved. The court found that the investigation by the Commission was potentially flawed and the evidence provided by the parties was in conflict. The court found that the grant of a temporary injunction was too severe a remedy because the apartment owner would have had no recourse as to its reputation in the event it was determined that no discrimination had taken place. The court denied the Commission's motion for injunction.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Commission's application for temporary injunction.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- discrimination in housing -- on conflicting affidavits, State Commission for Human Rights should not be granted preliminary injunction restraining landlord from renting to anyone except complainant.

On conflicting affidavits, a preliminary injunction should not be granted to the State Commission for Human Rights enjoining a landlord from renting an apartment to anyone other than the complainant, pending a final determination by the commission as to whether the landlord's refusal to rent to this complainant is indeed based on complainant's race or color (Executive Law, § 297, subd. 4). The court suggests that this matter should have been referred in the first instance to the city's Commission on Human Rights which has been formed under the General Municipal Law (art. 12-D, §§ 239-o, 239-q).

 

2143 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Heid


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


50 Misc. 2d 409; 270 N.Y.S.2d 474; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1970



April 20, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment of conviction is reversed and a new trial is ordered consistent with this opinion. Submit order on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought review of his conviction by the trial court (New York) of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1129 (a).


OVERVIEW: The driver was pulled over and charged with violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1129. The driver contended that the statute was unconstitutional and sought reversal of his conviction The court found that the statute was not unconstitutional because it lacked specific standards that would have rendered it unconstitutional. The court determined that the driver was entitled to a new trial on the matter because the trial court judge had referenced a rule of thumb in his determination of the driver's guilt that was not in the statute and was not applicable in all cases. The court reversed the driver's conviction and ordered a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the driver's conviction and ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- following too closely -- subdivision (a) of section 1129 of Vehicle and Traffic Law forbidding driver from following another vehicle too closely is constitutional -- where defendant was found guilty of violating section on basis of rule of thumb, not in statute, of one car length for each 10 miles per hour, conviction is reversed and new trial is ordered.

1. Subdivision (a) of section 1129 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law which forbids a driver from following another vehicle "more closely than is reasonable and prudent, having due regard for the speed of such vehicles and the traffic upon and the condition of the highway" is constitutional.

2. Where defendant was found guilty of violating section 1129 on the basis of a rule of thumb that the safe distance is one car length for each 10 miles per hour, which rule of thumb is not contained in the statute, defendant's conviction is reversed and a new trial ordered. Application of the rule of thumb dehors the record and without any proof to sustain it may not be a basis for conviction. The [***2] section does not prohibit one following a car closely but does make an unreasonable and improvident operation in close proximity a violation.

 

2144 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bathrick Enterprises, Inc. v. Murphy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


50 Misc. 2d 14; 269 N.Y.S.2d 305; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1987



April 15, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In plaintiff corporation's action for a declaratory judgment declaring that the receipts of coin-operated amusement devices were not subject to the tax imposed by N.Y. Tax Law art. 28, defendant state tax commission filed a motion for an order dismissing the complaint on the ground that it failed to state a cause of action and that the court did not have jurisdiction of the cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The tax commission contended that the exclusive remedies provisions in N.Y. Tax Law art. 28, § 1140 were binding on the corporation and that it was thereby precluded from bringing a declaratory judgment action. The court found that the complaint contained allegations sufficient to invoke the court's powers to render a decision on the question of whether N.Y. Tax Law art. 28 was applicable to the corporation, and treated the motion as a motion for summary judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(c). The court held that because the corporation authorized, empowered, or permitted the general public and, in particular, the patrons of the establishments where its amusement devices were located, to use its coin operated devices, provided the user inserted the proper amount of coins in consideration for such use, the corporation's operations amounted to the granting of licenses to use tangible personal property for a consideration. The court concluded that the proceeds of the corporation's coin-operated amusement devices were, therefore, taxable as retail sales under N.Y. Tax Law art. 28, § 1105(a).


OUTCOME: The court determined that the proceeds of the corporation's coin-operated amusement devices were taxable as retail sales.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- New York State sales tax -- corporation's receipts from its coin-operated amusement devices are taxable as retail sales (Tax Law, art. 28, § 1101, subd. [b], par. [5]; § 1105, subd. [a] -- action for declaratory judgment maintainable; taxpayer not necessarily restricted to article 78 CPLR proceeding.

1. The State tax on retail sales includes licenses to use tangible personal property for a consideration (Tax Law, art. 28, § 1101, subd. [b], par. [5]; § 1105, subd. [a]). Plaintiff's receipts from its coin-operated amusement devices result from such licenses and are therefore taxable as retail sales.

2. Where the jurisdiction of a taxing authority is challenged on the basis of the tax statute itself, a provision restricting the taxpayer to an article 78 CPLR proceeding (cf. Tax Law, § 1140) is inapplicable, and the taxpayer may maintain an action for a declaratory judgment.

 

2145 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Blaikie v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


49 Misc. 2d 612; 268 N.Y.S.2d 356; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2060; 53 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,481



March 24, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's motion for an injunction is denied; defendants' motions to dismiss the complaint are granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought a judgment declaring 1966 N.Y. Laws ch. 6 unconstitutional as violative of U.S. Const. art. I, § 11 and U.S. Const. art. III, § 17 and the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, and also sought injunctive relief prohibiting defendants, New York City transit Authority and the Municipal Civil Service Commission, from enforcing the legislation. Defendant mayor and intervenor attorney general moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: A transit strike was settled on the basis of paying increased salaries in contravention of the Condon-Wadlin Law, N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 108, which prohibited pay increases to striking public employees until three years from the date of their re-employment. The taxpayer challenged legislation authorizing the payments as violative of U.S. Const. art. I, § 11 and U.S. Const. art. III, § 17 and the Equal Protection Clause. A motion to dismiss by the mayor and the attorney general was granted. The court held that any resulting discrimination was not based upon race, color, creed, or religion, and that public employees were not deprived of their privileges and immunities as citizens by the penalties of the Condon-Wadlin Law being selectively applied to public employees. The court held that the distinction was reasonable, and that public employees were not denied equal protection of the laws by virtue of the legislature's choice to restrict a small group, where it could have applied the legislation to an entire class of workers, provided the distinction was not unreasonable or arbitrary. Taxpayer status, absent direct injury, did not confer standing on the taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's motion for an injunction and granted defendants' motion to dismiss the taxpayer's complaint challenging the constitutionality of legislation authorizing the payments of increased salary to striking public employees.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- strikes by public employees -- statute (L. 1966, ch. 6) authorizing New York City Transit Authority and another public benefit corporation to pay increased compensation to employees who struck illegally (Civil Service Law, § 108) is constitutional -- whatever privilege or immunity is granted is not to private persons or associations but is to public benefit corporations, and in any event, is not exclusive of any possible similar grant to anyone else -- moreover, action to adjudge statute unconstitutional and to enjoin public officials cannot be maintained by citizen-taxpayer in absence of any direct personal or property interest.

1. The Civil Service Law ( § 108 ["Condon-Wadlin Act"]) prohibits strikes by public employees, including employees of any public authority, and provides that a striking employee abandons his employment but may be re-employed and, if re-employed, his compensation shall not exceed what it was before he struck and shall not be increased for three years. Any taxpayer may bring suit to declare illegal and to restrain payment [***2] of compensation in violation of any provision of the Civil Service Law and to recover from appointing and fiscal officials any sums thus illegally paid (Civil Service Law, § 102, subds. 1, 2). Chapter 6 of the Laws of 1966, enacted and effective February 16, 1966, declares and finds (§ 1) that the recent strike of the employees of the New York City Transit Authority and of the Manhattan and Bronx Surface Transit Operating Authority brought severe economic hardship and chaos to some 16,000,000 persons in the New York City metropolitan region, that the strike has been settled on the terms recommended by the mediation panel and the strikers have returned to their jobs, and that this act of the Legislature is necessary in order to protect the health, safety and welfare of said 16,000,000 people. Said chapter 6 thereupon provides (§ 2) that, notwithstanding any provisions of law to the contrary, said two public authorities are hereby authorized and empowered to continue their employees in the same status as before the strike and to pay them the increased compensation pursuant to which they resumed their duties. Said chapter 6 of the Laws of 1966 is valid and constitutional.

2. Chapter [***3] 6 of the Laws of 1966 does not discriminate against any person because of his race, color or creed, or deny him the equal protection of the laws, or abridge his privileges and immunities (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11); nor is this a law "Granting to any private corporation, association or individual any exclusive privilege, immunity or franchise whatever" (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17). Whatever this statute grants is to two public benefit corporations (Public Authorities Law, §§ 1201, 1203-a); and, in any event, it is not exclusive of any similar grant which might be made to anyone else.

3. Moreover, neither at common law nor under the General Municipal Law ( § 51), may this action, to declare a State statute unconstitutional and to enjoin city officials and public authorities from carrying out its provisions, be maintained by a citizen-taxpayer on behalf of all others similarly situated, in the absence of a showing of a direct personal or property interest.

4. Accordingly, the complaint is dismissed.

 

2146 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dennison v. State


Claim No. 38868


Court of Claims of New York


49 Misc. 2d 533; 267 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2099



March 15, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant individual filed an action for false imprisonment against defendant State for his allegedly unlawful confinement to a state hospital. The State filed a motion for dismissal.


OVERVIEW: At the age of 16 in 1925, the individual pled guilty to burglary in the third degree for breaking into a roadside stand and stealing about $ 5 worth of candy. Although he was given a suspended sentence, he violated his terms of probation by failing to report monthly to a minister. He was then sentenced to a reformatory. After observation at the reformatory, he was classified as a low-grade moron and transferred to an institution for male delinquents. Save for a brief release, he was confined until 1936. He was then transferred upon a "Certificate of Lunacy" to the state hospital, which was a prison with facilities for controlling psychotic convicts, where he remained until 1960. The court noted that his criminal sentence expired in 1936. The court found that the individual was held over at the hospital pursuant to N.Y. Correction Law § 384, under an order that had been obtained without notice. The court also found that independent tests administered by three highly qualified psychologists subsequent to his discharge conclusively established that he possessed average intelligence and could not possibly have been a low-grade moron at some other point in his life.


OUTCOME: The court denied the State's motion and awarded the individual $ 115,000.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] False imprisonment -- unlawful confinement beyond expiration of prison sentence -- claimant was unlawfully detained in Dannemora State Hospital for 24 years after expiration of prison term -- award of $ 115,000 made.

1. Claimant upon the expiration of his sentence upon conviction for burglary in the third degree, arising from stealing, when he was 16 years of age, $ 5 worth of candy, was held over at Dannemora State Hospital from September 17, 1936 to December 16, 1960 pursuant to section 384 of the Correction Law, under color of an order obtained without notice. Such confinement was illegal. Moreover, even if notice had been given, the statutory procedure utilized by the State in continuing his confinement is unconstitutional ( Baxstrom v. Herold, 383 U.S. 107).

2. Early in his confinement, and in 1927, he was classified as a low-grade moron. In 1936, when he was transferred to Dannemora State Hospital, he was certified as insane. At the time his criminal sentence expired he had recovered from his insanity. Subsequent to his discharge from the hospital, three psychologists determined by independent tests that claimant possessed average intelligence and that he [***2] could not possibly have been a low-grade moron at any time in his life. For the 24 years of confinement, and taking into consideration the economic loss as well as the years of degradation, humiliation and frustration which claimant experienced, an award of $ 115,000 is made.

 

2147 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Crichton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Westchester County


49 Misc. 2d 405; 267 N.Y.S.2d 706; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2116



March 9, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The request for injunctive relief is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner executor conducted an accounting proceeding of the decedent's estate. Movants, decedent's daughter (daughter) and former wife (wife), filed objections to the accounting. The daughter brought a motion for partial summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The daughter sought summary judgment in connection with her objections, seeking to disallow the wife's claim to community property rights to decedent's property located in Louisiana. The wife's claim to such was based on La. Civ. Cod. art. 2400, which allowed non-resident married persons who acquired property within the state of Louisiana to be subject to the laws that regulate community property in Louisiana. The daughter argued that since the wife was omitted from the decedent's will, her interest in his estate was limited to the statutory right of election. The court determined that since the property that the wife was claiming was movable personal property, the laws of New York applied because the parties were married and lived in New York during their marriage. Therefore, the wife acquired no community property rights in personal property of the decedent.


OUTCOME: The court granted the daughter's motion for summary judgment, sustained the daughter's objections, dismissed the wife's objections, and denied the daughter's motion for injunctive relief in the executrix' accounting proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Descent and distribution -- foreign community property laws -- widow of New York domiciliary acquired no community property rights under Louisiana law in intangible personal property kept in Louisiana -- question properly before court of domicile -- court gives no effect to Louisiana's attempt to apply its law -- court does not enjoin widow and executrix from pressing claim in Louisiana.

1. The widow of a New York domiciliary who kept intangible assets consisting of stocks, bonds and bank accounts in Louisiana for the purpose of investment acquired no community property rights in such assets under Louisiana law. The law of New York, as the State of the matrimonial domicile, the State of the domicile of the spouse first acquiring the property and the State of the most significant contacts, governs ownership of such property. Louisiana law does not apply.

2. The question is properly before this court as the court of domicile.

3. This court will not give effect to, and will not be bound by, Louisiana's attempt to apply its community property laws to the personal property of persons not domiciled in that State.

4. The court [***2] will not enjoin the widow from pressing her claim in the Louisiana courts and the executrix from abetting her. Triable issues of fact exist as to whether the foreign suit was brought to evade domiciliary law or in an attempt to evade the public policy of this State.

 

2148 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Yolen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


49 Misc. 2d 470; 267 N.Y.S.2d 925; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2151



February 28, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly the charge here under consideration is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was charged with violation of New York City, N.Y., Dept. of Parks, art. II, § 6 for flying a kite, advocating the election of a candidate as mayor of New York City, sought to have the charge dismissed, claiming § 6 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant admitted to flying a kite in a public park in New York City for political purposes. He argued that his acts were protected by U.S. Const. amend. I and XIV. The court agreed with defendant and held that New York City, N.Y., Dept. of Parks, R. and Regs. art. II, § 6 was unconstitutional in that it did not fall within the proper exercise of police power because its prohibitions were too broad. The court noted that § 6 sought to restrict the flying of kites for the purpose of expressing political preferences. The court further discounted the State's argument that the flying of kites raised safety problems that were not involved in the distribution of political literature. The court found that the liberties of the press were not confined to newspapers, periodicals, pamphlets, and leaflets and that the wording of § 6 was so vague that it was impracticable of enforcement or obedience. Additionally, the court held that unless the method used was manifestly offensive or there was some substantial evidence that it interfered with the health, safety, comfort, or convenience of the general public, then the State or its political subdivisions were not able to interfere.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charge against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- flying kites with political advertising in parks -- prohibition against flying kites containing political advertising in parks is unconstitutional.

Section 6 of article II of the Rules and Regulations of the Department of Parks of the City of New York which prohibits the display within any public park of a kite containing political advertising shows no clear and substantial connection with the lawful objective of providing for the safety, comfort and convenience of the people in their use of the parks, is too broad in its prohibitions, does not fall within a proper exercise of police power and is unconstitutional.

 

2149 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Berman v. Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


49 Misc. 2d 713; 268 N.Y.S.2d 125; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2159



February 25, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is accordingly granted and the complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendantelection officials filed a motion requesting that the trial court (New York) dismiss plaintiff voter's claim for damages for designating a church as a place to register and vote.


OVERVIEW: The election officials designated a church as a place to register in a political party and vote in general elections. The voter, who belonged to a different religion, objected to the designation and claimed that it prohibited him from registered in a party, though he did vote in an election at the church. The voter filed suit, claiming that the designation was a violation of the principle of separation of church and state. The election officials sought dismissal on the grounds of legal insufficiency. On review of the motion, the court found that the voter's complaint was not couched in terms proper to raise the question of the separation of church and state and granted the election officials' request for relief.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- church as polling place -- Board of Elections may designate church as polling place (Election Law, § 66); voter's complaint seeking damages dismissed.

Among the places which may be designated by Boards of Elections for registration and voting are churches (Election Law, § 66). Plaintiff, a voter, alleges that the defendants, the members of a Board of Elections, in so designating a church, violated the constitutional principle of separation of church and State and were guilty of malice and culpable negligence, wherefore he seeks $ 100,000 damages. A court of original jurisdiction will not assume the statute to be unconstitutional. Nor does plaintiff offer any substantiation as to malice or as to damages. Defendants' motion for dismissal of the complaint is granted.

 

2150 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Trammell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, Erie County


50 Misc. 2d 179; 267 N.Y.S.2d 434; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2191



February 15, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a demurrer to a two-count indictment, which charged defendant, along with two others, with the crime of conspiracy, and one of the other defendants with the crime of perjury.


OVERVIEW: The State issued an indictment charging defendant and two others with conspiracy, and one of the other defendants with perjury. The State alleged that defendants conspired in their failure to disclose to the city council certain facts in order to bring about the purchase of certain real estate by the city at a price substantially higher than the owner's asking price. The State contended that defendants conspired to keep the city ignorant of the true asking price in order to profit from the city's purchase of the property at the greater price. Defendant filed a demurrer to the indictment. The court disallowed the demurrer, holding that the conspiracy count did not charge more than one crime where it accused him of engaging in a conspiracy in violation of several provisions of the penal law. The gist of the crime was not the act of doing the things that violated the several sections of the penal code; rather, the crime was the agreement to do them, which constituted a single act. Further, the indictment stated a crime against him, even though the duties of his office as Director of Real Estate were not specified, because it stated his role as a member of the alleged conspiracy.


OUTCOME: The court disallowed defendant's demurrer to the two count indictment charging defendant and two others with the crime of conspiracy, and charging one of the other defendants with the crime of perjury.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- indictment -- demurrer -- count of indictment charging defendant city official and others with conspiracy does not charge more than one crime because overt acts set forth in various subdivisions of section 580 of Penal Law are stated to have been done -- collateral crimes stated to have been committed are not charged in indictment but only single crime of conspiracy -- indictment not defective because city official's duties are not detailed -- overt acts alleged are not barred by Statute of Limitations -- by requiring city official to sign waiver of immunity before testifying before Grand Jury as condition to retaining his job, no constitutional rights are violated -- count of perjury against codefendant not severed; not prejudicial.

1. A count of an indictment charges moving defendant, a city official, and two others with conspiracy in that they planned to bring about the purchase of certain real estate by a city at an exaggerated price and to divide the profits among them. More than one crime is not charged because the acts set forth in several subdivisions of section 580 of the Penal Law are stated to have been done. The gist of the crime is not the doing of [***2] the things set forth in the subdivisions of section 580 but in the agreement to do any of them. The commission of one or more of the items prohibited establishes but one crime of conspiracy.

2. A conspiracy to commit a crime is an independent crime, distinct from the collateral crime or crimes contemplated and stated to have been committed in the indictment. The indictment does not charge the implementary substantive crimes mentioned therein and it does not therefore charge more than one crime.

3. The indictment is not defective because it fails to allege, in detail, the duties defendant as a city official was required to do and which he willfully neglected.

4. An objection that the named overt acts are barred by the Statute of Limitations is overruled.

5. The State has power to impose conditions which must be accepted if one is to be a public officer. When defendant executed the waiver of immunity before testifying before the Grand Jury in order to retain his job, he was not deprived of any constitutional rights. There was no compulsion. The provisions of the State Constitution requiring the execution of a waiver of immunity by a public officer as a condition to testifying [***3] or losing his public office does not violate the Fifth Amendment, providing that no person shall be compelled in a criminal case to be a witness against himself, or the Fourteenth Amendment.

6. A count of perjury in the indictment against one of the codefendants should not be severed. Its presence in the indictment is not prejudicial to moving defendant.

 

2151 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Weinstein v. New York City Transit Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


49 Misc. 2d 170; 267 N.Y.S.2d 111; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2209; 61 L.R.R.M. 2323; 53 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,443



February 9, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a resident of the City of New York, on behalf of himself and others similarly situated, sought an order restraining, prohibiting and enjoining as illegal the offering or paying by respondents, the New York City Transit Authority and the City of New York, to those employees of the Transit Authority that had participated in a strike, any amount in excess of their compensation. Respondents filed a cross-motion to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: Transit Authority employees went on strike, which brought the city's transportation facilities to a halt on January 1. Operations resumed after January 13. Petitioner sought an order enjoining respondents from paying the strikers any amount in excess of their compensation in effect prior to the strike for at least three years after resumption of their reemployment. Petitioner alleged that such payments would have been illegal under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 108. Respondents filed a cross-motion before their answer to dismiss the petition. The court denied both parties' motions, holding that petitioner's action properly named the City and its officials as parties to the action because there was an interrelation between the City and the Transit Authority under New York law. Moreover, petitioner, as a private citizen, had standing to bring a mandamus action because respondents' conduct allegedly violated New York's Civil Service laws, which constituted both misfeasance and malfeasance. The court ruled that misfeasance and malfeasance were proper subjects for the maintenance of a mandamus action by a private citizen.


OUTCOME: The court denied both parties' motions in petitioner's action to enjoin respondents from paying Transit Authority workers that had participated in a strike any amount in excess of their compensation.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- strikes by public employees -- motion of New York City Transit Authority and City of New York and its Mayor, Comptroller and City Civil Service Commission, to dismiss petition of citizen and resident of New York City for order restraining, for three years, payment of any increased compensation to employees of New York City Transit Authority as result of their illegal strike, denied -- Condon-Wadlin Law (Civil Service Law, § 108) is constitutional -- Civil Service Law ( §§ 95-108) provides for civil and criminal proceedings against any public officer who authorizes or pays compensation in violation of Civil Service Law.

1. Petitioner, on behalf of himself and all other citizens of New York City similarly situated, asked for an order restraining and enjoining, for three years, the New York City Transit Authority and the City of New York and the Mayor and the Comptroller of the city and the City Civil Service Commission from authorizing and/or paying any increased compensation to the 30,000 employees of said New York City Transit Authority as a result of an illegal strike of said employees (Civil Service Law, § 108 [Condon-Wadlin Law]; Civil Service Law, art.  [***2] 7, §§ 95, 97, 100, 101, 102; Public Authorities Law, tit. 9, §§ 1201-1210). Said respondents moved to dismiss the petition, upon their objections in point of law, including the objections (a) that the Civil Service Law ( § 108) was amended (by L. 1963, ch. 702) so as to authorize a review proceeding under CPLR article 78 and that said amendment expired on July 1, 1965; (b) that the Civil Service Law ( § 102) provides for a plenary "taxpayer's action", not a proceeding under CPLR article 78; and (c) that this proceeding was premature in any event, absent any finding of fact that any particular employee participated in the strike. Respondents' motion for dismissal of the petition was denied and they were permitted to serve and file answers ( CPLR 7804, subd. [f]), including the defense by answers or otherwise, as they might be advised, that the effect of the amendment (L. 1963, ch. 702) was to repeal the penalty provisions of said section 108 of the Civil Service Law.

2. The Condon-Wadlin Law (Civil Service Law, § 108) has been held constitutional in other cases.

3. The Condon-Wadlin Law (Civil Service Law, § 108) prohibits strikes by public employees, and provides that an employee [***3] who strikes thereby abandons his position, and that he may be reappointed but at no higher pay, and that his pay cannot be increased until after three years, and that for five years he is subject to summary dismissal. The section further provides that any employee who asserts that he did not participate in the strike may within 10 days demand that a proceeding be commenced to determine that he did not violate the statute. The statute is made part of article 7 ( §§ 95-108) of the Civil Service Law, which is concerned with enforcement, prohibitions, penalties, certification of payrolls, duties of public officers, and civil and criminal proceedings against public officers who pay any compensation in contravention of the Civil Service Law. Plainly, the respondents are forbidden for three years from authorizing or paying any increase of compensation to the strikers.

 

2152 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Walus v. Millington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Oneida County


49 Misc. 2d 104; 266 N.Y.S.2d 833; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2227



February 4, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners filed an action for a declaratory judgment and injunction, seeking to declare unconstitutional a zoning ordinance passed by a town board that rezoned property belonging to defendant property owner and since conveyed to defendant realty corporation. The amendment reclassified the property from R-1, One-Family Residence District to B-General Business District.


OVERVIEW: An application was made to rezone the property to allow construction of a restaurant. In due course, the application was referred to the Zoning Board of Appeals for consideration and public hearing and thereafter referred back to the town board with the unanimous approval of the Zoning Board of Appeals, subject to certain conditions. The Planning Board recommended that the entire tract be rezoned "Planned Business District." A public hearing was held and the ordinance amending the existing zoning ordinance and rezoning the premises by placing it in a B-General Business District was passed. Plaintiffs then challenged the amendment as "spot zoning."The court agreed with plaintiffs and held that it was not the presently intended use but the many permitted uses authorized by the amendment for the vacant land that had to be judged. There was nothing in the record giving assurance that the land would be used solely for a restaurant and motel. Unless all the permitted uses are in harmony with the character of the neighborhood and the general community program, the amendment was not a part of a comprehensive plan, because all of the uses could be legally enjoyed.


OUTCOME: The court found that the zoning ordinance amendment was void, and defendants were restrained from acting pursuant to it.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- spot zoning invalidated -- restriction agreement between owner and his grantee insufficient to carry out recommendations of Planning Board.

A zoning ordinance amendment which is not based on a comprehensive plan for the welfare of the whole community (Town Law, §§ 261, 263), but which, instead, singles out a portion of a prime residential area and rezones it, apparently for the sole benefit of the owner of this rezoned spot, for any of the numerous uses which are permitted in a business zone, is an unreasonable exercise of the police power and is void. This property owner wished to build, on the main highway frontage, a building for a well-known chain of restaurants and motels. Instead, the broad rezoning of this strip permitted him to use it for any of the uses permitted in a business zone, such as gasoline service stations or several dozen different businesses and incidental manufacturing processes, or even possibly for trailer parks. The Planning Board and the Zoning Board of Appeals had recommended that definite conditions and restrictions be imposed by the town; but, instead, a setback agreement was made between the owner and his [***2] grantee corporation, which contained some general verbiage about "existing trees and natural beauty" and nothing about restricting the use to a restaurant and motel, and which in any event was unrecorded and was to last only three years or until some new zoning ordinance of unspecified character might be adopted. The changes which had occurred in this area since the adoption of the original zoning ordinance had not been of a character to downgrade the area. The rezoning of this spot discriminated against the rest of the area and against the general welfare of the whole town. The owner and his grantee and the Town Board are restrained from acting pursuant to this void amendment.

 

2153 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Martin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


49 Misc. 2d 268; 267 N.Y.S.2d 404; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2230



February 3, 1966 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument February 24, 1966.



DISPOSITION: The motion for reargument or modification is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant doctor filed a motion to suppress the evidence obtained as the result of an illegal search and seizure in the state's 27-count indictment against him of the crime of abortion. The doctor asserted that his appointment pad, directory of patients, medical records and records of incoming calls were "mere evidence" not constitutionally the subject of a search under the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: A doctor was charged with the crime of abortion after the district attorney's office secured his files and reviewed the files to support the charges. The search warrant which was issued was based on a detective affidavit following a statement given to him by a woman confined in the hospital. The search of the doctor's office was for information connected with his performance of illegal abortions. Two filed cabinets were seized, reviewed, and then returned before the indictment was returned against the doctor. The doctor asserted that the items seized were not constitutionally the subject of a search under the Fourth Amendment. The court held that the doctor was entitled to an order suppressing all evidence and evidentiary leads obtained or derived from the unreasonable search and seizure and intended to be used in support of counts 2 to 27 inclusive, that the exclusionary rule covered the fruits as well as the evidence obtained by an unlawful search, and that the state could proceed only on the basis of knowledge of the facts shown to have been gained from independent and untainted sources. The court denied the state's motion for reargument or modification.


OUTCOME: The motion for reargument or modification of the order granting suppression of evidence obtained as the result of an illegal search and seizure in an indictment against a doctor for abortion was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- subdivision 4 of section 792 of Code of Criminal Procedure permitting seizure of "property constituting evidence of crime" does not violate constitutional prohibition against unreasonable searches and seizures -- use of subdivision 4 to authorize exploratory search of individual's private papers for possible evidence of crime is impermissible; defendant entitled to order suppressing private papers seized.

1. Subdivision 4 of section 792 of the Code of Criminal Procedure which provides that "property constituting evidence of crime" may be seized under a search warrant does not, per se, violate the prohibition of the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution against unreasonable searches and seizures and is valid.

2. The use of subdivision 4 to authorize a general and exploratory search of an individual's private papers for possible evidence of crime or leads to obtain such evidence is constitutionally impermissible. The warrant here authorizing such general search not pertaining to a named patient of defendant physician charged with [***2] abortion was issued without probable cause. Defendant is entitled to an order suppressing all evidence and evidentiary leads obtained or derived from such unreasonable search and seizure and intended to be used in support of counts 2 to 27 inclusive of the indictment.

 

2154 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bookcase, Inc. v. Broderick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


49 Misc. 2d 355; 267 N.Y.S.2d 415; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2238



February 1, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment is denied and defendants' cross motion for summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bookseller filed a motion for summary judgment in a declaratory judgment action by the bookseller against defendant, the Commissioner of Police of the City of New York, to declare that N.Y. Penal Law §§ 484-h and 484-i, which made the sale of sexually offensive literature to persons under the age of 17 and 18 unlawful, were unconstitutional. The Commissioner filed a cross motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Penal Law §§ 484-h and 484-i made the sale of sexually offensive literature to persons under the age of 17 and 18 unlawful. The bookseller was threatened with prosecution for violations of §§ 484-h and 484-i. The bookseller filed an action to declare the statutes unconstitutional on the ground that obscenity was the sole test of constitutionality and that variable standards of obscenity could not be applied to adults and minors. The bookseller filed a motion for summary judgment. The Commissioner filed a cross-motion for summary judgment. The court found that the bookseller's challenge to the constitutionality of the statutes was without merit. The court ruled that the State of New York had a legitimate interest in preventing the dissemination of material deemed harmful to children and that it was not unconstitutional to prohibit the dissemination of material that was not obscene in the hands of adults but that could be harmful to children because the bookseller's constitutional right to make a profit could not take precedence over the legitimate and exigent power of the state to protect the welfare of its youth.


OUTCOME: The court denied the bookseller's motion for summary judgment. The court granted the Commissioner's cross-motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sale of book to minor -- in action to have declared unconstitutional statutes forbidding sale of sexually offensive literature to persons under ages of 17 and 18 (Penal Law, §§ 484-h, 484-i), plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment is denied -- State has right to forbid sale of books on basis of age of purchaser, flowing from its power to protect welfare of youth.

1. In an action to have adjudged invalid and unconstitutional sections 484-h and 484-i of the Penal Law, forbidding the sale of sexually offensive literature to persons under 17 and 18 years of age, respectively, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment on the ground that there cannot be variable standards of obscenity, one applying to adults and the other to minors, is denied. The State has the right to forbid the sale of books on the basis of the age of the purchaser, flowing from its power to protect the welfare of its youth.

2. Defendants' cross motion for summary judgment dismissing the action is granted.

 

2155 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dibble


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Herkimer County


49 Misc. 2d 545; 267 N.Y.S.2d 846; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2283



January 20, 1966 



DISPOSITION: The appeal is, therefore, denied and the conviction complained of is hereby affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Special Sessions, Herkimer County (New York), which convicted him being a vagrant in violation of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 887(4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted that he was denied his right to a trial by jury and that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§887 and 891-a were unconstitutional and void. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that the original New York Constitution and its amendments did not guarantee a trial by jury in Courts of Special Sessions. Each subsequent amendment to the Constitution carried the same omnibus provision for the trial by jury to remain inviolate, but in each of these instances, the framers had in mind the trial by 12 individuals. Under all of the circumstances, the alleged constitutional attack was not warranted by law. Vagrancy cases had been tried for years in which both men and women had been involved in the Magistrates' Courts of the City of New York and in other courts without a jury, and it had long been recognized that this was not a violation of the Constitution, nor the violation of the constitutional rights of any defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- vagrancy -- conviction of vagrancy and imposition of reformatory sentence, by Justice of Peace without jury, constitutional (Code Crim. Pro., § 887, subd. 4; § 891-a).

The Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 887, subd. 4; § 891-a) defines a certain type of vagrant and provides for a reformatory sentence. A conviction thereunder, by a Court of Special Sessions held by a Justice of the Peace without a jury, is constitutional, and the sentence is not excessive (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2; Civil Rights Law, § 12; Penal Law, § 2). The judgment is affirmed by the County Court.

 

2156 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Nardone v. Ryan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


49 Misc. 2d 93; 266 N.Y.S.2d 847; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2296



January 17, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners filed an action against defendants, village board of trustees and related parties, seeking a declaration determining the landowners' rights in a zoning proceeding. The landowners filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The landowners brought an action for a declaratory judgment to determine their rights in connection with proceedings taken by the village in considering and voting upon a resolution having as its purpose amendment of its Zoning Ordinance. The landowners contended that under Village of Hempstead, N.Y., Village Law § 179 (1956), the resolution approving the requested zoning change was effective when adopted by a vote of a three-fourths majority. The landowners claimed that, to the extent Village of Hempstead, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance § 2004 was inconsistent with the Village Law, it was invalid, and, since the resolution was adopted as provided in the Village Law, it was effective. The village contended that the passage of the zoning resolution required an unanimous vote by the board because over 20 percent of the owners of the area affected submitted written protests. The court held that Village Law, §179 (1) governed the voting requirements for a change in its zoning ordinance by the board. The resolution amending the Zoning Ordinance was validly adopted and the court directed the village to implement the resolution.


OUTCOME: The court granted the landowners motion for summary judgment and directed the village to implement the zoning ordinance that was validly adopted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- amendment of Village Law ( § 179) requiring only three-fourths vote of trustees instead of unanimous vote, in order to adopt proposed zoning amendment which is opposed by owners of 20% or more of affected area, superseded theretofore existing and unamended provision of village's zoning ordinance requiring unanimous vote of board.

A proposed village zoning ordinance amendment, if opposed by the owners of 20% or more of the affected area, cannot validly be adopted "except by the favorable vote of two-thirds of the members of the board of trustees in villages having three members on the board of trustees and three-fourths of the members of the board of trustees in all the other villages" (Village Law, § 179). The quoted words formerly read "except by the favorable vote of all the members of the board of trustees", but were changed in 1956 to read as above. Such an amendment of the Village Law controls and supersedes any inconsistent provision of a village's zoning ordinance, such as the theretofore existing and unchanged provision of the Village of Hempstead's Zoning Ordinance (§ 2004) requiring a unanimous vote. Accordingly, an amendment [***2] adopted in 1965 by the village's five-man Board of Trustees by a vote of four to one -- that is, by more than "three-fourths of the members" -- was validly adopted, and the court so adjudges.

 

2157 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dona v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, St. Lawrence County


48 Misc. 2d 876; 266 N.Y.S.2d 229; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2298



January 17, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers brought an action for a declaration that the present apportionment of defendant Board of Supervisors of St. Lawrence County (board) was unconstitutional because it failed to adhere to the judicially mandated principle of one person, one vote.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the apportionment of the board was invalid and violated the principle of one person, one vote because 20 percent of the population controlled the voting of the entire population. The court declared that the present apportionment of the board was unconstitutional and granted the taxpayers summary judgment because there was no issue of fact in dispute. The court also declared N.Y. County Law § 150 and N.Y. Town Law §§ 29(14), 41(7) unconstitutional because they constituted the law dealing with the election and the makeup of the board. The court ordered the board to adopt a constitutional plan of apportionment by a certain date and directed that if such a plan was not timely completed, that a hearing be held to determine whether weighted voting could be applied.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the taxpayers in their action to declare the board's present apportionment unconstitutional. The court also declared unconstitutional certain statutes relating to election of the board's members. The court directed the board to adopt a constitutional plan of apportionment by a specific date.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- Board of 36 Supervisors of St. Lawrence County, one of whom represents 87 people and another of whom represents 17,750 people, is unconstitutionally organized -- present Board of Supervisors is directed to adopt constitutional plan of representation no later than July 1, 1966, and their present terms of office shall expire on December 31, 1966 -- students at colleges, and patients in State hospital, are to be counted at their respective homes -- population includes minors.

1. The Board of Supervisors of the County of St. Lawrence consists of 36 Supervisors -- one from each of the 32 towns, and a total of 4 from the 4 wards of the City of Ogdensburg. The towns vary in population from 87 to 17,750, yet each town has only one vote on the board. Approximately 20% of the population of the county controls the board. Insofar as this is authorized by law ( County Law, § 150; Town Law, § 29, subd. 14; § 41, subd. 7) it is unconstitutional as violating the basic principle of one person, one vote.

2. Summary judgment is granted directing the Board of Supervisors to adopt a constitutional plan of apportionment no later [***2] than July 1, 1966; and directing that the present terms of office of all Supervisors shall terminate on December 31, 1966; and directing that, if a valid plan be not completed by July 1, 1966, a hearing shall be held to determine whether weighted voting can be applied to the board of this county.

3. The New York State Constitution (art. II, § 4) provides that "no person shall be deemed to have gained or lost a residence, by reason of his presence or absence * * * while a student * * * nor while kept at any * * * asylum". Therefore, students at the four colleges in the county, and patients at the State hospital in the county, are to be counted at their respective homes.

4. The action is not premature. Action by the State is not a condition precedent to its commencement.

 

2158 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Greenburgh v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


49 Misc. 2d 116; 266 N.Y.S.2d 998; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2332



January 6, 1966 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voters sought a declaration that certain provisions of the county and town charters, which established the membership of defendant Board of Supervisors were unconstitutional under the New York Constitution and the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The voters claimed that the method for establishing the membership of the Board was unconstitutional. The court agreed. The court found that the method for establishing membership of the Board was unconstitutional because it violated the one person, one vote principle. The court found that the voters established their grievance because they showed that the weight of their votes had been substantially diluted when compared with the votes of those living in other municipalities in the county. However, the court declined to fashion a remedy to this problem. The court found that the legislature was the proper body to fashion a remedy to this constitutional infirmity. Therefore, the court retained jurisdiction over the matter pending the action of the legislature to develop a constitutional method for establishing membership on the Board.


OUTCOME: The court found that the provisions in question were unconstitutional but declined to fashion a remedy until the legislative bodies in question had the opportunity to remedy the constitutional infirmity.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- where 40% of population elects majority of members of Board of Supervisors, board is unconstitutionally organized -- existence of political remedies is not bar to judicial relief -- charters of six cities within county are not unconstitutional -- Town Law, insofar as it provides that each town shall have one supervisor, is not invalid -- provisions of Westchester County Charter and Administrative Code are unconstitutional -- other judicial relief denied for present.

1. Where a majority of the Board of Supervisors of Westchester County can be elected by the voters in towns and cities which have less than 40% of the population of the county, and legislation can be enacted or defeated by the supervisors so elected, the present apportionment or districting of the Board of Supervisors is unconstitutional as violating the principle of one person, one vote.

2. That plaintiffs may invoke political remedies which will give them adequate relief is not a bar to judicial relief.

3. The City Charters of the Cities of Mount Vernon, New Rochelle, Peekskill, Rye, White Plains and Yonkers to the extent that they require the election [***2] of the number of supervisors specified therein, without regard to population, are not unconstitutional. The supervisors elected in the cities, although automatically becoming members of the County Board of Supervisors are not elected as such but as city officers, exercising their voting power as supervisors under other general and other special or local laws.

4. The Town Law is not invalid insofar as it provides that each town in Westchester County shall have a supervisor. Inequality in representation, as between cities and towns, results from the operation of other statutes.

5. Provisions of the Westchester County Charter and of its Administrative Code, insofar as they require or authorize the continuance of the Westchester County Board of Supervisors as presently constituted and as its voting power is presently apportioned are unconstitutional.

6. Plaintiffs' demands for other relief are denied. Legislative apportionment is primarily a matter for legislative consideration and judicial relief becomes appropriate only when a legislative body fails to reapportion according to constitutional requisites in due time after having had an adequate opportunity to do so. However, the [***3] court retains jurisdiction of the action for all purposes with the right to any party to seek further relief.

Park Ave. Clinical Hospital v. Kramer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


48 Misc. 2d 826; 266 N.Y.S.2d 147; 1966 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2343; 53 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,448



January 3, 1966 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion for judgment in favor of the defendants is granted and the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, State Labor Relations Board and its members (Board), filed a motion for summary judgment seeking dismissal of plaintiff nonprofit hospital's complaint for a declaratory judgment adjudging that N.Y. Lab. Law §§ 701, 713, 715 and 716 were unconstitutional and void with respect to the hospital.


OVERVIEW: The union filed with the Board a petition, pursuant to N.Y. Lab. Law § 705, asking to certified as a representative for the purpose of collective bargaining for a unit at the nonprofit hospital. The hospital filed an action against the Board, seeking a declaration that N.Y. Lab. Law §§ 701, 713, 715 and 716 were unconstitutional and void with respect to the hospital. The Board filed a motion for summary judgment. The hospital contended that the sections of the N.Y. Labor Relations Act in question were unconstitutional for the reason that charitable hospitals were the only class of employers subjected to the sections, The court found that the hospital failed to show that the classification was arbitrary and that a classification between a private proprietary hospital and a nonprofit hospital was valid. The court found that §§ 701, 713, 715 and 716 were constitutional and applied to the nonprofit hospital.


OUTCOME: The motion for judgment in favor of the Board was granted and the complaint was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Hospitals -- labor relations -- nonprofit hospitals and residential care centers are subject to special and constitutionally valid enactments which ban strikes and picketing and which empower Industrial Commissioner to compel arbitration; implied standards governing arbitrators are fairness and reasonableness.

Proprietary hospitals, like other employers, are subject to the usual provisions of the State Labor Relations Act (Labor Law, art. 20); but nonprofit or charitable hospitals and residential care centers are subject to special and constitutionally valid provisions which ban strikes, lockouts and picketing, and which make arbitration compulsory when so directed by the Industrial Commissioner after a mediation board has certified that it has been unable to effect a voluntary settlement of a dispute concerning the negotiation of terms and conditions of employment (Labor Law, §§ 701, 705, 713, 715, 716; N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 11, 17; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.). The standards which shall govern the arbitrators are implied and are constitutionally sufficient, namely, a reasonable [***2] inquiry into the facts and a fair and reasonable determination.

 

2160 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Auad v. Director, Buffalo State Hospital


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


48 Misc. 2d 1009; 266 N.Y.S.2d 495; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1211



December 31, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator sought, by way of a writ of habeas corpus, her release from respondent state hospital upon the following grounds: (a) the unconstitutionality, defectiveness and invalidity of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 78-4 under which the proceedings against her were initiated; and (b) the defectiveness and invalidity of the proceedings resulting in the detention.


OVERVIEW: Relator's petition stated that her detention was pursuant to an order of the court made on the 22d day of November, 1965, which found relator to be a person mentally ill, and in need of retention for care and treatment, and which directed her transfer to the state hospital. Relator alleged and the hospital did not deny that relator was first committed pursuant to an order made by a Justice of the Peace on the 23d day of October, 1965. That order was made following an appearance by relator before that Magistrate pursuant to a warrant issued by him and recited that it was made pursuant to the provisions of § 78-4. The court held that the Magistrate's order of October 23, 1965, became functus officio 30 days after that date and was not the cause of relator's detention. The latter order was made following a hearing held at the request of relator for an order directing the retention of relator for treatment as a mentally ill person. The court held that the Supreme Court's jurisdiction to conduct a judicial inquiry into the findings and recommendations was completely independent from the proceedings in the Justice's Court.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writ and ordered that relator stay in the custody of the state hospital.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Insane and other incompetent persons -- commitment to State hospital -- regardless of any question of validity of preliminary 30-day commitment to hospital by Magistrate, commitment to State hospital for six months was made by Justice of Supreme Court after hearing and did not depend on physicians' certificates; writ of habeas corpus is dismissed (Mental Hygiene Law, §§ 72, 73, 74, 78, 426; Judiciary Law, § 147-a).

An allegedly mentally ill person was first committed to a hospital in a city, for 30 days' observation, by an order of a Justice of the Peace (Mental Hygiene Law, § 78, subds. 1, 4), and then, on the 30th day, was committed to a State hospital for a period not to exceed six months, by an order of a Justice of the Supreme Court, made after a hearing (Mental Hygiene Law, § 72, subd. 3; §§ 73, 74; § 78, subd. 2). By writ of habeas corpus (Mental Hygiene Law, § 426), she attacks the constitutionality of the statute under which the Justice of the Peace acted. But that issue is moot. The Supreme Court Justice had jurisdiction (Judiciary Law, § 147-a; N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 7, subd. a). She does not challenge the Supreme Court's finding that she is mentally [***2] ill. That finding was based on testimony at the hearing; it does not depend for its validity on the physicians' certificates referred to in sections 72 and 78 (subd. 2) of the Mental Hygiene Law. The writ of habeas corpus is dismissed.

 

2161 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mitchell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, New York County


51 Misc. 2d 82; 272 N.Y.S.2d 523; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1212



December 30, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is accordingly denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes or, in the alternative, to dismiss the indictment against them for feloniously possessing a narcotic drug in an automobile.


OVERVIEW: A narcotic drug was found in a car in which both defendants were present. Under N.Y. Penal Law § 1751, subd. 4, a presumption arose that every person present in the car had knowing possession and control of the narcotics. Defendants moved to inspect the Grand Jury records or to dismiss the indictment on the grounds that the presumption was unconstitutional, the presumption did not apply because the quantity of narcotics found was not great enough to trigger it, and the narcotics were obtained as a result of an illegal search and seizure. The court denied the motion. The court held that the presumption was constitutional because it was neither conclusive nor arbitrary. It was rebuttable, and there was a rational and pragmatic connection between the facts proved and the presumption. Although the amount of narcotics found in the car was not great enough to trigger the presumption, brown paper bags containing drugs that defendants threw out of the car when officers approached also counted in the total amount. The question regarding the search and seizure should have been raised by a motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- narcotics -- subdivision 4 of section 1751 of Penal Law provides that presence in automobile of stated amount of narcotic is presumptive evidence of possession in violation of law by every person in automobile; such presumption is constitutional -- throwing away under observation of some or all of drug does not avoid statutory presumption -- legality of search and seizure may not be challenged by motion to inspect Grand Jury minutes.

1. Subdivision 4 of section 1751 of the Penal Law provides that the presence in an automobile, other than a public omnibus, of any narcotic drug in a stated amount or more shall be presumptive evidence of its possession and control knowingly in violation of law by each and every person found in such automobile at the time such narcotic drug is found. Such presumption is constitutional and does not destroy the presumption of innocence.

2. Occupants of an automobile cannot avoid the effect of the statutory presumption by throwing out of the automobile when an officer is approaching enough of the drugs so as to leave in their possession less than the stated statutory amount. The "presence" of narcotics [***2] in the automobile occupied by defendants is established by evidence of such attempt to divest themselves of possession as well as by what is actually found. Persons found in an automobile at the time such narcotic drug is found, whether it is actually then in the automobile or found in the vicinity after being seen to have been thrown from the automobile, are subject to the presumption.

3. The legality of the search and seizure cannot be raised on a motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes but by a motion to suppress.

 

2162 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Locust Club of Rochester v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


48 Misc. 2d 763; 265 N.Y.S.2d 744; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1213



December 30, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff concerned citizens brought an action for a declaratory judgment seeking a declaration that the enactment by defendant, Council of the City of Rochester (city), of an ordinance establishing a Police Advisory Board (board) was invalid. Rochester, N.Y. Municipal Code ch. 10, vol. II, §§ 10-10.1 through 10-10.25. The city brought a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Sections 10-10.1 through 10-10.25 created the board, required the chief of police to conduct an initial investigation when a complaint was received regarding the conduct of a police officer, and authorized the board to evaluate the chief's report, and if necessary, conduct its own investigation. Only when the board and the chief were unable to arrive at a common conclusion was the board required to report its findings to the commissioner of police. The court struck certain provisions from the municipal code, concluding that in some instances the board had been given the authority to do that which could have only been done by the commissioner. The court explained that § 387 provided that the commissioner was to cause a preliminary investigation to have been made when a complaint regarding the conduct of an officer was made and that the commissioner was to punish any officer found guilty of the charges against him. The court concluded that the city manager, whom was given the authority to appoint the board, had no authority pursuant to § 387 to participate in a disciplinary proceeding against a police officer, either directly or by means of an agency or board appointed by him.


OUTCOME: The court determined that portions of the ordinance were invalid and ordered those provisions stricken from the ordinance. The court ordered any matter in a personnel file of a police officer emanating from a procedure not in compliance with the municipal code expunged from that officer's record.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- police -- ordinance creating citizens' "Police Advisory Board" is adjudged invalid insofar as it empowers board to investigate and hear complaints and publish reports that any policeman has used excessive force -- board's report would constitute reprimand without finding of guilt and, in practice, would affect policeman's service record rating and his fitness for promotion (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6).

1. A civic organization, and three policemen of the City of Rochester who are under investigation by a citizens' "Police Advisory Board" on charges of having used excessive or unnecessary force, are granted summary judgment declaring that the major portions of the ordinance creating the board are invalid.

2. The ordinance amends the city's Municipal Code as follows: The City Manager shall appoint a board of nine persons, none of whom shall be an active law-enforcement officer, to investigate and hear complaints that any policeman has used excessive or unnecessary physical force, and to recommend appropriate action; however, the board is "without any judicial or disciplinary powers". Under the ordinance, the Chief of Police must report to the board [***2] his own findings, recommendations and report of action taken, together with all evidence on which they are based. Then the board may investigate. The board must hold a closed hearing if the aggrieved person so requests in writing. If the board agrees with the Police Chief's report, it shall so report to the complainant, the City Manager, the Commissioner of Public Safety and the Police Chief. If the report of the board and the report of the Police Chief disagree, the Police Chief may conduct further investigations and the board and the Police Chief may endeavor to arrive at a "common conclusion". If this fails, the board shall report the disagreement and its own findings and recommendations to the City Manager and the Commissioner of Public Safety, and if satisfactory action is not taken within two weeks the board may make its recommendations public.

3. This amendment of the city's Municipal Code is inconsistent with an existing section of the city's Municipal Code which provides that the Commissioner of Public Safety shall investigate any charges against any policeman and that the Commissioner or his delegate shall hold hearings on such charges and that if the policeman is found [***3] guilty, the Commissioner may punish him by reprimand or fine or demotion or dismissal from the service. The board's public findings would, in effect, constitute a reprimand without a finding of guilt. Yet, in the case of at least one plaintiff policeman, the board's findings have been made a part of the official personnel file. Apparently the board's report might affect a policeman's service record ratings and his merit and fitness for promotion (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6). The court adjudges that the major sections of the ordinance are invalid, and that any matter in the personnel files of a policeman which has come from the board and which has not been processed in accordance with the pre-existing disciplinary procedures should be expunged.

 

2163 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Creasey v. Bloomenstiel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


48 Misc. 2d 771; 265 N.Y.S.2d 877; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1218



December 28, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant contested the State's right to serve him with warrants of arrest because he was in the State voluntarily as he had to testify in another proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a resident of another state, was the president of a company that was in bankruptcy proceedings. During the proceedings, two complaints and arrest warrants were issued for defendant in this State. The complaints alleged that defendant committed a misdemeanor by failing to pay welfare and vacation plan contributions while president of the company. Defendant was informed that he had to leave his place of residence and go to New York to testify in the bankruptcy proceedings. Defendant did so and was arrested on the arrest warrants. Defendant objected to his arrest because he was immune to such service because he was making a voluntary appearance in New York. The court agreed and held that because defendant had made a voluntary appearance in New York, he was immune from service of the arrest warrants.


OUTCOME: The court found that defendant was immune from being serviced with arrest warrants because he was in the State voluntarily.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- immunity from arrest -- nonresident, appearing as witness in bankruptcy proceeding in United States District Court for Southern District of New York, in response to telephone request from United States Attorney, was here voluntarily and is immune from arrest and prosecution on misdemeanor charge.

1. In response to a telephone request from the United States Attorney's office, defendant, a nonresident, came to New York to testify in bankruptcy proceedings involving a steamship company of which he had at one time been president. Although his appearance could have been compelled, his presence here was voluntary. Service of a subpoena upon him by the United States Attorney's office after he had arrived and conferred with that office about the case, did not destroy the voluntary character of his presence.

2. Under the circumstances, defendant was immune from arrest and criminal prosecution in an action based on alleged failure of the steamship company to pay welfare and vacation plan contributions in the period during which he was president (Penal Law, § 962-a).

 

2164 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Strauss


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


48 Misc. 2d 1006; 266 N.Y.S.2d 431; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1221



December 28, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating the New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § 755(3)-2.1 after a police officer observed his table on public property that contained literature opposing American foreign policy in Vietnam. After the trial started the charge was amended because the code section was renumbered. All counsel agreed to a mistrial and the same day defendant was tried for a violation of § 692h-1.0.


OVERVIEW: Defendant set up a table on a sidewalk close to the curb in Greenwich Village. The table contained literature opposing American foreign policy in Vietnam and condemned the armed intervention there. An American flag was attached to the table. A police officer observed a crowd of people near defendant's table and requested defendant to move the table. Defendant refused and asserted his constitutional right to erect a table for the purpose of informing passers-by of his opposing views on American foreign policy by speaking and distributing literature. Defendant argued that double jeopardy applied and that the statute was unconstitutional because it violated his freedom of expression. The court held that double jeopardy did not apply because defendant's counsel consented to the mistrial and the new trial. The court determined that defendant was not guilty of littering. The court held that although the sidewalk was partly obstructed, there was no evidence that anyone was seriously inconvenienced. The pedestrians were not so inconvenienced as to justify a police officer in stilling the voice of defendant's protest.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant was not guilty.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- freedom of speech -- statute prohibiting obstructing of street cannot constitutionally be applied against defendant who set up table containing literature against American foreign policy in Viet Nam, since pedestrians were not unduly inconvenienced -- on earlier charge of littering, mistrial was declared with defendant's consent; no double jeopardy.

1. One night, on the sidewalk close to the curb, defendant set up a table, 4 feet by 4 feet, containing literature opposing American foreign policy in Viet Nam. He also planned to speak to passers-by from this site. Since pedestrians were not seriously inconvenienced by defendant's exercise of his constitutional right of free speech and peaceful protest, defendant is not guilty of violating section 692h-1.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which prohibits the incumbering or obstructing of any street. Such a statute cannot constitutionally be applied to this situation.

2. At first, defendant was charged with violating section 755 (2)-7.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York (littering); but, during the trial, all counsel agreed in defendant's presence to a mistrial, and the [***2] court so ordered; and, on the same day, the defendant was tried before another Judge for violating section 692h-1.0 of said code. Defendant knowingly participated in this procedure and he cannot now assert double jeopardy.

 

2165 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Catherwood v. Purvis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


48 Misc. 2d 848; 265 N.Y.S.2d 865; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1237; 53 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,452



December 22, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly the petition is granted as prayed for and the answer of respondent is ordered stricken.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Industrial Commission sought an order to compel respondent employer to comply with his order which required an answer to item No. 10 on the Employer Report Form known as LP-1, as mandated by article 20-A (N.Y. Lab. Law § 726(3)d).


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner argued that the employer was required by N.Y. Lab. Law § 726 to file a financial report. The employer filed the report but made no answer to one question. The employer stated that its refusal for noncompliance was not based on belief that he stood in danger of criminal prosecution by giving a responsive answer in his report but on the belief that the question showed contempt for his dignity as a citizen presumed to be innocent.
